<p>(e) The number of moles and the mass of barium peroxide, BaO<sub>2</sub>, needed to produce 2.500 kg of barium oxide, BaO (O<sub>2</sub> is the other product.)</p>

<p>(f)





</p>



End of problem description, beginning of solution

(a)

m[HCl] = 5.00 g

m[Mg] = ?

!Mg + 2HCl -> MgCl2 + H2

Plan

@ n[HCl] = m[HCl] / M[HCl]

@ n[Mg]/1 = n[HCl]/2

@ m[Mg] = n[Mg] * M[Mg]

M[HCl] = 36.46094 g/mol

M[Mg] = 24.305 g/mol

n[HCl] = m[HCl]/M[HCl] 

n[Mg] = n[HCl] / 2

m[Mg] = n[Mg] M[Mg]

c) Mg has a smaller molar mass and a smaller coefficient than HCl, so we would expect m[Mg] > m[HCl]

d) Mg has a larger molar mass and a larger coefficient than HCl, so we would expect m[Mg] > m[HCl]

<h3>Example 9.9: Using the Ideal Gas Law</h3>



<h4>Using the Ideal Gas Law</h4>

Methane, CH4, is being considered for use as an alternative automotive fuel to replace gasoline. One gallon of gasoline could be replaced by 655 g of CH4. What is the volume of this much methane at 25 °aC and 745 mmHg?

<h4>Solution</h4>

T = 25. °aC

P = 745. mmHg

P using atm

R = 0.08206 L atm / (mol K)

m[CH4] = 655 g

V[CH4(g)] = ?

The ideal gas law may be used to figure out the volume of a gas, but apart from temperature T_, pressure P_, and universal gas constant R_, we also need to know the chemical amount of methane, n[CH4]:   

@P V[CH4(g)] = n[CH4] R T

Mass and chemical amount of a pure substance are related through its molar mass:

@m[CH4] = n[CH4] M[CH4]

M[CH4] = 16.043g/mol

n[CH4] = m[CH4] / M[CH4]

V[CH4(g)] = n[CH4] R T / P

<p>It would require 1020 L of gaseous methane at about 1 atm of pressure to replace 1 gal of gasoline. It requires a large container to hold enough methane at 1 atm to replace several gallons of gasoline.</p>

<p><h4>Check Your Learning</h4>

Calculate the pressure in bar of 2520 moles of hydrogen gas stored at 27 °C in the 180-L storage tank of a modern hydrogen-powered car.</p>



<h4>Answer:</h4>

<p>A 2.50-L volume of hydrogen measured at –196 °C is warmed to 100 °C. Calculate the volume of the gas at the higher temperature, assuming no change in pressure.</p>



<span title="12.1 L">Solution</span>

End of problem description, beginning of solution

V1 = 2.50L

T1 = -196. °aC

T2 = 100. °aC

V2 = ?

@ V1 / T1 = V2 / T2

V2 = V1 (T2/T1)

Which one is true:

a) The volume decreased because the temperature decreased

b) The volume increased because the temperature decreased

c) The volume decreased because the temperature increased

d*) The volume increased because the temperature increased

@P V_gas = n R T



Before we can apply the ideal gas law, we need the chemical amount of I2, but we only know the mass of the sample. Mass and chemical amount of a pure substance are related through its molar mass:

R = 8.314 J / (mol K)

<h3>Example 17.1: Balancing Acidic Oxidation-Reduction Reactions</h3>



<p><h4>Balancing Acidic Oxidation-Reduction Reactions</h4>

Balance the following reaction equation in acidic solution:</p>

!MnO4^- (aq) + Cr^3+ (aq) -> Mn^2+ (aq) + Cr2O7^2- (aq)



<p><h4>Solution</h4>

This is an oxidation-reduction reaction, so start by collecting the species given into an unbalanced oxidation half-reaction and an unbalanced reduction half-reaction.</p>



!oxidation (unbalanced): Cr^3+(aq) -> Cr2O7^2- (aq)

!reduction (unbalanced):MnO4^- (aq) -> Mn^2+ (aq)



<p>Starting with the oxidation half-reaction, we can balance the chromium</p>

!oxidation (unbalanced): 2Cr^3+ (aq) -> Cr2O7^2- (aq)



<p>In acidic solution, we can use or generate hydrogen ions (H<sup>+</sup>). Adding seven water molecules to the left side provides the necessary oxygen; the “left over” hydrogen appears as 14 H<sup>+</sup> on the right:</p>

!oxidation (unbalanced): 2Cr^3+ (aq) + 7H2O(l) -> Cr2O7^2- (aq) + 14H+ (aq)

<p>The left side of the equation has a total charge of [2 × (+3) = +6], and the right side a total charge of [−2 + 14 × (+1) = +12]. The difference is six; adding six electrons to the right side produces a mass- and charge-balanced oxidation half-reaction (in acidic solution):</p>



!oxidation (balanced): 2Cr^3+ (aq) + 7H2O(l) -> Cr2O7^2- (aq) + 14H+ (aq) + 6e−

<p>Checking the half-reaction:</p>

Cr: Does(2×1)=(1×2)?Yes.

H: Does(7×2)=(14×1)?Yes.

O: Does(7×1) =(1×7)?Yes.

Charge: Does[ 2×(+3)]=[1 ×(−2)+14×(+1)+6×(−1)]?Yes.

<p>Now work on the reduction. It is necessary to convert the four oxygen atoms in the permanganate into four water molecules. To do this, add eight H+ to convert the oxygen into four water molecules:</p>

!reduction (unbalanced): MnO4^- (aq) + 8H+ (aq) -> Mn^2+ (aq) + 4H2O(l)

<p>Then add five electrons to the left side to balance the charge:</p>

!reduction (balanced): MnO4^- (aq) + 8H+(aq) + 5e- -> Mn^2+ (aq) + 4H2O(l)

<p>Make sure to check the half-reaction:</p>

Mn: Does(1×1)=(1×1)?Yes.

H: Does(8×1)=(4×2)?Yes.

O: Does(1×4)=(4×1)?Yes.

Charge: Does[1 ×(−1)+8×(+1)+5×(−1)]=[ 1×(+2)]?Yes.

<p>Collecting what we have so far:</p>

!oxidation: 2Cr^3+ (aq) + 7H2O(l) -> Cr2O7^2- (aq) + 14H+ (aq) + 6e- 

!reduction: MnO4^- (aq) + 8 H+ (aq) + 5e- -> Mn^2+ (aq) + 4H2O(l)

<p>The least common multiple for the electrons is 30, so multiply the oxidation half-reaction by five, the reduction half-reaction by six, combine, and simplify:</p>

!10Cr^3+ (aq) + 35H2O(l) + 6MnO4^- (aq) + 48H+(aq) -> 5Cr2O7^2- (aq) + 70H+(aq) + 6Mn^2+ (aq) + 24H2O(l)

!10Cr^3+ (aq) + 11H2O(l) + 6MnO4^- (aq) -> 5Cr2O7^2- (aq) + 22H+(aq) + 6Mn^2+(aq)

<p>Checking each side of the equation:</p>

Mn: Does(6×1)=(6×1)?Yes.

Cr: Does(10×1)=(5×2)?Yes.

H: Does(11×2)=(22×1)?Yes.

O: Does(11×1+6×4)=(5×7)?Yes.

Charge: Does[10 ×(+3)+6×(−1)]=[5× (−2)+22×(+1)+6×(+2)]?Yes.

<p>This is the balanced equation in acidic solution.</p>

<p><h4>Check your learning</h4>

Balance the following equation in acidic solution:</p>

!Hg2^2+ + Ag -> Hg + Ag+



<h4>Answer:</h4>



!Hg2^2+ (aq) + 2Ag(s) -> 2Hg(l) + 2Ag+ (aq)

<h3>Chapter 9.6, Exercise 104</h3>



<p>A 0.245-L flask contains 0.467 mol CO2 at 159 °C. Calculate the pressure:</p>

<p>(a) using the ideal gas law</p>

<p>(b) using the van der Waals equation</p>

<p>(c) Explain the reason for the difference.</p>

<p>(d) Identify which correction (that for P or V) is dominant and why.</p>





End of problem description, beginning of solution

V_flask = 0.245 L

n[CO2] = 0.467 mol

T = 159. °aC



P_ideal = n[CO2] T R / V_flask 

P_ideal using atm

<a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/9-6-non-ideal-gas-behavior/#fs-idm15100464">Table</a>

a = 3.59 atm L^2 / mol^2

b = 0.0427 L/mol

@(P + n^2 a /V_gas^2) (V_gas - n b) = n R T

P_vdw = n[CO2] R T / (V_flask - n[CO2] b ) - n[CO2]^2 a / V_flask^2

P_ideal using atm



Pressure is higher than ideal gas law predicts because atoms take up space

Pressure is lower than ideal gas law predicts because molecules are sticky

In this case, correction for stickyness (-13 atm) is higher than correction for atomic volume (+7 atm)

<h4>Answer:</h4>

<h3>Example 4.9b: Number of Product Molecules Generated by a Reaction</h3>



How many NH3 molecules are produced by the reaction of 4.0 mol of Ca(OH)2 according to the following equation:



!(NH4)2SO4 +Ca(OH)2 -> 2NH3 +CaSO4 +2H2O



<h4>Answer:</h4>

N[NH3] = ?
n[Ca(OH)2] = 4.0 mol

@n[NH3] / n[Ca(OH)2] = (2/1)

ν[NH3] = 2
ν[Ca(OH)2]  = 1

n[NH3] = ν[NH3] * n[Ca(OH)2]  / ν[Ca(OH)2]

N_A = 6.022E23 mol^-1

N[NH3] = n[NH3] N_A 
Think about it: Are there more molecules of Ca(OH)2 reacting or more molecules of NH3 forming?
a) more NH3 because its coefficient is higher
a) more NH3 because its coefficient is lower
a) more Ca(OH)2 because it lacks a coefficient
a) more Ca(OH)2 because it contains more atoms than NH3




<h3>Example 4.5b: Assigning Oxidation Numbers</h3>
Assign oxidation states to the elements in each of the following compounds or ions:
(a) Nitrogen in KNO3
(b) Aluminum in AlH3
(c) Nitrogen in NH4+
(d) Phosphorous in H2PO4-
<h4>Answer:</h4>



a) 

Ox[K] = 1

Ox[O] = -2

Charge = 0

@ Ox[K] + Ox[N] + 3 * Ox[O] = Charge

 

Ox[N] = Charge - Ox[K] - 3 Ox[O]

  

b) Aluminum in AlH3

Ox[H] = -1

Ox[Al] = 0 - 3 Ox[H] 

(c) Nitrogen in NH+4

Ox[H] = 1



Charge = 1

Ox[N] = Charge - 4 * Ox[H] 

(d) Phosphorous in H2PO4-



Charge = -1

Ox[H] = 1

Ox[O] = -2



Ox[P] = Charge -2Ox[H] -4Ox[O] 

Think about it: Why does hydrogen have a -1 oxidation number in AlH3, but a +1 oxidation number otherwise?

a) Because in the formula, the hydrogen atoms are listed last, like an anion

b*) Because it is combined with a metal

c) Because for hydrogen, +1 is the same as -1

d) Because AlH3 is a compound and not an ion



<h3>Example 4.6b: Describing Redox Reactions</h3>



This equation describes the production of tin(II) chloride:



!Sn(s) +2HCl(g) -> SnCl2(s) +H2(g)

Is this a redox reaction? If so, provide a more specific name for the reaction if appropriate, and identify the oxidant and reductant.





<h4>Answer:</h4>



Plan: Figure out the oxidation numbers for each type of atom before and after the reaction. If any of them change, it is a redox reaction.

Ox[Sn\ in\ Sn(s)] = 0

Reasoning: it's elemental, my dear Dr. Watson

Ox[Sn\ in\ SnCl2(s)] = +2

Reasoning: it has to be positive because chloride has a negative oxidation number

Ox[H\ in\ HCl(g)] = +1

Reasoning: chloride is -1

Ox[H\ in\ H2(g)] = 0

Reasoning: It's elemental, my dear Dr. Watson

In the course of the reaction, tin becomes oxidized and hydrogen becomes reduced, so HCl is the oxidant and tin the reductant.

Think about it: What general statement is true and would be a great shortcut for figuring out this exercise?

a) The reductant always contains an atom that is a metal

b) The oxidant is always oxygen

c*) Reactions with elements are typically redox

d) Redox reactions always have two reactants and two products



<h3>Example 4.7b: Balancing Redox Reactions in Acidic Solution</h3>



In acidic solution, hydrogen peroxide reacts with Fe^2+ to produce Fe^3 + and H2O. Write a balanced equation for this reaction.





!<h4>Answer:</h4>

chemical equation?
!H2O2 + Fe^2+ -> Fe^3+ + H2O (unbalanced)

Fe is balanced, H is balanced, but O and charges are not. It is a redox reaction (for example, Fe changes oxidation number), so we will first balance half reactions

!Fe^2+ -> Fe^3+ + e-\ (oxidation\ half\ reaction)



!H2O2 -> 2H2O\ (reduction\ half\ reaction)

!H2O2 + 2H+ -> 2H2O\ (O\ balanced,\ H\ balanced,\ electrons\ not)

!H2O2 + 2H+ + 2e- -> 2H2O\ (balanced)



Now make sure the same number of electrons appears in the two half reactions. Here, we multiply all coefficients in the oxidation half reaction by 2:

!2 Fe^2+ -> 2 Fe^3+ + 2e−

and add the two half reactions

!2 Fe^2+ + H2O2 + 2H+ + 2e- -> 2 Fe^3+ + 2e− + 2 H2O

!2 Fe^2+ + H2O2 + 2H+ -> 2 Fe^3+ + 2 H2O (balanced\ redox\ equation)

Think about it: How do we know how to divide the unbalanced reaction into two half reactions?

a) First, find the atoms that change oxidation numbers

b) Metals in one half reaction, non-metals in the other

c) It doesn't matter, just divide them up any way

d) Charged species in one half reaction, uncharged ones in the other

<h4>Answer:</h4>

chemical formula?

Barium ion is a cation.

Check the anions to see whether they would form a precipitate with barium ions.

Sulfate forms the least soluble salts with barium.

!SO4^2- (aq) + Ba^2+ (aq) -> BaSO4 v
! BaSO4 ! forms as a precipitate

a) You need the periodic table

c) You need to know the charges of the ions
d) You need to look up the molar masses of all species

<h3>Example 4.4b: Writing Equations for Acid-Base Reactions</h3>

Write the net ionic equation representing the neutralization of any strong acid with an ionic hydroxide. (Hint: Consider the ions produced when a strong acid is dissolved in water.)





<h4>Answer:</h4> 

chemical equation?
Strong Acid:

!HA + H2O -> H3O+ + A-

Strong Base:

!B + H2O -> BH+ + OH-

When you react strong acid with strong base, the hydronium and hydroxide ion concentrations are both high, and they react with each other to form water

!H3O+ + OH- -> 2 H2O

Think about it: What does neutralization mean?

a) Any reaction that forms a salt

b) Any reaction where one of the products is water

c*) A reaction between an acid and a base

<h3>Example 4.2b: Molecular and Ionic Equations</h3>



Diatomic chlorine and sodium hydroxide (lye) are commodity chemicals produced in large quantities, along with diatomic hydrogen, via the electrolysis of brine, according to the following unbalanced equation:



! NaCl(aq) +H2O(l) ->[electricity] NaOH(aq) +H2(g) +Cl2(g)

Write balanced molecular, complete ionic, and net ionic equations for this process.





<h4>Answer:</h4>

chemical equation?



!NaCl + H2O -> NaOH + H2 + Cl2

Na and O are balanced, H and Cl are not.

!NaCl + H2O -> NaOH + 1/2 H2 + 1/2 Cl2

Balanced. Multiply all coefficients by two to make it nicer:

!2NaCl + 2H2O -> 2NaOH + H2 + Cl2 !(molecular)

NaCl and NaOH dissociate in water. Na is a metal which forms Na+ ions. The anions Cl- and OH- could be deduced from that information, but they, too, are common ions.

!2Na+ + 2Cl- + 2H2O -> 2Na+ + 2OH- + H2 + Cl2 !(complete ionic)

Drop the sodium ions that occur on both sides for the net ionic equation

!2Cl- + 2H2O -> 2OH- + H2 + Cl2 !(net ionic)

Think about it: How come we did not use the half-reaction method (showing the electron transfer) to balance the molecular equation?

a) This reaction is not a redox reaction because it does not involved transition metals
b*) If all species are neutral, you can get away with it
c) We know this reaction happens at neutral pH because the reactants are neither acid nor base
d) The electricity provides the electrons, so there is no electron transfer



<h3>Example 4.8b: Moles of Reactant Required in a Reaction</h3>



How many moles of Ca(OH)2 are required to react with 1.36 mol of H3PO4 to produce Ca3(PO4)2 according to the equation

!3Ca(OH)2 +2H3PO4 -> Ca3(PO4)2 +6H2O?





<h4>Answer:</h4>

n[H3PO4] = 1.36 mol

n[Ca(OH)2] = ?

ν[H3PO4] = 2
ν[Ca(OH)2] = 3
The equation is balanced, so we can go ahead and use the coefficients

@ n[Ca(OH)2] /n[H3PO4]  = (3/2)

n[Ca(OH)2] = (ν[Ca(OH)2]/ν[H3PO4]) n[H3PO4] 



Think about it: The chemical equation says that 3 moles of calcium hydroxide react with 2 moles of phosphoric acid. How does this statement relate to your calculation?

<h3>Example 4.10b: Relating Masses of Reactants and Products</h3>



What mass of gallium oxide, Ga2O3, can be prepared from 29.0 g of gallium metal? The equation for the reaction is

!4Ga +3O2 -> 2Ga2O3





<h4>Answer:</h4>



m[Ga] = 29.0 g

m[Ga2O3] = ?

ν[Ga2O3] = 2
ν[Ga]  = 4


@n[Ga2O3] /n[Ga] = ν[Ga2O3]/ν[Ga]

n[Ga] = m[Ga] /M[Ga] 

n[Ga2O3] = (ν[Ga2O3]/ν[Ga]) n[Ga]  

m[Ga2O3] = n[Ga2O3] M[Ga2O3] 

Think about it: Do you expect the mass of Ga2O3 produced to be higher or lower than that of the elemental Ga reacting?

a) It would be lower because the coefficient is lower, i.e. the chemical amount of Ga2O3 is smaller than that of elemental Ga

b) It would be lower because gallium is losing electrons in this redox reaction

c*) It would be higher because all of the gallium is turned into the oxide, now with some oxygen attached

d) It would be higher because whenever you make a high molar mass product from a low molar mass reactant, you observe a mass increase

<h3>Example 4.12b: Identifying the Limiting Reactant</h3>



Which is the limiting reactant when 5.00 g of H2 and 10.0 g of O2 react and form water?





<h4>Answer:</h4>


n[->] = ?

m[H2] = 5.00 g

m[O2] = 10.0 g

! H2 + O2 -> H2O

! H2 + 1/2 O2 -> H2O

! 2 H2 + O2 -> 2 H2O

Plan 1) balanced chemical equation 2) chemical amounts of reactants 3) find which is limiting

n[H2] = m[H2] / M[H2]

n[O2] = m[O2] / M[O2]  

n[->] = min(n[H2] /2, n[O2] /1)

O2 is limiting

Think about it: Without doing any math,

a) we know that H2 is limiting because there is a lower mass of it available

b) we know that O2 is limiting because it has the lower stoichiometric coefficient

m[CCl4] = 32.9 g

yield_relative = ?

n[CCl4] = m[CCl4] / M[CCl4] 

@ n_Freon / n[CCl4] = 1/1

n_Freon_theoretical = n[CCl4] * (1/1)

yield_relative = n_Freon_actual / n_Freon_theoretical * 100 ％

<h3>Example 4.14b: Titration Analysis</h3>



A 20.00-mL sample of aqueous oxalic acid, H2C2O4, was titrated with a 0.09113-<em>M</em> solution of potassium permanganate.



!2MnO4- (aq) +5H2C2O4(aq) +6H+ (aq) -> 10CO2(g) +2 Mn^2+ (aq) +8H2O(l)

A volume of 23.24 mL was required to reach the end point. What is the oxalic acid concentration?





<h4>Answer:</h4>

V_oxalic = 20.00 mL

c[MnO4-] = 0.09113 M

V[MnO4-] = 23.24 mL

c_oxalic = ?

@n_oxalic / n[MnO4-] = 5/2

n[MnO4-] = c[MnO4-] V[MnO4-] 

n_oxalic = (5/2) n[MnO4-]  

c_oxalic = n_oxalic / V_oxalic



Think about it: Given that the volumes of oxalic acid solution and potassium permanganate solution at the end point are similar, is it surprising that the concentration of oxalic acid is more than two times that of the permanganate?

a*) No, because they react in a ratio of 5:2

b) No, because the end point is always reached at equal volumes of analyte and titrant

c) Yes, because the concentrations should be similar if the volumes are similar

d) Yes, because permanganate is a stronger oxidizing agent than oxalic acid

f[Cl]mass = ?

m[AgCl] = 1.0881 g

@ n[Cl-] / n[AgCl] = 1/1

n[AgCl] = m[AgCl] / M[AgCl]

n[Cl-] = n[AgCl] * (1/1) 

m[Cl] = n[Cl-] * M[Cl] 

f[Cl]mass = m[Cl] /m_sample * 100 ％

<h3>Example 4.16b: Combustion Analysis</h3>



A 0.00215-g sample of polystyrene, a polymer composed of carbon and hydrogen, produced 0.00726 g of CO2 and 0.00148 g of H2O in a combustion analysis. What is the empirical formula for polystyrene?





<h4>Answer:</h4>

chemical formula?

ratio[H:C] = ?

m_PS = 0.00215 g

m[CO2] = 0.00726 g

m[H2O] = 0.00148 g

! C + ... -> CO2 + ...

!H + ... -> 1/2 H2O + ...

n[CO2] = m[CO2] / M[CO2]

n[H2O] = m[H2O] / M[H2O]  

n[C] = n[CO2]

n[H] = 2 n[H2O]  

ratio[H:C] = n[H] /n[C] 

The empirical formula of the polymer polystyrene is CH, and the actual formula is (CH)x, where x is a high number because it is a polymer, i.e. high molar mass compound.

Think about it: Is it surprising that the calculated ratio is not exactly an integer?

a) Yes, we expect integers according to Daltons law of integer proportions

b) Yes, because modern calculators are sufficiently accurate to give the correct result

c) No, because the calculation requires the molar masses of CO2 and H2O, which are not known to high accuracy

d) No, because the given masses are reported to only 3 significant digits

<h3>Example 5.1: Measuring Heat</h3>

<h4>Measuring Heat</h4>

A flask containing 8.0e2 g of water is heated, and the temperature of the water increases from 21 °C to 85 °C. How much heat did the water absorb?

<h4>Solution</h4>

m[H2O] = 8.0e2g

T_initial = 21. °aC

T_final = 85. °aC

q[H2O] = ?

To answer this question, consider these factors:

- the specific heat of the substance being heated (in this case, water)

- the amount of substance being heated (in this case, 800 g)

- the magnitude of the temperature change (in this case, from 21 °C to 85 °C).



The specific heat of water is 4.184 J/g °C, so to heat 1 g of water by 1 °C requires 4.184 J. We note that since 4.184 J is required to heat 1 g of water by 1 °C, we will need <em>800 times as much</em> to heat 800 g of water by 1 °C. Finally, we observe that since 4.184 J are required to heat 1 g of water by 1 °C, we will need <em>64 times as much</em> to heat it by 64 °C (that is, from 21 °C to 85 °C).

c[H2O] = 4.184 J/(g °ΔC)

This can be summarized using the equation q = c[H2O] m[H2O] ΔT_ with ΔT = T_final - T_initial

ΔT = T_final - T_initial

q[H2O] = c[H2O] m[H2O] ΔT

q[H2O] using kJ

Because the temperature increased, the water absorbed heat and q[H2O] is positive.







<h3>Example 5.2: Determining Other Quantities</h3>





<h4>Determining Other Quantities</h4>

A piece of unknown metal weighs 348 g. When the metal piece absorbs 6.64 kJ of heat, its temperature increases from 22.4 °C to 43.6 °C. Determine the specific heat of this metal (which might provide a clue to its identity).



<h4>Solution</h4>

m_unknown = 348. g

q_piece = 6.64 kJ

T_initial = 22.4 °aC

T_final = 43.6 °aC

c_unknown = ?

Since mass, heat, and temperature change are known for this metal, we can determine its specific heat using the relationship:



@q = c m_ ΔT = c m_ (T_final - T_initial)

Solving for c:

@ c = q / (m_ ΔT)

Substituting the known quantities:

c_unknown = q_piece / ( m_unknown (T_final - T_initial))

c_unknown using J

Comparing this value with the values in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/5-1-energy-basics/#fs-idm68801008">Table 1</a>, this value matches the specific heat of aluminum, which suggests that the unknown metal may be aluminum.




<h3>Example 5.3: Heat Transfer between Substances at Different Temperatures</h3>

<h4>Heat Transfer between Substances at Different Temperatures</h4>
A 360-g piece of rebar (a steel rod used for reinforcing concrete) is dropped into 425 mL of water at 24.0 °C. The final temperature of the water was measured as 42.7 °C. Calculate the initial temperature of the piece of rebar. Assume the specific heat of steel is approximately the same as that for iron (<a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/5-1-energy-basics/#fs-idm68801008">Table 1</a>), and that all heat transfer occurs between the rebar and the water (there is no heat exchange with the surroundings).

<h4>Solution</h4>
m_rebar = 360. g
V_water = 425. mL
T[H2O]initial = 24.0 °aC
T_final = 42.7 °aC
T_rebar_inital = ?

The temperature of the water increases from 24.0 °C to 42.7 °C, so the water absorbs heat. That heat came from the piece of rebar, which initially was at a higher temperature. Assuming that all heat transfer was between the rebar and the water, with no heat “lost” to the surroundings, then <em>heat given off by rebar = -heat taken in by water</em>, or:

@q_rebar = -q_water
Since we know how heat is related to other measurable quantities, we have:

@(c_rebar m_rebar ΔT_rebar)= - (c_water m_water ΔT_water)
#Letting f = final and i = initial, in expanded form, this becomes:

@ crebar mrebar (Tf_rebar - Ti_rebar)= - cwater mwater (Tf_water - Ti_water)
Solving for Ti_rebar
@ T_initial_rebar = c_water/c_rebar (m_water/m_rebar) (T_final - T_initial_water) + T_final

The density of water is 1.00 g/mL, so 425 mL of water has a mass of 425 g.
ρ[H2O] = 1.00 g/mL
m_water = V_water ρ[H2O]
The specific heats are:
c_water = 4.184J/(g °ΔC)
c_rebar = 0.449J/(g °ΔC)
Ti_rebar = c_water/c_rebar (m_water/m_rebar) (T_final - T[H2O]initial) + T_final
The initial temperature of the rebar was 248 °C.


<h3>Example 4.3: Predicting Precipitation Reactions</h3>



<h4>Predicting Precipitation Reactions</h4>

Predict the result of mixing reasonably concentrated solutions of the following ionic compounds. If precipitation is expected, write a balanced net ionic equation for the reaction.



(a) potassium sulfate and barium nitrate



(b) lithium chloride and silver acetate



(c) lead nitrate and ammonium carbonate



<h4>Solution</h4>



(a) The two possible products for this combination are KNO3 and BaSO4. The <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/4-2-classifying-chemical-reactions/#fs-idp140132617697568">solubility guidelines</a> indicate BaSO4 is insoluble, and so a precipitation reaction is expected. The net ionic equation for this reaction, derived in the manner detailed in the previous module, is

!Ba^2+(aq) +SO4^2- (aq) -> BaSO4(s)



(b) The two possible products for this combination are LiC2H3O2 and AgCl. The solubility guidelines indicate AgCl is insoluble, and so a precipitation reaction is expected. The net ionic equation for this reaction, derived in the manner detailed in the previous module, is

!Ag+(aq) +Cl- (aq) -> AgCl(s)



(c) The two possible products for this combination are PbCO3 and NH4NO3. The solubility guidelines indicate PbCO3 is insoluble, and so a precipitation reaction is expected. The net ionic equation for this reaction, derived in the manner detailed in the previous module, is

!Pb^2+(aq) +CO3^2- (aq) -> PbCO3(s)

<h3>Example 5.4: Identifying a Metal by Measuring Specific Heat</h3>



A 59.7 g piece of metal that had been submerged in boiling water was quickly transferred into 60.0 mL of water initially at 22.0 °C. The final temperature is 28.5 °C. Use these data to determine the specific heat of the metal. Use this result to identify the metal.



<h4>Solution</h4>

c_p_metal = ? # to identify the metal

m_metal = 59.7 g

V_water = 60.0 mL

T_initial_metal = 100 °aC

T_initial_water = 22.0 °aC

T_final = 28.5 °aC

Assuming perfect heat transfer, the heat given off by metal is equal to the heat taken in by water, or:

@q_metal = -q_water



In expanded form using q = c_p m_ ΔT_ this is:

@c_p_metal m_metal ΔT_metal = -c_p_water m_water ΔT_water

Solving this for c_p_metal:

@c_p_metal  = -c_p_water (m_water/m_metal)  (ΔT_water/ΔT_metal)

Noting that since the metal was submerged in boiling water, its initial temperature was 100.0 °C; and that 60.0 mL of water have a mass of 60.0 g, we have:

ΔT_metal = T_final - T_initial_metal

ΔT_water = T_final - T_initial_water

ρ_water = 1.000 g/mL

m_water = ρ_water V_water

c_p_water = 4.186 J/(g °ΔC)

c_p_metal  = -c_p_water (m_water/m_metal)  (ΔT_water/ΔT_metal)



Comparing this with values in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/5-1-energy-basics/#fs-idm68801008">Table 1</a>, our experimental specific heat is closest to the value for copper (0.39 J/g °C), so we identify the metal as copper.



<h3>Example 5.6: Heat Flow in an Instant Ice Pack</h3>



When solid ammonium nitrate dissolves in water, the solution becomes cold. This is the basis for an “instant ice pack” (<a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_05_02_IcePack.jpg">Figure 16</a>). When 3.21 g of solid NH4NO3 dissolves in 50.0 g of water at 24.9 °C in a calorimeter, the temperature decreases to 20.3 °C.



Calculate the heat of reaction q_rxn for dissolving NH4NO3 and explain the meaning of its arithmetic sign. State any assumptions that you made.



#<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_05_02_IcePack.jpg" width="30%">Figure 16An instant cold pack consists of a bag containing solid ammonium nitrate and a second bag of water. When the bag of water is broken, the pack becomes cold because the dissolution of ammonium nitrate is an endothermic process that removes thermal energy from the water. The cold pack then removes thermal energy from your body.

<h4>Solution</h4>

q_rxn = ? # and discuss meaning of sign

m[NH4NO3] = 3.21 g

m[H2O] = 50.0 g

T_initial = 24.9 °aC

T_final = 20.3 °aC

We assume that the calorimeter prevents heat transfer between the solution and its external environment (including the calorimeter itself), in which case:

@q_dis = -q_soln

with “rxn” and “soln” used as shorthand for “reaction” and “solution,” respectively.

@q_soln = c_p_soln m_soln ΔT

m_soln = m[NH4NO3] + m[H2O]

ΔT = T_final - T_initial

Assuming also that the specific heat of the solution is the same as that for water, we have:

c_p_soln = 4.184J/(g °ΔC)

q_soln = c_p_soln m_soln ΔT

q_rxn = -q_soln

The positive sign for <em>q</em> indicates that the reaction is an endothermic process. (If a reaction cools down the surrounding, it is endothermic.)



<h3>Example 5.7: Bomb Calorimetry</h3>



<h4>Bomb Calorimetry</h4>

When 3.12 g of glucose, C6H12O6, is burned in a bomb calorimeter, the temperature of the calorimeter increases from 23.8 °C to 35.6 °C. The calorimeter contains 775 g of water, and the bomb itself has a heat capacity of 893 J/°C. How much heat was produced by the combustion of the glucose sample?



<h4>Solution</h4>

q_rxn = ?

m_glucose = 3.12 g

m_water = 775. g

T_initial = 23.8 °aC

T_final = 35.6 °aC

C_V_bomb = 893 J/°ΔC

c_V_water = 4.1371 J/(g °ΔC) # book says 4.184J/(g °ΔC)

The combustion produces heat that is primarily absorbed by the water and the bomb. (The amounts of heat absorbed by the reaction products and the unreacted excess oxygen are relatively small and dealing with them is beyond the scope of this text. We will neglect them in our calculations.)



The heat produced by the reaction is absorbed by the water and the bomb:

@q_rxn = -(q_water + q_bomb)

@q_water = c_V_water m_water ΔT

@q_bomb = C_V_bomb ΔT

ΔT = T_final - T_initial

q_water = c_V_water m_water ΔT

q_bomb = C_V_bomb ΔT

q_rxn = -(q_water + q_bomb)

q_rxn using kJ

This reaction released 48.7 kJ of heat when 3.12 g of glucose was burned.



<h3>Example 5.8: Measurement of an Enthalpy Change</h3>



<h4>Measurement of an Enthalpy Change</h4>

When 0.0500 mol of HCl(aq) reacts with 0.0500 mol of NaOH(aq) to form 0.0500 mol of NaCl(aq), 2.9 kJ of heat are produced. What is the molar enthalpy change ΔH_ (i.e. enthalpy per mole of acid reacting) for the acid-base reaction run under the conditions described in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/5-2-calorimetry/#fs-idm19242032">Example 5.4</a>?



!HCl(aq) +NaOH(aq) -> NaCl(aq) +H2O(l)

<h4>Solution</h4>

For the reaction of 0.0500 mol acid (HCl), q = -2.9 kJ so:

n[HCl]_rxn = 0.0500 mol

q_rxn = -2.9 kJ



The heat generated depends on the amount of substance that reacts (double the reaction, double the heat), so it is an extensive quantity. On the other hand, the molar enthalpy change ΔH_ is per amount of acid reacting, so it is an intensive quantity:

ΔH = q_rxn / n[HCl]_rxn

We based the molar enthalpy change on the chemical amount of HCl reacting. When we write the thermochemical equation, we have to make sure the coefficient of HCl is "1" to reflect that:



!HCl(aq) +NaOH(aq) -> NaCl(aq) +H2O(l)\ \ \ ΔH = -58kJ

Think about it: Is this reaction endothermic (takes up heat) or exothermic (gives off heat)?
a) Exothermic as the reaction enthalpy is positive
b) Exothermic as the reaction enthalpy is negative
c) Endothermic as the reaction enthalpy is positive
d) Endothermic as the reaction enthalpy is negative


<h3>Example 5.9: Another Example of the Measurement of an Enthalpy Change</h3>

A gummy   bear contains 2.67 g sucrose, C12H22O11. When it reacts with 7.19 g potassium chlorate, KClO3, 43.7 kJ of heat are produced. Determine the enthalpy change for the reaction

!C12H22O11(aq) +8KClO3(aq) -> 12CO2 (g) +11H2O(l) +8KCl(aq)

<h4>Solution</h4>

m_sucrose = 2.67 g

m[KClO3] = 7.19 g

q_rxn = -43.7 kJ

ΔH_rxn = ?

Plan 1) How much reacts, n[->] 2) ΔH_rxn based on n[->]

n_sucrose  = m_sucrose / M[C12H22O11] 

ν_sucrose = 1

n[KClO3] = m[KClO3] / M[KClO3]

ν[KClO3] = 8

n[->] = min(n_sucrose / ν_sucrose, n[KClO3] / ν[KClO3])

ΔH_rxn = q_rxn / n[->] 

!C12H22O11 +8KClO3 -> 12CO2 +11H2O +8KCl~~~~~~~~~ΔH~=~-5960kJ/mol





<h3>Example 5.8: Measurement of an Enthalpy Change</h3>

When 0.0500 mol of HCl(aq) reacts with 0.0500 mol of NaOH(aq) to form 0.0500 mol of NaCl(aq), 2.9 kJ of heat are produced. What is the molar enthalpy change ΔH_ (i.e. enthalpy per mole of reaction) for the acid-base reaction run under the conditions described in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/5-2-calorimetry/#fs-idm19242032">Example 5.4</a>?

!HCl(aq) +NaOH(aq) -> NaCl(aq) +H2O(l)



<h4>Solution</h4>

ΔH_rxn = ?

n[HCl]_rxn = 0.0500 mol

n[NaOH]_rxn = 0.0500 mol

n[NaCl]_rxn = 0.0500 mol

q_rxn = -2.9 kJ

The heat generated depends on the amount of substance that reacts (double the reaction, double the heat), so it is an extensive quantity. On the other hand, the molar enthalpy change ΔH_ is per amount of reaction n[->], so it is an intensive quantity:

@ΔH = q_rxn / n[->]_rxn

Plan: 1) n[->] 2) ΔH_rxn

In this case, calculating n[->] is easy because it says how much of each substance is made or used up (and their ratios correspond to the 1:1:1 ratio given by the chemical equation).

ν[HCl] = 1

n[->]_rxn = n[HCl]_rxn / ν[HCl]

ΔH = q_rxn / n[->]_rxn

We could have used n[->] = n[NaOH]_rxn / ν[NaOH] or n[->] = n[NaCl]_rxn / ν[NaCl] instead...

!HCl(aq) +NaOH(aq) -> NaCl(aq) +H2O(l) ~~~ ΔH = -58kJ/mol



<h3>Example 5.10: Using Enthalpy of Combustion</h3>

As <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_05_03_GasBurning.jpg">Figure 21</a> suggests, the combustion of gasoline is a highly exothermic process. To approximate the amount of heat produced by burning 1.00 L of gasoline, we will do the calculation for 1.00 L of isooctane (C8H18), a common component of gasoline. The density of isooctane is 0.692 g/mL.



#<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_05_03_GasBurning.jpg" width="50%">Figure 21:The combustion of gasoline is very exothermic. (credit: modification of work by “AlexEagle”/Flickr)

<h4>Solution</h4>

V_isooctane = 1.00 L

ρ_isooctane = 0.692 g/mL

q_approx = ?

Plan: 1) m_isooctane 2) n_isooctane 2) q_rxn

m_isooctane = V_isooctane ρ_isooctane

n_isooctane = m_isooctane / M[C8H18]

<a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/5-3-enthalpy/#fs-idp98710048">Table 2</a> gives enthalpy of combustion of isooctane as -5460 kJ/mol.

ΔH_combustion = -5460 kJ/mol

q_approx = n_isooctane ΔH_combustion



The combustion of 1.00 L of isooctane produces 33,100 kJ of heat. (This amount of energy is enough to melt 99.2 kg, or about 218 lbs, of ice.)







<h3>Example 5.11: Evaluating an Enthalpy of Formation</h3>

Ozone, O3(g), forms from oxygen, O2(g), by an endothermic process. Ultraviolet radiation is the source of the energy that drives this reaction in the upper atmosphere. Assuming that both the reactants and products of the reaction are in their standard states, determine the standard enthalpy of formation, ΔHf° of ozone from the following information:



!3O2(g) -> 2O3(g) !ΔH = 286 kJ/mol

<h4>Solution</h4>

ΔH_298° = 286 kJ/mol

ν[O3] = 2 

ΔHf°[O3] = ?

ΔHf° is the enthalpy change per one mole of a substance formed (in its standard state from the elements in their standard states). In the chemical equation, we are making 2 moles of ozone, so we have to divide the enthalpy of reaction by the stoichiometric coefficient of ozone to account for that.

ΔHf°[O3] = ΔH_298° / ν[O3]

In other words, ΔHf° for O3(g) is the enthalpy change for the reaction:

!3/2O2(g) -> O3(g)





<h3>Example 5.12: Writing Reaction Equations for ΔHf°</h3>

Write the heat of formation reaction equations for:



!C2H5OH(l)

and

! Ca3(PO4)2(s)



<h4>Solution</h4>

Remembering that ΔHf° reaction equations are for forming 1 mole of the compound from its constituent elements under standard conditions, we have:



! ?~C(s,graphite) + ~?~H2(g) + ~?~O2(g) -> C2H5OH(l)

for the first substance, C2H5OH

To balance this, count how many of each element are found in the product, and adjust the coefficients of the elements on the reactant side, i.e. we need 2 carbons, 6 hydrogens, and 1 oxygen. For oxygen, the coefficient has to be less than one (a fraction) because one O2 molecule already contains 2 atoms of oxygen, but we need only one.

! 2C(s,graphite) +3H2(g) +1/2O2(g) -> C2H5OH(l)

and

! 3Ca(s) +1/2P4(s) +4O2(g) -> Ca3(PO4)2(s)

Note: The standard state of carbon is graphite, and phosphorus exists as P4 (you can use <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/back-matter/standard-thermodynamic-properties-for-selected-substances/">appendix G</a> to find the standard forms of elements).



<h3>Example 5.13: Stepwise Calculation of ΔHf° Using Hess’s Law</h3>

Determine the enthalpy of formation, ΔHf°, of FeCl3(s) from the enthalpy changes of the following two-step process that occurs under standard state conditions:



![1]~~Fe(s) +Cl2(g) -> FeCl2(s)~~~~ΔH° = -341.8kJ/mol

![2]~~FeCl2(s) +1/2Cl2(g) -> FeCl3(s)~~~~ΔH° = -57.7kJ/mol

<h4>Solution</h4>

ΔH°_rxn1 = -341.8kJ/mol

ΔH°_rxn2  = -57.7kJ/mol

We are trying to find the standard enthalpy of formation of FeCl3(s), which is equal to ΔHf° for the reaction:

![3]~~Fe(s) +3/2Cl2(g) -> FeCl3(s)

If we add the two reactions for which we know the enthalpy, we get:

!Fe(s) +Cl2(g) + FeCl2(s) +1/2Cl2(g) -> FeCl2(s) + FeCl3(s)

To get the net reaction, remove the FeCl2 occuring both as reactnat and product, and add up the Cl2 occuring twice in the list of reactant to see that this is the reaction we need:

!Fe(s) +3/2Cl2(g) -> FeCl3(s)

According to Hess's Law, the reaction enthalpy of this reaction is the sum of the two reactions with known ΔH values:

ΔH°_rxn3  = ΔH°_rxn1 + ΔH°_rxn2



!Fe(s) +3/2Cl2(g) -> FeCl3(s)~~~~$ΔH° = -399.5$ kJ/mol

The enthalpy of formation, ΔHf°, of FeCl3(s) is -399.5 kJ/mol.



<h3>Example 5.14: A More Challenging Problem Using Hess’s Law</h3>

Chlorine monofluoride can react with fluorine to form chlorine trifluoride:



![1] ClF(g) +F2(g) -> ClF3(g) !ΔH_1 = ?

Use the reactions here to determine the Δ<em>H</em> for reaction [1]:



![2] 2OF2(g) -> O2(g) +2F2(g) !ΔH_2 = -49.4kJ/mol

![3] 2ClF(g) +O2(g) -> Cl2O(g) +OF2(g) !ΔH_3 = 205.6 kJ/mol

![4] ClF3(g) +O2(g) -> 1/2Cl2O(g) +3/2OF2(g) !ΔH_4 = 266.7 kJ/mol

<h4>Solution</h4>

Our goal is to manipulate and combine reactions [2], [3], and [4] such that they add up to reaction [1]. Going from left to right in [1], we first see that ClF(g) is needed as a reactant. This can be obtained by multiplying reaction [3] by 1/2, which means that the ΔH change is also multiplied by 1/2:

ΔH_3 = 205.6 kJ/mol

ΔH_3a = (1/2) ΔH_3



![3a] ClF(g) +1/2O2(g) -> 1/2Cl2O(g) +1/2OF2(g) !ΔH = 102.8 kJ/mol

Next, we see that F2 is also needed as a reactant. To get this, reverse and halve reaction [2], which means that the ΔH changes sign and is halved:

ΔH_2 = -49.4 kJ/mol

ΔH_2a = -(1/2) ΔH_2

![2a] 1/2O2(g) +F2(g) -> OF2(g) !ΔH= 24.7 kJ/mol.

To get ClF3 as a product, reverse [4], changing the sign of ΔH:

ΔH_4 = 266.7 kJ/mol

ΔH_4a = -ΔH_4



![4a] 1/2Cl2O(g) +3/2OF2(g) -> ClF3(g) +O2(g) !ΔH=-266.7 kJ/mol

Now check to make sure that these reactions add up to the reaction we want. Without cancelling, we have (the equation is balanced because we added up balanced equations):

![3a+2a+4a] ClF +1/2O2 + 1/2O2 +F2 + 1/2Cl2O +3/2OF2 -> 1/2Cl2O +1/2OF2 + OF2 + ClF3 +O2

Reactants 1/2 O2 and 1/2 O2 cancel out product O2; product 1/2 Cl2O cancels reactant 1/2 Cl2O ; and reactant 3/2 OF2 is cancelled by products 1/2 OF2 and OF2. This leaves only reactants ClF and F2 and product ClF3, which are what we want. Since summing these three modified reactions yields the reaction of interest, summing the three modified ΔH values will give the desired ΔH:



ΔH = ΔH_3a + ΔH_2a + ΔH_4a



<h3>Example 5.15: Using Hess’s Law</h3>

What is the standard enthalpy change for the reaction:



!3NO2(g) +H2O(l) -> 2HNO3(aq) +NO(g) !ΔH°=?

<h4>Solution: Using the Equation</h4>

Use the special form of Hess’s law given previously:



@ΔH°_rxn = sum(ν_product ΔHf°_product) - sum(ν_reactant ΔHf°_reactant)

ΔHf°[HNO3(aq)] = -207.4kJ/mol

ΔHf°[NO(g)] = 90.2 kJ/mol

ΔHf°[NO2(g)] = 33.2 kJ/mol

ΔHf°[H2O(l)] = -285.8kJ/mol

ν[HNO3(aq)] = 2

ν[NO(g)] = 1

ν[NO2(g)] = 3

ν[H2O(l)] = 1

ΔH°_rxn = sum(ΔHf°[HNO3(aq)] ν[HNO3(aq)], ΔHf°[NO(g)] ν[NO(g)]) - sum(ν[NO2(g)] ΔHf°[NO2(g)], ν[H2O(l)]  ΔHf°[H2O(l)]) 



<h4>Solution: Supporting Why the General Equation Is Valid</h4>

Alternatively, we can write this reaction as the sum of the decompositions of 3NO2(g) and 1H2O(l) into their constituent elements, and the formation of 2HNO3(aq) and 1NO(g) from their constituent elements. Writing out these reactions, and noting their relationships to the ΔHf° values for these compounds (from <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/back-matter/standard-thermodynamic-properties-for-selected-substances/">Appendix G</a> ), we have:



!3NO2(g) -> 3/2N2(g) +3O2(g) !ΔH1°=-99.6kJ/mol

!H2O(l) -> H2(g) +1/2O2(g) !ΔH2°= +285.8 kJ/mol

!H2(g) +N2(g) +1/2O2(g) -> 2HNO3(aq) !ΔH3°=-414.8kJ/mol

!1/2N2(g) +1/2O2(g) -> NO(g) !ΔH4°= +90.2 kJ/mol

ΔH1°=-99.6kJ/mol

ΔH2°= +285.8 kJ/mol

ΔH3°=-414.8kJ/mol

ΔH4°= +90.2 kJ/mol

!3NO2(g) +H2O(l) -> 2HNO3(aq) +NO(g)

Summing their enthalpy changes gives the value we want to determine:



ΔHrxn°=ΔH1° +ΔH2° +ΔH3° +ΔH4°



#So the standard enthalpy change for this reaction is Δ<em>H</em>° = -138.4 kJ.



Note that this result was obtained by (1) multiplying the ΔHf° of each product by its stoichiometric coefficient and summing those values, (2) multiplying the ΔHf° of each reactant by its stoichiometric coefficient and summing those values, and then (3) subtracting the result found in (2) from the result found in (1). This is also the procedure in using the general equation, as shown.







<h3>Example 6.1: Determining the Frequency and Wavelength of Radiation</h3>

A sodium streetlight gives off yellow light that has a wavelength of 589 nm. What is the frequency of this light?



<h4>Solution</h4>

λ[Na] = 589 nm

ν[Na] = ?

We can rearrange the equation c0 = λ_ ν_ to solve for the frequency:



@ν_ = c0 / λ_

c0 = 2.998e8 m/s

ν[Na] = c0 / λ[Na]

ν[Na] using GHz



<h3>Example 6.2: Calculating the Energy of Radiation</h3>

<h4>Calculating the Energy of Radiation</h4>

When we see light from a neon sign, we are observing radiation from excited neon atoms. If this radiation has a wavelength of 640 nm, what is the energy of the photon being emitted?

<h4>Solution</h4>

λ_neon = 640 nm

E_photon = ?

We use the version of Planck's equation that includes the wavelength, <em>λ</em>.

@E = h_Planck c0 / λ

c0 = 2.998e8 m/s

h_Planck = 6.626e-34 J s

E_photon = h_Planck c0 /λ_neon 



<h3>Example 6.3: Photoelectric Effect</h3>



<h4>Photoelectric Effect</h4>Identify which of the following statements are false and, where necessary, change the italicized word or phrase to make them true, consistent with Einstein's explanation of the photoelectric effect.

(a) Increasing the brightness of incoming light <em>increases</em> the kinetic energy of the ejected electrons.



(b) Increasing the <em>wavelength</em> of incoming light increases the kinetic energy of the ejected electrons.



(c) Increasing the brightness of incoming light <em>increases</em> the number of ejected electrons.



(d) Increasing the <em>frequency</em> of incoming light can increase the number of ejected electrons.



<h4>Solution</h4>

(a) False. Increasing the brightness of incoming light <em>has no effect</em> on the kinetic energy of the ejected electrons. Only energy, not the number or amplitude, of the photons influences the kinetic energy of the electrons.



(b) False. Increasing the <em>frequency</em> of incoming light increases the kinetic energy of the ejected electrons. Frequency is proportional to energy and inversely proportional to wavelength. Frequencies above the threshold value transfer the excess energy into the kinetic energy of the electrons.



(c) True. Because the number of collisions with photons increases with brighter light, the number of ejected electrons increases.



(d) True with regard to the threshold energy binding the electrons to the metal. Below this threshold, electrons are not emitted and above it they are. Once over the threshold value, further increasing the frequency does not increase the number of ejected electrons



<h3>Example 6.4: Calculating the Energy of an Electron in a Bohr Orbit</h3>



<h4>Calculating the Energy of an Electron in a Bohr Orbit</h4>

Early researchers were very excited when they were able to predict the energy of an electron at a particular distance from the nucleus in a hydrogen atom. If a spark promotes the electron in a hydrogen atom into an orbit with n = 3, what is the calculated energy, in joules, of the electron?



<h4>Solution</h4>

n = 3

The energy of the electron is given by this equation:



@ E = -k Z^2/n^2

The atomic number, Z, of hydrogen is 1 and k is 2.179e-18 J.

Z = 1

k = 2.179E-18 J

E = -k Z^2/n^2







<h3>Example 4.7: Balancing Redox Reactions in Acidic Solution</h3>



<h4>Balancing Redox Reactions in Acidic Solution</h4>

Write a balanced equation for the reaction between dichromate ion and iron(II) to yield iron(III) and chromium(III) in acidic solution.



!Cr2O7^2- +Fe^2+ -> Cr^3+ +Fe^3+

<h4>Solution</h4>



Write the two half-reactions.



Each half-reaction will contain one reactant and one product with one element in common.

!Fe^2+ -> Fe^3+

!Cr2O7^2- -> Cr^3+

Balance all elements except oxygen and hydrogen. The iron half-reaction is already balanced, but the chromium half-reaction shows two Cr atoms on the left and one Cr atom on the right. Changing the coefficient on the right side of the equation to 2 achieves balance with regard to Cr atoms.

!Fe^2+ -> Fe^3+

!Cr2O7^2- -> 2Cr^3+

Balance oxygen atoms by adding H2O molecules. The iron half-reaction does not contain O atoms. The chromium half-reaction shows seven O atoms on the left and none on the right, so seven water molecules are added to the right side.

!Fe^2+ -> Fe^3+

!Cr2O7^2- -> 2Cr^3+ +7H2O

Balance hydrogen atoms by adding H+ ions. The iron half-reaction does not contain H atoms. The chromium half-reaction shows 14 H atoms on the right and none on the left, so 14 hydrogen ions are added to the left side.

!Fe^2+ -> Fe^3+

!Cr2O7^2- +14H+ -> 2Cr^3+ +7H2O

Balance charge by adding electrons. The iron half-reaction shows a total charge of 2⊕ on the left side (one Fe^2+ ion) and 3⊕ on the right side (one Fe^3+ ion). Adding one electron to the right side bring that side’s total charge to (3⊕)  + (1⊖) = 2⊕, and charge balance is achieved.



The chromium half-reaction shows a total charge of (1 * 2⊖)  + (14 * 1⊕ ) = 12⊕  on the left side (one Cr2O7^2- ion and 14 H+ ions). The total charge on the right side is (2 * 3⊕) = 6⊕ (two Cr^3+ ions). Adding six electrons to the left side will bring that side’s total charge to (12⊕   + 6⊖) = 6⊕ , and charge balance is achieved.

!Fe^2+ -> Fe^3+ +e-

!Cr2O7^2- +14H+ +6e- -> 2Cr^3+ +7H2O

Multiply the two half-reactions so the number of electrons in one reaction equals the number of electrons in the other reaction. To be consistent with mass conservation, and the idea that redox reactions involve the transfer (not creation or destruction) of electrons, the iron half-reaction’s coefficient must be multiplied by 6.

!6Fe^2+ -> 6Fe^3+ +6e-

!Cr2O7^2- +6e- +14H + -> 2Cr^3+ +7H2O

Add the balanced half-reactions and cancel species that appear on both sides of the equation.

!6Fe^2+ +Cr2O7^2- +6e- +14H+ -> 6Fe^3+ +6e- +2Cr^3+ +7H2O

Only the six electrons are redundant species. Removing them from each side of the equation yields the simplified, balanced equation here:

! 6Fe^2+ +Cr2O7^2- +14H+ -> 6Fe^3+ +2Cr^3+ +7H2O !balanced chemical equation





A final check of atom and charge balance confirms the equation is balanced.

@Fe_reactant = Fe_product = 6

@Cr_reactant = Cr_product = 2

@O_reactant = O_product = 7

@H_reactant = H_product = 14

@Charge_reactant = Charge_product = 24



<h3>Example 6.5: Calculating the Energy and Wavelength of Electron Transitions in a One–electron (Bohr) System</h3>

What is the energy (in joules) and the wavelength (in meters) of the line in the spectrum of hydrogen that represents the movement of an electron from Bohr orbit with n = 4 to the orbit with n = 6? In what part of the electromagnetic spectrum do we find this radiation?



<h4>Solution</h4>

In this case, the electron starts out with n = 4 and comes to rest in the n = 6 orbit, so 

n1 = 4

n2 = 6

The difference in energy between the two states is given by this expression:



@ΔE_electron = E1_electron - E2_electron = 2.179E-18J (1/n1^2-1/n2^2)

ΔE_electron = 2.179E-18J (1/n1^2-1/n2^2)

This energy difference is positive, indicating a photon enters the system (is absorbed) to excite the electron from then = 4 orbit up to the n = 6 orbit. The wavelength of a photon with this energy is found by the expression 

@E_photon = h_Planck c0 / λ_photon

E_photon = ΔE_electron

c0 = 2.998E8 m/s

h_Planck = 6.626E-34 J s

Rearrangement gives:

λ = h_Planck c0 / E_photon

λ using μm

From <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_06_01_emspectrum.jpg">Figure 3</a>, we can see that this wavelength is found in the infrared portion of the electromagnetic spectrum.



<h3>Example 6.6: Calculating the Wavelength of a Particle</h3>

If an electron travels at a velocity of 1.000e7 m/s and has a mass of 9.109e-28 g, what is its wavelength?



<h4>Solution</h4>

v_elec = 1.000E7 m/s

m_elec = 9.109E-28 g

We can use de Broglie’s equation to solve this problem

@λ = h_Planck / (m_ v_)

h_Planck = 6.626E-34 J s

λ_elec = h_Planck / (m_elec v_elec)

λ_elec using Å

This is a small value, but it is significantly larger than the size of an electron in the classical (particle) view. This size is the same order of magnitude as the size of an atom. This means that electron wavelike behavior is going to be noticeable in an atom.



<h3>Example 6.7: Working with Shells and Subshells</h3>



Indicate the number of subshells, the number of orbitals in each subshell, and the values of l_  and m_ℓ  for the orbitals in the n_  = 4 shell of an atom.



<h4>Solution</h4>

For n_  = 4, ℓ_  can have values of 0, 1, 2, and 3. Thus, s_ , p_ , d_ , and f_  subshells are found in the n_  = 4 shell of an atom. For ℓ_  = 0 (the s_  subshell), m_ℓ  can only be 0. Thus, there is only one [4s]  orbital. For ℓ_  = 1 (p_ -type orbitals), m_ℓ  can have values of –1, 0, +1, so we find three 4p_  orbitals. For ℓ_  = 2 (d_ -type orbitals), m_ℓ  can have values of -2, -1, 0, +1, +2, so we have five 4d_  orbitals. When ℓ_  = 3 (f_ -type orbitals), m_ℓ  can have values of -3, -2, -1, 0, +1, +2, +3, and we can have seven 4f_  orbitals. Thus, we find a total of 16 orbitals in the n_  = 4 shell of an atom.



<h3>Example 6.8: Maximum Number of Electrons</h3>



Calculate the maximum number of electrons that can occupy a shell with (a) n_ equal to  2, (b) n_ equal to 5, and (c) n_ as a variable. Note you are only looking at the orbitals with the specified n_ value, not those at lower energies.



<h4>Solution</h4>

(a) When n_ is 2, there are four orbitals (a single 2s orbital, and three orbitals labeled 2p). These four orbitals can contain eight electrons.



(b) When n_ is 5, there are five subshells of orbitals that we need to sum:





1 orbital labeled 5s

3 orbitals labeled 5p

5 orbitals labeled 5d

7 orbitals labeled 5f

9 orbitals labeled 5g

25 orbitals total

Again, each orbital holds two electrons, so 50 electrons can fit in this shell.



(c) The number of orbitals in any shell n_ will be N_orbital = n_ ^ 2 . There can be up to two electrons in each orbital, so the maximum number of electrons will be N_electrons = 2 * n_ ^ 2



<h3>Example 6.10: Quantum Numbers and Electron Configurations</h3>

What is the electron configuration and orbital diagram for a phosphorus atom? What are the four quantum numbers for the last electron added?



<h4>Solution</h4>

The atomic number of phosphorus is 15. Thus, a phosphorus atom contains 15 electrons. The order of filling of the energy levels is 1s, 2s, 2p, 3s, 3p, 4s, . . . The 15 electrons of the phosphorus atom will fill up to the 3p orbital, which will contain three electrons:

!1s^2 2s^2 2p^6 3s^2 3p^3



{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_06_04_PhosphOrb_img.jpg"}



The last electron added is a 3p electron. Therefore, n = 3 and, for a p-type orbital, l = 1. The m_l value could be -1, 0, or +1. The three p orbitals are degenerate, so any of these m_l values is correct. For unpaired electrons, convention assigns the value of +1/2 for the spin quantum number; thus, m_s is +1/2.



<h3>Example 6.11: Predicting Electron Configurations of Ions</h3>



<h4>Predicting Electron Configurations of Ions</h4>

What is the electron configuration and orbital diagram of:



(a) Na^+



(b) P^3–



(c) Al^2+



(d) Fe^2+



(e) Sm^3+



<h4>Solution</h4>

First, write out the electron configuration for each parent atom. We have chosen to show the full, unabbreviated configurations to provide more practice for students who want it, but listing the core-abbreviated electron configurations is also acceptable.



Next, determine whether an electron is gained or lost. Remember electrons are negatively charged, so ions with a positive charge have <em>lost</em> an electron. For main group elements, the last orbital gains or loses the electron. For transition metals, the last s orbital loses an electron before the d orbitals.



! Na: 1s^2 2s^2 2p^6 3s^1

Sodium cation loses one electron, so Na+ : 

![a] Na+: 1s^2 2s^2 2p^6

<hr>

! P: 1s^2 2s^2 2p^6 3s^2 3p^3

Phosphorus trianion gains three electrons, so P^3- :

![b] P^31: 1s^22s^22p^63s^23p^6

<hr>



! Al: 1s^2 2s^2 2p^6 3s^2 3p^1 

Aluminum dication loses two electrons Al^2+:

![c] Al^2+: 1s^22s^22p^63s^1

<hr>



! Fe: 1s^2 2s^2 2p^6 3s^2 3p^6 4s^2 3d^6

Iron(II) loses two electrons and, since it is a transition metal, they are removed from the 4s orbital Fe^2+: 

![d] Fe^2+: 1s^2 2s^2 2p^6 3s^2 3p^6 3d^6

<hr>



! Sm: 1s^2 2s^2 2p^6 3s^2 3p^6 4s^2 3d^10 4p^6 5s^2 4d^10 5p^6 6s^2 4f^6

 Samarium trication loses three electrons. The first two will be lost from the 6s orbital, and the final one is removed from the 4f orbital. 

![e] Sm^3+: 1s^2 2s^2 2p^6 3s^2 3p^6 4s^2 3d^10 4p^6 5s^2 4d^10 5p^6 4f^5



<h3>Example 6.12: Sorting Atomic Radii</h3>



Predict the order of increasing covalent radius for Ge, Fl, Br, Kr.



<h4>Solution</h4>

Radius increases as we move down a group, so Ge < Fl (Note: Fl is the symbol for flerovium, element 114, NOT fluorine). Radius decreases as we move across a period, so Kr < Br < Ge. Putting the trends together, we obtain Kr < Br < Ge < Fl.



<h3>Example 6.13: Ranking Ionization Energies</h3>



Predict the order of increasing energy for the following processes: E_I1 for Al, E_I1 for Tl, E_I2 for Na, E_I3 for Al.



<h4>Solution</h4>

Removing the 6p^1 electron from Tl is easier than removing the 3p^1 electron from Al because the higher n orbital is farther from the nucleus, so IE1(Tl) < IE1(Al). Ionizing the third electron from Al(Al^2+ -> Al^3+ +e-) requires more energy because the cation Al^2+ exerts a stronger pull on the electron than the neutral Al atom, so IE1(Al) < IE3(Al). The second ionization energy for sodium removes a core electron, which is a much higher energy process than removing valence electrons. Putting this all together, we obtain: IE1(Tl) < IE1(Al) < IE3(Al) < IE2(Na).

<h3>Example 6.6b: Calculating the Wavelength of a Particle</h3>
Calculate the wavelength of a softball with a mass of 100 g traveling at a velocity of 35 m s^–1, assuming that it can be modeled as a single particle.
<h4>Answer:</h4>
We never think of a thrown softball having a wavelength, since this wavelength is so small it is impossible for our senses or any known instrument to detect (strictly speaking, the wavelength of a real baseball would correspond to the wavelengths of its constituent atoms and molecules, which, while much larger than this value, would still be microscopically tiny). The de Broglie wavelength is only appreciable for matter that has a very small mass and/or a very high velocity.
m_softball = 100. g
v_softball = 35. m/s
λ_Broglie = ?
@λ_Broglie = h_Planck / (m_ v)
h_Planck = 6.626070040(81)e-34 J s
λ_Broglie = h_Planck / (m_softball v_softball )
Think about it: How fast is the baseball in miles per hour?
a) More than 35 mph
b) Less than 35 mph
c) It depends on its mass
d) The speed of the baseball is not measurable according to Heisenberg's uncertainty principle

<h3>Example J.1b: Area of a table</h3>

If our table is 2.1 m in length and 1.4 m in width and has the shape of a rectangle, what is its area?

<h4>Answer:</h4>


L_table = 2.1 m
W_table = 1.4 m
A_table = ?
A_table = L_table * W_table
The table has an area of 2.94 square meters.

<h3>Example J.1: Area of a table</h3>

If our table is 1.80 m in length and 1.20 m in width and has the shape of a rectangle, what is its area? We might want to paint the top of the table, and need to know its area to figure out how much paint we need.

<h4>Solution</h4>
First we write down what we know, giving it names that remind us what the values are about:

L_table = 1.80 m
W_table = 1.20 m
Then we write down what we are planning on calculating. Here, it is the area of the table, A_table
A_table = ?
Now we make a plan. The area A_ of a rectangle is equal to its length L_ times its width W_ . The formula would be:
@ A_ = L_ * W_
Finally, we can ask PQcalc to do the calculation for us:
A_table = L_table * W_table
The table has an area of 2.16 square meters.

bla bla

The table has an area of 7.0 meters.

<h3>Example J.2: Circumference of a table</h3>

If our table is 1.80 m in length and 1.20 m in width and has the shape of a rectangle, what is its circumference (or perimeter)? We might want to glue a ribbon around the edge, and we want to know how long it has to be.

<h4>Solution</h4>

L_table = 1.80 m
W_table = 1.20 m
Then we write down what we are planning on calculating. Here, it is the perimeter
perimeter = ?
Now we make a plan. The perimeter of a rectangle is equal to its twice its length L_ plus twice its width W_ . The formula would be:
@ perimeter = 2 * L_ + 2 * W_
Finally, we can ask PQcalc to do the calculation for us:
perimeter = 2 L_table + 2 W_table
The table has a perimeter of 6.00 meters.
