<h3>Example 3.3: Deriving Moles from Grams for an Element</h3>

<h4>Deriving Moles from Grams for an Element</h4>

According to nutritional guidelines from the US Department of Agriculture, the estimated average requirement for dietary potassium is 4.7 g. What is the estimated average requirement of potassium in moles?

<h4>Solution</h4>

The mass of K is provided, and the corresponding amount of K in moles is requested. 

m[K] = 4.7 g

n[K] = ?

Referring to the periodic table, the molar mass of K is 39.10 g/mol. The given mass of K (4.7 g) is a bit more than one-tenth of the mass of one mole (39.10 g), so a reasonable “ballpark” estimate of the number of moles would be slightly greater than 0.1 mol.

The molar amount of a substance may be calculated by dividing its mass (g) by its molar mass (g/mol):

n[K] = m[K] / M[K]

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_03_02_potassium_img.jpg"}

The factor-label method supports this mathematical approach since the unit “g” cancels and the answer has units of “mol:”

The calculated chemical amount of K (0.12 mol) is consistent with our ballpark expectation, since it is a bit greater than 0.1 mol.
<h3>Example 3.4: Deriving Grams from Moles for an Element</h3>

<h4>Deriving Grams from Moles for an Element</h4>

A liter of air contains 9.2e-4 mol argon. What is the mass of Ar in a liter of air?

<h4>Solution</h4>

n[Ar] = 9.2e-4 mol

m[Ar] = ?

The chemical amount of Ar is provided and must be used to derive the corresponding mass in grams. Since the amount of Ar is less than 1 mole, the mass will be less than the mass of 1 mole of Ar, approximately 40 g. The molar amount in question is approximately one-one thousandth (~10^-3) of a mole, and so the corresponding mass should be roughly one-one thousandth of the molar mass (~0.04 g):

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_03_02_argon_img.jpg"}

The molar mass M is defined as

@ M_ = m_ /n_

Solving for m gives

@ m_ = n_ * M_

m[Ar] = n[Ar] * M[Ar]

The result is in agreement with our expectations, around 0.04 g Ar.
<h3>Example 3.5: Deriving Number of Atoms from Mass for an Element</h3>

<h4>Deriving Number of Atoms from Mass for an Element</h4>

Copper is commonly used to fabricate electrical wire (<a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_03_02_copper.jpg">Figure 8</a>). How many copper atoms are in 5.00 g of copper wire?

<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_03_02_copper.jpg">Figure 8Copper wire is composed of many, many atoms of Cu. (credit: Emilian Robert Vicol)

<h4>Solution</h4>

m[Cu] = 5.00 g

N[Cu]atoms = ?

The number of Cu atoms in the wire may be conveniently derived from its mass by a two-step computation: first calculating the chemical amount of Cu, and then using Avogadro’s constant N_A to convert this amount to number of Cu atoms:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_03_02_copperMoles_img.jpg"}

Considering that the provided sample mass (5.00 g) is a little less than one-tenth the mass of 1 mole of Cu (~64 g), a reasonable estimate for the number of atoms in the sample would be on the order of one-tenth N_A, or approximately 1e22 Cu atoms. Carrying out the two-step computation yields:

n[Cu] = m[Cu] / M[Cu]

N_A = 6.022e23 / mol

N[Cu]atoms = N_A n[Cu]

The computed result is on the order of 1e22 as expected.
<h3>Example 3.6: Deriving Moles from Grams for a Compound</h3>

Our bodies synthesize protein from amino acids. One of these amino acids is glycine, which has the molecular formula C2H5O2N. How many moles of glycine molecules are contained in 28.35 g of glycine?

<h4>Solution</h4>

m_glycine = 28.35 g

n_glycine = ?

We can derive the number of moles of a compound from its mass following the same procedure we used for an element in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/3-1-formula-mass-and-the-mole-concept/#fs-idp76540048">Example 2</a>:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_03_02_glycine_img.jpg", width="40%"}

The molar mass of glycine is required for this calculation. One mole of glycine, C2H5O2N, contains 2 moles of carbon, 5 moles of hydrogen, 2 moles of oxygen, and 1 mole of nitrogen:

M[C2H5O2N] = sum(2 M[C], 5 M[H], 2 M[O], 1M[N]) 

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_03_01_glycinemass_img.jpg", width="50%"}

The provided mass of glycine (~28 g) is a bit more than one-third the molar mass (~75 g/mol), so we would expect the computed result to be a bit greater than one-third of a mole (~0.33 mol). Dividing the compound’s mass by its molar mass yields:

n_glycine = m_glycine / M[C2H5O2N]

This result is consistent with our rough estimate.
<h3>Example 3.7: Deriving Grams from Moles for a Compound</h3>

Vitamin C is a covalent compound with the molecular formula C6H8O6. The recommended daily dietary allowance of vitamin C for children aged 4–8 years is 1.42e-4 mol. What is the mass of this allowance in grams?

<h4>Solution</h4>

n_VitC = 1.42e-4 mol

m_VitC = ?

As for elements, the mass of a compound can be derived from its chemical amount as shown:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_03_02_vitC_img.jpg"}

The molar mass for this compound is computed to be 176.124 g/mol. The given amount is a very small fraction of a mole (~10^-4 or one-ten thousandth); therefore, we would expect the corresponding mass to be about one-ten thousandth of the molar mass (~0.02 g). Performing the calculation, we get:
m_VitC = n_VitC * M[C6H8O6]

This is consistent with the anticipated result.
<h3>Example 4.9: Number of Product Molecules Generated by a Reaction</h3>

<h4>Number of Product Molecules Generated by a Reaction</h4>

How many carbon dioxide molecules are produced when 0.75 mol of propane is combusted according to this equation?

!C3H8 +5O2 -> 3CO2 +4H2O

<h4>Solution</h4>

n[C3H8] = 0.75 mol

The approach here is the same as for <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/4-3-reaction-stoichiometry/#fs-idp48900096">Example 7</a>, though the absolute number of molecules is requested, not the number of moles of molecules. This will simply require use of Avogadro’s constant relating chemical amount and number of molecules.

The balanced equation shows that carbon dioxide is produced from propane in a 3:1 ratio:

@ n[CO2] / n[C3H8] = 3/1

Using this stoichiometric factor, the provided molar amount of propane, and Avogadro’s number,

n[CO2] = (1/3) n[C3H8]

N_A = 6.022E23 mol^-1

N[CO2 molecules] = N_A * n[CO2]

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_04_03_moleratio2_img.jpg" width="50%"}
<h3>Example 3.20: Volume of a Diluted Solution</h3>

What volume of 0.12 M HBr can be prepared from 11 mL (0.011 L) of 0.45 M HBr?

<h4>Solution</h4>

We are given the volume and concentration of a stock solution, V1 and c1, and the concentration of the resultant diluted solution, c2. We need to find the volume of the diluted solution, V2. 

c1 = 0.45 M

V1 = 0.011 L

c2 = 0.12 M

V2 = ?

Since the diluted concentration (0.12 M) is slightly more than one-fourth the original concentration (0.45 M), we would expect the volume of the diluted solution to be roughly four times the original volume, or around 44 mL. 

To calculate the actual volume, we rearrange the dilution equation in order to isolate V2.

@c1 V1 = c2 V2

Solving for the unknown volume yields:

V2 = V1 (c1/c2)

The volume of the 0.12 M solution is 0.041 L (41 mL). The result is reasonable and compares well with our rough estimate.
<h3>Example 3.21: Volume of a Concentrated Solution Needed for Dilution</h3>

What volume of 1.59 M KOH is required to prepare 5.00 L of 0.100 M KOH?

<h4>Solution</h4>

We are given the concentration of a stock solution, c1, and the volume and concentration of the resultant diluted solution, V2 and c2. We need to find the volume of the stock solution, V1. 

c1 = 1.59 M

c2 = 0.100 M

V2= 5.00 L

V1 = ?

Since the concentration of the diluted solution 0.100 M is roughly one-sixteenth that of the stock solution (1.59 M), we would expect the volume of the stock solution to be about one-sixteenth that of the diluted solution, or around 0.3 liters. 

To obtain the accurate volume, we rearrange the dilution equation in order to isolate V1:

@ c1 V1 = c2 V2

V1 = V2 (c2 / c1)

Thus, we would need 0.314 L of the 1.59 M solution to prepare the desired solution. This result is consistent with our rough estimate.
<h3>Example 4.1: Balancing Chemical Equations</h3>

Write a balanced equation for the reaction of molecular nitrogen (N2) and oxygen (O2) to form dinitrogen pentoxide.

<h4>Solution</h4>

First, write the unbalanced equation.

!N2 +O2 -> N2O5 !unbalanced

Next, count the number of each type of atom present in the unbalanced equation.

@atoms_N_left = 2

@atoms_N_right = 2

@atoms_O_left = 2

@atoms_O_right = 5

Though nitrogen is balanced, changes in coefficients are needed to balance the number of oxygen atoms. To balance the number of oxygen atoms, a reasonable first attempt would be to change the coefficients for the O2 and N2O5 to integers that will yield 10 O atoms (the least common multiple for the O atom subscripts in these two formulas).

!N2 +5O2 -> 2N2O5

@atoms_N_left = 2

@atoms_N_right = 2 * 2

@atoms_O_left = 5 * 2

@atoms_O_right = 2 * 5

The N atom balance has been upset by this change; it is restored by changing the coefficient for the reactant N2 to 2.

!2N2 +5O2 -> 2N2O5 !balanced

@atomsN_left = 2 * 2

@atomsN_right = 2 * 2

@atomsO_left = 5 * 2

@atomsO_right = 2 * 5

The numbers of N and O atoms on either side of the equation are now equal, and so the equation is balanced.
<h3>Example 4.2: Molecular and Ionic Equations</h3>

When carbon dioxide is dissolved in an aqueous solution of sodium hydroxide, the mixture reacts to yield aqueous sodium carbonate and liquid water. Write balanced molecular, complete ionic, and net ionic equations for this process.

<h4>Solution</h4>

Begin by identifying formulas for the reactants and products and arranging them properly in chemical equation form:

!CO2(aq) +NaOH(aq) -> Na2CO3(aq) +H2O(l) !(unbalanced)

Balance is achieved easily in this case by changing the coefficient for NaOH to 2, resulting in the molecular equation for this reaction:

!CO2(aq) +2NaOH(aq) -> Na2CO3(aq) +H2O(l) ! balanced

The two dissolved ionic compounds, NaOH and Na2CO3, can be represented as dissociated ions to yield the complete ionic equation:

!CO2(aq) +2Na+ (aq) +2OH- (aq) -> 2Na+ (aq) +CO3^2- (aq) +H2O(l) ! Complete ionic

Finally, identify the spectator ion(s), in this case Na+(aq), and remove it from each side of the equation to generate the net ionic equation:

!CO2(aq) +2OH-(aq) -> CO3^2-(aq) +H2O(l) ! Net ionic
<h3>Example 4.3: Predicting Precipitation Reactions</h3>

Predict the result of mixing reasonably concentrated solutions of the following ionic compounds. If precipitation is expected, write a balanced net ionic equation for the reaction.

(a) potassium sulfate and barium nitrate

(b) lithium chloride and silver acetate

(c) lead nitrate and ammonium carbonate

<h4>Solution</h4>

(a) The two possible products for this combination are KNO3 and BaSO4. The solubility guidelines indicate BaSO4 is insoluble, and so a precipitation reaction is expected. The net ionic equation for this reaction, derived in the manner detailed in the previous module, is

!Ba^2+(aq) +SO4^2-(aq) -> BaSO4(s)

(b) The two possible products for this combination are LiC2H3O2 and AgCl. The solubility guidelines indicate AgCl is insoluble, and so a precipitation reaction is expected. The net ionic equation for this reaction, derived in the manner detailed in the previous module, is

!Ag+(aq) +Cl-(aq) -> AgCl(s)

(c) The two possible products for this combination are PbCO3 and NH4NO3. The solubility guidelines indicate PbCO3 is insoluble, and so a precipitation reaction is expected. The net ionic equation for this reaction, derived in the manner detailed in the previous module, is

!Pb^2+(aq) +CO3^2-(aq) -> PbCO3(s)
<h3>Example 4.4: Writing Equations for Acid-Base Reactions</h3>

Write balanced chemical equations for the acid-base reactions described here:

(a) the weak acid hydrogen hypochlorite reacts with water

(b) a solution of barium hydroxide is neutralized with a solution of nitric acid

<h4>Solution</h4>

(a) The two reactants are provided, HOCl and H2O. Since the substance is reported to be an acid, its reaction with water will involve the transfer of H+ from HOCl to H2O to generate hydronium ions, H3O+ and hypochlorite ions, OCl-.

!HOCl(aq) +H2O(l) <=> OCl-(aq) +H3O+(aq)

A double-arrow is appropriate in this equation because it indicates the HOCl is a weak acid that has not reacted completely.

(b) The two reactants are provided, Ba(OH)2 and HNO3. Since this is a neutralization reaction, the two products will be water and a salt composed of the cation of the ionic hydroxide (Ba^2+) and the anion generated when the acid transfers its hydrogen ion (NO3- ).

!Ba(OH)2(aq) +2HNO3(aq) -> Ba(NO3)2(aq) +2H2O(l)
<h3>Example 4.5: Assigning Oxidation Numbers</h3>

Follow the guidelines in this section of the text to assign oxidation numbers to all the elements in the following species:

(a) H2S

(b) SO3^2-

(c) Na2SO4

<h4>Solution</h4>

(a) According to guideline 1, the oxidation number for H is +1.

Using this oxidation number and the compound’s formula, guideline 4 may then be used to calculate the oxidation number for sulfur:

@charge[H2S] = 0 = (2 *  +1) + (1 * ON[S])

@ON[S] = 0 - (2 * +1) = -2

(b) Guideline 3 suggests the oxidation number for oxygen is -2.

Using this oxidation number and the ion’s formula, guideline 4 may then be used to calculate the oxidation number for sulfur:

@charge[SO3^2-] = -2 = (3 * -2)+(1 * ON[S])

@ON[S] = -2-(3×-2)=+4

(c) For ionic compounds, it’s convenient to assign oxidation numbers for the cation and anion separately.

According to guideline 2, the oxidation number for sodium is +1.

Assuming the usual oxidation number for oxygen (-2 per guideline 3), the oxidation number for sulfur is calculated as directed by guideline 4:

@charge[SO4^2-] = -2 = (4 * -2)+(1 * ON[S])

@ON[S] = -2 - (4 * -2) = +6
<h3>Example 4.6: Describing Redox Reactions</h3>

Identify which equations represent redox reactions, providing a name for the reaction if appropriate. For those reactions identified as redox, name the oxidant and reductant.

![a] ZnCO3(s) -> ZnO(s) +CO2(g)

![b] 2Ga(l) +3Br2(l) -> 2GaBr3(s)

![c] 2H2O2(aq) -> 2H2O(l) +O2(g)

![d] BaCl2(aq) +K2SO4(aq) -> BaSO4(s) +2KCl(aq)

![e] C2H4(g) +3O2(g) -> 2CO2(g) +2H2O(l)

<h4>Solution</h4>

Redox reactions are identified per definition if one or more elements undergo a change in oxidation number.

(a) This is not a redox reaction, since oxidation numbers remain unchanged for all elements.

(b) This is a redox reaction. Gallium is oxidized, its oxidation number increasing from 0 in Ga(l) to  +3 in GaBr3(s). The reducing agent is Ga(l). Bromine is reduced, its oxidation number decreasing from 0 in Br2(l) to -1 in GaBr3(s). The oxidizing agent is Br2(l).

(c) This is a redox reaction. It is a particularly interesting process, as it involves the same element, oxygen, undergoing both oxidation and reduction (a so-called disproportionation reaction). Oxygen is oxidized, its oxidation number increasing from -1 in H2O2(aq) to 0 in O2(g). Oxygen is also reduced, its oxidation number decreasing from -1 in H2O2(aq) to -2 in H2O(l). For disproportionation reactions, the same substance functions as an oxidant and a reductant.

(d) This is not a redox reaction, since oxidation numbers remain unchanged for all elements.

(e) This is a redox reaction (combustion). Carbon is oxidized, its oxidation number increasing from -2 in C2H4(g) to  +4 in CO2(g). The reducing agent (fuel) is C2H4(g). Oxygen is reduced, its oxidation number decreasing from 0 in O2(g) to -2 in H2O(l). The oxidizing agent is O2(g).
<h3>Example 4.6: Describing Redox Reactions</h3>

Identify which equations represent redox reactions, providing a name for the reaction if appropriate. For those reactions identified as redox, name the oxidant and reductant.

![a] ZnCO3(s) -> ZnO(s) +CO2(g)

![b] 2Ga(l) +3Br2(l) -> 2GaBr3(s)

![c] 2H2O2(aq) -> 2H2O(l) +O2(g)

![d] BaCl2(aq) +K2SO4(aq) -> BaSO4(s) +2KCl(aq)

![e] C2H4(g) +3O2(g) -> 2CO2(g) +2H2O(l)

<h4>Solution</h4>

Redox reactions are identified per definition if one or more elements undergo a change in oxidation number.

(a) This is not a redox reaction, since oxidation numbers remain unchanged for all elements.

(b) This is a redox reaction. Gallium is oxidized, its oxidation number increasing from 0 in Ga(l) to  +3 in GaBr3(s). The reducing agent is Ga(l). Bromine is reduced, its oxidation number decreasing from 0 in Br2(l) to -1 in GaBr3(s). The oxidizing agent is Br2(l).

(c) This is a redox reaction. It is a particularly interesting process, as it involves the same element, oxygen, undergoing both oxidation and reduction (a so-called disproportionation reaction). Oxygen is oxidized, its oxidation number increasing from -1 in H2O2(aq) to 0 in O2(g). Oxygen is also reduced, its oxidation number decreasing from -1 in H2O2(aq) to -2 in H2O(l). For disproportionation reactions, the same substance functions as an oxidant and a reductant.

(d) This is not a redox reaction, since oxidation numbers remain unchanged for all elements.

(e) This is a redox reaction (combustion). Carbon is oxidized, its oxidation number increasing from -2 in C2H4(g) to  +4 in CO2(g). The reducing agent (fuel) is C2H4(g). Oxygen is reduced, its oxidation number decreasing from 0 in O2(g) to -2 in H2O(l). The oxidizing agent is O2(g).
<h3>Example 4.7: Balancing Redox Reactions in Acidic Solution</h3>
<h4>Balancing Redox Reactions in Acidic Solution</h4>
Write a balanced equation for the reaction between dichromate ion and iron(II) to yield iron(III) and chromium(III) in acidic solution.
!Cr2O7^2- +Fe^2+ -> Cr3+ +Fe^3+
<h4>Solution</h4>
Write the two half-reactions.
Each half-reaction will contain one reactant and one product with one element in common.
!Fe^2+ -> Fe^3+
!Cr2O7^2- -> Cr^3+
Balance all elements except oxygen and hydrogen. The iron half-reaction is already balanced, but the chromium half-reaction shows two Cr atoms on the left and one Cr atom on the right. Changing the coefficient on the right side of the equation to 2 achieves balance with regard to Cr atoms.
!Fe^2+ -> Fe^3+
!Cr2O7^2- -> 2Cr^3+
Balance oxygen atoms by adding H2O molecules. The iron half-reaction does not contain O atoms. The chromium half-reaction shows seven O atoms on the left and none on the right, so seven water molecules are added to the right side.
!Fe^2+ -> Fe^3+
!Cr2O7^2- -> 2Cr^3+ +7H2O
Balance hydrogen atoms by adding H^ +ions. The iron half-reaction does not contain H atoms. The chromium half-reaction shows 14 H atoms on the right and none on the left, so 14 hydrogen ions are added to the left side.
!Fe^2+ -> Fe^3+
!Cr2O7^2- +14H+ -> 2Cr^3+ +7H2O
Balance charge by adding electrons. The iron half-reaction shows a total charge of 2 + on the left side (1 Fe^2+ ion) and 3 + on the right side (1 Fe^3+ ion). Adding one electron to the right side bring that side’s total charge to (3+)  + (1-) = 2+, and charge balance is achieved.
The chromium half-reaction shows a total charge of (1 × 2-)  + (14 × 1 +) = 12 + on the left side (1 Cr2O7^2- ion and 14 H+ ions). The total charge on the right side is (2 × 3 +) = 6  + (2 Cr^3+ ions). Adding six electrons to the left side will bring that side’s total charge to (12 +  + 6-) = 6 +, and charge balance is achieved.
!Fe^2+ -> Fe^3+ +e-
!Cr2O7^2- +14H+ +6e- -> 2Cr^3+ +7H2O
Multiply the two half-reactions so the number of electrons in one reaction equals the number of electrons in the other reaction. To be consistent with mass conservation, and the idea that redox reactions involve the transfer (not creation or destruction) of electrons, the iron half-reaction’s coefficient must be multiplied by 6.
!6Fe^2+ -> 6Fe^3+ +6e-
!Cr2O7^2- +6e- +14H+ -> 2Cr^3+ +7H2O
Add the balanced half-reactions and cancel species that appear on both sides of the equation.
!6Fe^2+ +Cr2O7^2- +6e- +14H+ -> 6Fe^3+ +6e- +2Cr^3+ +7H2O
Only the six electrons are redundant species. Removing them from each side of the equation yields the simplified, balanced equation here:
!6Fe^2+ +Cr2O7^2- +14H+ -> 6Fe^3+ +2Cr^3+ +7H2O
A final check of atom and charge balance confirms the equation is balanced.
@Fe_reactant = Fe_product = 6
@Cr_reactant = Cr_product = 2
@O_reactant = O_product = 7
@H_reactant = H_product = 14
@Charge_reactant = Charge_product = 24
<h3>Example 4.8: Moles of Reactant Required in a Reaction</h3>

<h4>Moles of Reactant Required in a Reaction</h4>

How many moles of I2 are required to react with 0.429 mol of Al according to the following equation (see <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_04_03_iodine.jpg">Figure 9</a>)?

!2Al +3I2 -> 2AlI3

<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_04_03_iodine.jpg">Figure 9 Aluminum and iodine react to produce aluminum iodide. The heat of the reaction vaporizes some of the solid iodine as a purple vapor. (credit: modification of work by Mark Ott)

<h4>Solution</h4>

Referring to the balanced chemical equation, the stoichiometric factors relating the two substances of interest are
ν[I2] = 3
ν[Al] = 2
@ n[I2] / n[Al] = (ν[I2]/ν[Al])

The chemical amount of iodine is derived by multiplying the provided chemical amount of aluminum by this factor:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_04_03_moleratio1_img.jpg"}

@n[I2] = (ν[I2]/ν[Al]) n[Al]

n[Al] = 0.429 mol

n[I2] = (ν[I2]/ν[Al]) n[Al]
<h3>Example 4.10: Relating Masses of Reactants and Products</h3>

<h4>Relating Masses of Reactants and Products</h4>

What mass of sodium hydroxide, NaOH, would be required to produce 16 g of the antacid milk of magnesia [magnesium hydroxide, Mg(OH)2] by the following reaction?

!MgCl2(aq) +2NaOH(aq) -> Mg(OH)2(s) +2NaCl(aq)

<h4>Solution</h4>

m[Mg(OH)2] = 16. g

The approach used previously in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/4-3-reaction-stoichiometry/#fs-idp48900096">Example 7</a> and <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/4-3-reaction-stoichiometry/#fs-idp9124448">Example 8</a> is likewise used here; that is, we must derive an appropriate stoichiometric factor from the balanced chemical equation and use it to relate the amounts of the two substances of interest. In this case, however, masses (not molar amounts) are provided and requested, so additional steps of the sort learned in the previous chapter are required. The calculations required are outlined in this flowchart:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_04_03_map2_img.jpg", width="40%"}

@ n[NaOH] / n[Mg(OH)2] = 2/1

n[Mg(OH)2] = m[Mg(OH)2] / M[Mg(OH)2]

n[NaOH] = (2/1) n[Mg(OH)2]

m[NaOH] = n[NaOH] * M[NaOH]
<h3>Example 4.10: Relating Masses of Reactants and Products</h3>

<h4>Relating Masses of Reactants and Products</h4>

What mass of sodium hydroxide, NaOH, would be required to produce 16 g of the antacid milk of magnesia [magnesium hydroxide, Mg(OH)2] by the following reaction?

!MgCl2(aq) +2NaOH(aq) -> Mg(OH)2(s) +2NaCl(aq)

<h4>Solution</h4>

m[Mg(OH)2] = 16. g

The approach used previously in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/4-3-reaction-stoichiometry/#fs-idp48900096">Example 7</a> and <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/4-3-reaction-stoichiometry/#fs-idp9124448">Example 8</a> is likewise used here; that is, we must derive an appropriate stoichiometric factor from the balanced chemical equation and use it to relate the amounts of the two substances of interest. In this case, however, masses (not molar amounts) are provided and requested, so additional steps of the sort learned in the previous chapter are required. The calculations required are outlined in this flowchart:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_04_03_map2_img.jpg", width="40%"}

@ n[NaOH] / n[Mg(OH)2] = 2/1

n[Mg(OH)2] = m[Mg(OH)2] / M[Mg(OH)2]

n[NaOH] = (2/1) n[Mg(OH)2]

m[NaOH] = n[NaOH] * M[NaOH]
<h3>Example 4.11: Relating Masses of Reactants</h3>

What mass of oxygen gas, O2, from the air is consumed in the combustion of 702 g of octane, C8H18, one of the principal components of gasoline?

!2C8H18 +25O2 -> 16CO2 +18H2O

<h4>Solution</h4>

m[C8H18] = 702. g

The approach required here is the same as for the <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/4-3-reaction-stoichiometry/#fs-idp113495744">Example 9</a>, differing only in that the provided and requested masses are both for reactant species.

@ n[O2] / n[C8H18] = (25/2)

n[C8H18] = m[C8H18] / M[C8H18]

n[O2] = (25/2) n[C8H18]

m[O2] = n[O2] * M[O2]

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_04_03_map3_img.jpg", width="40%"}
<h3>Example 4.12: Identifying the Limiting Reactant</h3>

<h4>Identifying the Limiting Reactant</h4>

Silicon nitride is a very hard, high-temperature-resistant ceramic used as a component of turbine blades in jet engines. It is prepared according to the following equation:

!3Si(s) +2N2(g) -> Si3N4(s)

Which is the limiting reactant when 2.00 g of Si and 1.50 g of N2 react?

<h4>Solution</h4>

Compute the provided chemical amounts of reactants, and then compare these amounts to the balanced equation to identify the limiting reactant.

m[Si] = 2.00 g

m[N2] = 1.50 g

n[Si] = m[Si] / M[Si]

n[N2] = m[N2] / M[N2]

n[->] = min(n[Si] / 3, n[N2] / 2)

Comparing the ratios of chemical amount to stoichiometric coefficient shows that Si is limiting (smaller number, runs out while N2 is left over).

To check our results, we calculate how much is left over.

n[Si]leftover = n[Si] - n[->] * 3

n[N2]leftover = n[N2] - n[->] * 2

These chemical amounts should either be zero (for the limiting reactant) or larger than zero (for the reactants in excess). If we obtain any negative chemical amounts, we know there was a mistake in the setup or in the calculation.
<h3>Example 4.13: Calculation of Percent Yield</h3>

<h4>Calculation of Percent Yield</h4>

Upon reaction of 1.274 g of copper sulfate with excess zinc metal, 0.392 g copper metal was obtained according to the equation:

!CuSO4(aq) +Zn(s) -> Cu(s) +ZnSO4(aq)

What is the relative yield expressed in percent?

<h4>Solution</h4>

m[CuSO4] = 1.274 g

m[Cu(s)] = 0.392 g

@ n[Cu(s)]theo / n[CuSO4] = (1/1)

The provided information identifies copper sulfate as the limiting reactant, and so the theoretical yield is found by the approach illustrated in the previous module, as shown here:

n[CuSO4] = m[CuSO4] /  M[CuSO4]

n[Cu(s)]actual = m[Cu(s)] / M[Cu]

n[Cu(s)]theo = (1/1) n[CuSO4]

Using this theoretical yield and the provided value for actual yield, the percent yield is calculated to be

yield_rel = n[Cu(s)]actual / n[Cu(s)]theo * 100 ％


<h3>Example 4.14: Titration Analysis</h3>

<h4>Titration Analysis</h4>

The end point in a titration of a 50.00 mL sample of aqueous HCl was reached by addition of 35.23 mL of 0.250 M NaOH titrant. The titration reaction is:

!HCl(aq) +NaOH(aq) -> NaCl(aq) +H2O(l)

What is the concentration of the HCl?

<h4>Solution</h4>

V[HCl] = 50.00 mL

c[HCl] = ?

c[NaOH] = 0.250 M

V[NaOH] = 35.23 mL

At the end point of the titration, the reactants are present at stochiometric ratio

@n[HCl] / n[NaOH] = 1/1

As for all reaction stoichiometry calculations, the key issue is the relation between the chemical amounts of the chemical species of interest as depicted in the balanced chemical equation. The approach outlined in previous modules of this chapter is followed, with additional considerations required, since the amounts of reactants provided and requested are expressed as solution concentrations.

For this exercise, the calculation will follow the following outlined steps:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_04_05_map7_img.jpg", width="50%"}

n[NaOH] = c[NaOH] V[NaOH]

n[HCl] = n[NaOH] * (1/1)

c[HCl] = n[HCl] / V[HCl]
<h3>Example 4.15: Gravimetric Analysis</h3>

<h4>Gravimetric Analysis</h4>

A 0.4550 g solid mixture containing MgSO4 is dissolved in water and treated with an excess of Ba(NO3)2, resulting in the precipitation of 0.6168 g of BaSO4.

!MgSO4(aq) +Ba(NO3)2(aq) -> BaSO4(s) +Mg(NO3)2(aq)

What is the mass ratio (percent) of MgSO4 in the mixture?

<h4>Solution</h4>

m_mix = 0.4550 g

m[BaSO4] = 0.6168 g

The plan for this calculation is similar to others used in stoichiometric calculations, the central step being the connection between the moles of BaSO4 and MgSO4 through their stoichiometric factor. Once the mass of MgSO4 is computed, it may be used along with the mass of the sample mixture to calculate the requested percentage concentration.

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_04_05_map8_img.jpg", width="40%"}

n[BaSO4] = m[BaSO4] / M[BaSO4]

@ n[BaSO4] / n[MgSO4] = 1/1

n[MgSO4] = n[BaSO4]

The mass of MgSO4 that would yield the provided precipitate mass is

m[MgSO4] = n[MgSO4] * M[MgSO4]

The concentration of MgSO4 in the sample mixture is then calculated to be

ratio_mass[MgSO4] = m[MgSO4] / m_mix * 100 ％


<h3>Example 4.16: Combustion Analysis</h3>

<h4>Combustion Analysis</h4>

Polyethylene is a hydrocarbon polymer used to produce food-storage bags and many other flexible plastic items. A combustion analysis of a 0.00126 g sample of polyethylene yields 0.00394 g of CO2 and 0.00161 g of H2O. What is the empirical formula of polyethylene?

<h4>Solution</h4>

m_PE = 0.00126 g

m[CO2] = 0.00394 g

m[H2O] = 0.00161 g

The primary assumption in this exercise is that all the carbon in the sample combusted is converted to carbon dioxide, and all the hydrogen in the sample is converted to water:

!CxHy(s) +excess O2(g) -> xCO2(g) + y/2 H2O(g)

Note that a balanced equation is not necessary for the task at hand. To derive the empirical formula of the compound, only the subscripts x and y are needed.

First, calculate the chemical amounts of carbon and hydrogen in the sample, using the provided masses of the carbon dioxide and water, respectively. With these chemical amounts, the empirical formula for the compound may be written as described in the previous chapter of this text. An outline of this approach is given in the following flow chart:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_04_05_combmap_img.jpg"}

n[CO2] = m[CO2] / M[CO2]

n[H2O] = m[H2O] / M[H2O]

n[C]inPE = n[CO2]

n[H]inPE = n[H2O] * 2

The empirical formula for the compound is then derived by identifying the smallest whole-number multiples for these molar amounts. The H-to-C molar ratio is

ratio_HtoC = n[H]inPE / n[C]inPE

and the empirical formula for polyethylene is CH2.
<h3>Example 3.8: Deriving the Number of Atoms and Molecules from the Mass of a Compound</h3>

A packet of an artificial sweetener contains 40.0 mg of saccharin (C7H5NO3S), which has the structural formula:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_03_02_saccharin_img.jpg"}

Given that saccharin has a molar mass of 183.18 g/mol, how many saccharin molecules are in a 40.0-mg (0.0400-g) sample of saccharin? How many carbon atoms are in the same sample?

<h4>Solution</h4>

m_saccharin = 0.0400 g

M_saccharin = 183.18 g/mol

The number of molecules in a given mass of compound is computed by first deriving the number of moles, as demonstrated in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/3-1-formula-mass-and-the-mole-concept/#fs-idm1714048">Example 5</a>, and then multiplying by Avogadro’s constant:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_03_02_sacch_img.jpg"}

@ N_ = n * N_A

N_A = 6.022e23 /mol

Using the provided mass and molar mass for saccharin yields:

n_sacharin = m_saccharin / M_saccharin

N_saccharin = n_sacharin * N_A

The compound’s formula shows that each molecule contains seven carbon atoms, and so the number of C atoms in the provided sample is:

N_carbon_atoms = 7 N_saccharin
<h3>Example 3.9: Calculation of Percent Composition</h3>

Analysis of a 12.04-g sample of a liquid compound composed of carbon, hydrogen, and nitrogen showed it to contain 7.34 g C, 1.85 g H, and 2.85 g N. What is the percent composition of this compound?

<h4>Solution</h4>

m_liquid = 12.04 g

m_C = 7.34 g

m_H = 1.85 g

m_N = 2.85 g

We divide the experimentally derived mass of each element by the overall mass of the compound to get the fraction of each element - by mass - in the compound.

f_C = m_C / m_liquid * 100 ％

f_H = m_H / m_liquid * 100 ％

f_N = m_N / m_liquid * 100 ％

The analysis results indicate that the compound is 61.0% C, 15.4% H, and 23.7% N by mass.
<h3>Example 3.10: Determining Percent Composition from a Molecular Formula</h3>

Aspirin is a compound with the molecular formula C9H8O4. What is its percent composition?

<h4>Solution</h4>

To calculate the percent composition, we need to know the masses of C, H, and O in a known mass of C9H8O4. It is convenient to consider 1 mol of C9H8O4 and use its molar mass (180.159 g/mole, determined from the chemical formula) to calculate the percentages of each of its elements:

n[C9H8O4] = 1 mol

The chemical amounts of the atoms making up the molecule follow from the chemical formula

n[C] = 9 n[C9H8O4]

n[H] = 8 n[C9H8O4]

n[O] = 4 n[C9H8O4]

Now you can calculate all the masses

m[C9H8O4] = n[C9H8O4] M[C9H8O4]

m[C] = n[C] M[C]

m[H] = n[H] M[H]

m[O] = n[O] M[O]  

To calculate the fraction of a given element in the compound - by mass - we divide by the mass of the compound

f_C = m[C] /m[C9H8O4]  * 100 ％

f_H = m[H] /m[C9H8O4] * 100 ％ 

f_O = m[O] /m[C9H8O4] * 100 ％

The percent composition is 60.002% carbon, 4.476% hydrogen and 35.522%. Note that these percentages sum to equal 100.00% when appropriately rounded.

<hr>It is also possible to skip calculating masses and simply divide the molar mass of the element by the molar mass of the compound and multiply with the number of times the atom occurs in the compound. For carbon, for example, we would calculate:

f[C] = M[C] / M[C9H8O4] * 9
<h3>Example 7.1: Determining the Electronic Structures of Cations</h3>

There are at least 14 elements categorized as “essential trace elements” for the human body. They are called “essential” because they are required for healthy bodily functions, “trace” because they are required only in small amounts, and “elements” in spite of the fact that they are really ions. Two of these essential trace elements, chromium and zinc, are required as Cr^3+ and Zn^2+. Write the electron configurations of these cations.

<h4>Solution</h4>

First, write the electron configuration for the neutral atoms:

!Zn: [Ar] 3d^10 4s^2

!Cr: [Ar] 3d^5 4s^1

Next, remove electrons from the highest energy orbital. For the transition metals, electrons are removed from the s orbital first and then from the d orbital. For the p-block elements, electrons are removed from the p orbitals and then from the s orbital. Zinc is a member of group 12, so it should have a charge of 2+, and thus loses only the two electrons in its s orbital. Chromium is a transition element and should lose its s electrons and then its d electrons when forming a cation. Thus, we find the following electron configurations of the ions:

!Zn^2+: [Ar] 3d^10

!Cr^3+: [Ar] 3d^3

<h4>Solution</h4>

The formula for this compound indicates it contains Al^3+ and SO4^2- ions combined in a 2:3 ratio. For purposes of computing a formula mass, it is helpful to rewrite the formula in the simpler format, Al2S3O12. Following the approach outlined above, the formula mass for this compound is calculated as follows:

Selenium and iodine are two essential trace elements that form anions. Write the electron configurations of the anions.

<h4>Solution</h4>

! Se^2–: [Ar] 3d^10 4s^2 4p^6

! I^–: [Kr] 4d^10 5s^2 5p^6
<h3>Example 7.3: Electronegativity and Bond Polarity</h3>

Bond polarities play an important role in determining the structure of proteins. Using the electronegativity values in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_02_ENTable.jpg">Figure 6</a>, arrange the following covalent bonds — all commonly found in amino acids — in order of increasing polarity. Then designate the positive and negative atoms using the symbols δ+ and δ–:

C–H, C–N, C–O, N–H, O–H, S–H

<h4>Solution</h4>

The polarity of these bonds increases as the absolute value of the electronegativity difference increases. The atom with the δ– designation is the more electronegative of the two. <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/7-2-covalent-bonding/#fs-idm2614240">Table 1</a> shows these bonds in order of increasing polarity.

ΔEN[C-H] = 0.4

! C^{δ-}-H^{δ+}

<HR>

ΔEN[S–H] = 0.4

! S^{δ-}-H^{δ+}

<HR>

ΔEN[C–N] = 0.5

! C^{δ+}-N^{δ-}

<HR>

ΔEN[N–H] = 0.9

! N^{δ-}-H^{δ+}

<HR>

ΔEN[C–O] = 1.0

! C^{δ+}-O^{δ-}

<HR>

ΔEN[O–H] = 1.4

! O^{δ-}-H^{δ+}
<h3>Example 7.4: Writing Lewis Structures</h3>

NASA’s Cassini-Huygens mission detected a large cloud of toxic hydrogen cyanide (HCN) on Titan, one of Saturn’s moons. Titan also contains ethane (H3CCH3), acetylene (HCCH), and ammonia (NH3). What are the Lewis structures of these molecules?

<h4>Solution</h4>

Calculate the number of valence electrons.

VE[HCN] =  (1 * 1) + (1 * 4) + (1 * 5)

VE[H3CCH3] = (3 * 1) + (2 * 4) + (3 * 1)

VE[HCCH] = (1 * 1) + (2 * 4) + (1 * 1)

VE[NH3] = (1 * 5) + (1 * 3)

Draw a skeleton and connect the atoms with single bonds. Remember that H is never a central atom:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_03_ex070301_1_img.jpg", width="50%"}

Where needed, distribute electrons to the terminal atoms (don't use more electrons than available, though):

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_03_ex070301_2_img.jpg", width="50%"}

HCN: six electrons placed on N

H3CCH3: no electrons remain and no terminal atoms capable of accepting electrons

HCCH: no terminal atoms capable of accepting electrons

NH3: no terminal atoms capable of accepting electrons

<br>Where needed, place remaining electrons on the central atom:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_03_ex070301_3_img.jpg", width="50%"}

HCN: no electrons remain

H3CCH3: no electrons remain

HCCH: four remaining electrons placed on carbon

NH3: two remaining electrons placed on nitrogen

<br>Where needed, rearrange electrons to form multiple bonds in order to obtain an octet on each atom:

HCN: form two more C–N bonds

H3CCH3: all atoms have the correct number of electrons

HCCH: form a triple bond between the two carbon atoms

NH3: all atoms have the correct number of electrons

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_03_ex070301_4_img.jpg", width="50%"}
<h3>Example 7.5: Writing Lewis Structures: Octet Rule Violations</h3>

Xenon is a noble gas, but it forms a number of stable compounds. We examined XeF4 earlier. What are the Lewis structures of XeF2 and XeF6?

<h4>Solution</h4>

We can draw the Lewis structure of any covalent molecule by following the six steps discussed earlier. In this case, we can condense the last few steps, since not all of them apply.

Calculate the number of valence electrons:

VE[XeF2] = 8 + (2 * 7)

VE[XeF6] = 8 + (6 * 7)

Draw a skeleton joining the atoms by single bonds. Xenon will be the central atom because fluorine cannot be a central atom:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_03_Xeskeleton_img.jpg", width="40%"}

Distribute the remaining electrons.

<br>

XeF2: We place three lone pairs of electrons around each F atom, accounting for 12 electrons and giving each F atom 8 electrons. Thus, six electrons (three lone pairs) remain. These lone pairs must be placed on the Xe atom. This is acceptable because Xe atoms have empty valence shell d orbitals and can accommodate more than eight electrons. The Lewis structure of XeF2 shows two bonding pairs and three lone pairs of electrons around the Xe atom:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_03_XeF2_img.jpg", width="20%"}

XeF6: We place three lone pairs of electrons around each F atom, accounting for 36 electrons. Two electrons remain, and this lone pair is placed on the Xe atom:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_03_XeF6_img.jpg", width="40%"}
<h3>Example 7.6: Calculating Formal Charge from Lewis Structures</h3>

Assign formal charges to each atom in the interhalogen ion ICl4-.

<h4>Solution</h4>

We divide the bonding electron pairs equally for all I–Cl bonds:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_04_ICL4_img.jpg"}

We assign lone pairs of electrons to their atoms. Each Cl atom now has seven electrons assigned to it, and the I atom has eight.

Subtract this number from the number of valence electrons for the neutral atom:

FC[I] =  7 - 8

FC[Cl] = 7 - 7

The sum of the formal charges of all the atoms equals -1, which is identical to the charge of the ion (-1).
<h3>Example 7.7: Calculating Formal Charge from Lewis Structures</h3>

Assign formal charges to each atom in the interhalogen molecule BrCl3.

<h4>Solution</h4>

Assign one of the electrons in each Br–Cl bond to the Br atom and one to the Cl atom in that bond:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_04_BRCL3_img.jpg"}

Assign the lone pairs to their atom. Now each Cl atom has seven electrons and the Br atom has seven electrons.

Subtract this number from the number of valence electrons for the neutral atom. This gives the formal charge:

FC[Br] = 7 - 7

FC[Cl] = 7 - 7

All atoms in BrCl3 have a formal charge of zero, and the sum of the formal charges totals zero, as it must in a neutral molecule.
<h3>Example 7.8: Using Formal Charge to Determine Molecular Structure</h3>

Nitrous oxide, N2O, commonly known as laughing gas, is used as an anesthetic in minor surgeries, such as the routine extraction of wisdom teeth. Which is the likely structure for nitrous oxide?

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_04_N2O_img.jpg"}

<h4>Solution</h4>

Determining formal charge yields the following:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_04_N2Ofc_img.jpg"}

The structure with a terminal oxygen atom best satisfies the criteria for the most stable distribution of formal charge:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_04_NNO_img.jpg"}

The number of atoms with formal charges are minimized (Guideline 2), and there is no formal charge larger than one (Guideline 2). This is again consistent with the preference for having the less electronegative atom in the central position.
<h3>Example 7.9: Using Bond Energies to Calculate Approximate Enthalpy Changes</h3>

Methanol, CH3OH, may be an excellent alternative fuel. The high-temperature reaction of steam and carbon produces a mixture of the gases carbon monoxide, CO, and hydrogen, H2, from which methanol can be produced. Using the bond energies in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/7-5-strengths-of-ionic-and-covalent-bonds/#fs-idm44464336">Table 4</a>, calculate the approximate enthalpy change, ΔH, for the reaction here:

!CO(g) +2H2(g) -> CH3OH(g)

<h4>Solution</h4>First, we need to write the Lewis structures of the reactants and the products:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_05_CH3OHLew_img.jpg"}

From this, we see that ΔH for this reaction involves the energy required to break a C–O triple bond and two H–H single bonds, as well as the energy produced by the formation of three C–H single bonds, a C–O single bond, and an O–H single bond. We can express this as follows:

@ ΔH = sum(D_bonds_broken) - sum(D_bonds_formed)

@ ΔH = sum(D[C#O], 2 D[H-H]) - sum(3 D[C-H], D[C-O], D[O-H])

Using the bond energy values in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/7-5-strengths-of-ionic-and-covalent-bonds/#fs-idm44464336">Table 4</a>, we obtain:

D[C#O] = 1080 kJ/mol

D[H-H] = 436 kJ/mol

D[C-H] = 415 kJ/mol

D[C-O] = 350 kJ/mol

D[O-H] = 464 kJ/mol

ΔH_estimate = sum(D[C#O], 2 D[H-H]) - sum(3 D[C-H], D[C-O], D[O-H])

We can compare this value to the value calculated based on ΔHf° data from Appendix G:

ΔHf°[CH3OH(g)] = -201.0 kJ/mol

ΔHf°[CO(g)] = -110.52 kJ/mol

ΔHf°[H2] = 0

ΔH_measurement = ΔHf°[CH3OH(g)] - ΔHf°[CO(g)] - 2 ΔHf°[H2]

Note that there is a fairly significant gap between the values calculated using the two different methods. This occurs because D values are the average of different bond strengths; therefore, they often give only rough agreement with other data.
<h3>Example 7.10: Lattice Energy Comparisons</h3>

The precious gem ruby is aluminum oxide, Al2O3, containing traces of Cr^3+. The compound Al2Se3 is used in the fabrication of some semiconductor devices. Which has the larger lattice energy, Al2O3 or Al2Se3?

<h4>Solution</h4>

In these two ionic compounds, the charges Z<sup>+</sup> and Z<sup>-</sup> are the same, so the difference in lattice energy will depend upon R0. The O^2– ion is smaller than the Se^2– ion. Thus, Al2O3 would have a shorter interionic distance than Al2Se3, and Al2O3 would have the larger lattice energy.
<h3>Example 7.11: Predicting Electron-pair Geometry and Molecular Structure: CO2 and BCl3</h3>

Predict the electron-pair geometry and molecular structure for each of the following:

(a) carbon dioxide, CO2, a molecule produced by the combustion of fossil fuels

(b) boron trichloride, BCl3, an important industrial chemical

<h4>Solution</h4>

(a) We write the Lewis structure of CO2 as:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_CO2_img.jpg"}

This shows us two regions of high electron density around the carbon atom—each double bond counts as one region, and there are no lone pairs on the carbon atom. Using VSEPR theory, we predict that the two regions of electron density arrange themselves on opposite sides of the central atom with a bond angle of 180°. The electron-pair geometry and molecular structure are identical, and CO2 molecules are linear.

<br>

(b) We write the Lewis structure of BCl3 as:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_BCl3_img.jpg"}

Thus we see that BCl3 contains three bonds, and there are no lone pairs of electrons on boron. The arrangement of three regions of high electron density gives a trigonal planar electron-pair geometry. The B–Cl bonds lie in a plane with 120° angles between them. BCl3 also has a trigonal planar molecular structure (<a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_BCl3mol.jpg">Figure 21</a>).

<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_BCl3mol.jpg">

The electron-pair geometry and molecular structure of BCl3 are both trigonal planar. Note that the VSEPR geometry indicates the correct bond angles (120°), unlike the Lewis structure shown above.
<h3>Example 7.12: Predicting Electron-pair Geometry and Molecular Structure: Ammonium</h3>

Two of the top 50 chemicals produced in the United States, ammonium nitrate and ammonium sulfate, both used as fertilizers, contain the ammonium ion. Predict the electron-pair geometry and molecular structure of the NH4+ cation.

<h4>Solution</h4>

We write the Lewis structure of NH4+ as:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_NH4_img.jpg"}

We can see that NH4+ contains four bonds from the nitrogen atom to hydrogen atoms and no lone pairs. We expect the four regions of high electron density to arrange themselves so that they point to the corners of a tetrahedron with the central nitrogen atom in the middle (<a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_molgeom.jpg">Figure 19</a>). Therefore, the electron pair geometry of NH4+ is tetrahedral, and the molecular structure is also tetrahedral (<a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_NH4mol.jpg">Figure 22</a>).

<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_NH4mol.jpg">
<h3>Example 7.13: Predicting Electron-pair Geometry and Molecular Structure: Lone Pairs on the Central Atom</h3>

Predict the electron-pair geometry and molecular structure of a water molecule.

<h4>Solution</h4>

The Lewis structure of H2O indicates that there are four regions of high electron density around the oxygen atom: two lone pairs and two chemical bonds:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_H2O_img.jpg"}

We predict that these four regions are arranged in a tetrahedral fashion (<a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_H2Omol.jpg">Figure 23</a>), as indicated in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_molgeom.jpg">Figure 19</a>. Thus, the electron-pair geometry is tetrahedral and the molecular structure is bent with an angle slightly less than 109.5°. In fact, the bond angle is 104.5°.

<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_H2Omol.jpg", width="30%">
<h3>Example 7.14: Predicting Electron-pair Geometry and Molecular Structure: SF4</h3>

Sulfur tetrafluoride, SF4, is extremely valuable for the preparation of fluorine-containing compounds used as herbicides (i.e., SF4 is used as a fluorinating agent). Predict the electron-pair geometry and molecular structure of a SF4 molecule.

<h4>Solution</h4>

The Lewis structure of SF4 indicates five regions of electron density around the sulfur atom: one lone pair and four bonding pairs:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_SF4_img.jpg"}

We expect these five regions to adopt a trigonal bipyramidal electron-pair geometry. To minimize lone pair repulsions, the lone pair occupies one of the equatorial positions. The molecular structure is that of a seesaw (<a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_molgeom.jpg">Figure 19</a>).

<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_SF4mol.jpg">Figure 24(a): SF4 has a trigonal bipyramidal arrangement of the five regions of electron density. (b) One of the regions is a lone pair, which results in a seesaw-shaped molecular structure.
<h3>Example 7.15: Predicting Electron-pair Geometry and Molecular Structure: XeF4</h3>

Of all the noble gases, xenon is the most reactive, frequently reacting with elements such as oxygen and fluorine. Predict the electron-pair geometry and molecular structure of the XeF4 molecule.

<h4>Solution</h4>

The Lewis structure of XeF4 indicates six regions of high electron density around the xenon atom: two lone pairs and four bonds:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_XeF4_img.jpg"}

These six regions adopt an octahedral arrangement (panel A below), which is the electron-pair geometry. To minimize repulsions, the lone pairs should be on opposite sides of the central atom. The five atoms are all in the same plane and have a square planar molecular structure (panel B below).

<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_XEF4mol.jpg", width="30%">
<h3>Example 7.16: Predicting Structure in Multicenter Molecules</h3>

The Lewis structure for the simplest amino acid, glycine, H2NCH2CO2H, is shown here. Predict the local geometry for the nitrogen atom, the two carbon atoms, and the oxygen atom with a hydrogen atom attached:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_gly_img.jpg"}

<h4>Solution</h4>

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_07_06_gly3D_img.jpg"}

Consider each central atom independently. The electron-pair geometries:

nitrogen––four regions of electron density; tetrahedral

carbon (CH2)––four regions of electron density; tetrahedral

carbon (CO2)—three regions of electron density; trigonal planar

oxygen (OH)—four regions of electron density; tetrahedral

<br>

The local structures:

nitrogen––three bonds, one lone pair; trigonal pyramidal

carbon (CH2)—four bonds, no lone pairs; tetrahedral

carbon (CO2)—three bonds (double bond counts as one bond), no lone pairs; trigonal planar

oxygen (OH)—two bonds, two lone pairs; bent (109°)

<h3>Example 3.8: Deriving the Number of Atoms and Molecules from the Mass of a Compound</h3>

A packet of an artificial sweetener contains 40.0 mg of saccharin (C7H5NO3S), which has the structural formula:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_03_02_saccharin_img.jpg", width="20%"}

Given that saccharin has a molar mass of 183.18 g/mol, how many saccharin molecules are in a 40.0-mg (0.0400-g) sample of saccharin? How many carbon atoms are in the same sample?

<h4>Solution</h4>

m_packet = 40.0 mg

M[C7H5NO3S] = 183.18 g/mol

N_molecules = ?

N_C_atoms = ?

The number of molecules in a given mass of compound is computed by first deriving the number of moles, as demonstrated in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/3-1-formula-mass-and-the-mole-concept/#fs-idm1714048">Example 5</a>, and then multiplying by Avogadro’s constant:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_03_02_sacch_img.jpg", width="50%"}

@N_molecules = m_sample / M_compound * N_A

N_A = 6.022e23 /mol

Using the provided mass and molar mass for saccharin yields:

N_molecules = m_packet / M[C7H5NNO3S] * N_A

The compound’s formula shows that each molecule contains seven carbon atoms, and so the number of C atoms in the provided sample is:

N_C_atoms = 7 N_molecules
<h3>Example 3.9: Calculation of Percent Composition</h3>

Analysis of a 12.04-g sample of a liquid compound composed of carbon, hydrogen, and nitrogen showed it to contain 7.34 g C, 1.85 g H, and 2.85 g N. What is the percent composition of this compound?

<h4>Solution</h4>

m_sample = 12.04 g

m_C = 7.34 g

m_H = 1.85 g

m_N = 2.85 g

We are looking for the fraction by mass of these elements in the compound.

f_C = ?

f_H = ?

f_N = ?

To calculate percent composition, we divide the experimentally derived mass of each element by the overall mass of the compound, and then convert to a percentage:
f_C = m_C /m_sample  * 100 ％

f_H = m_H /m_sample  * 100 ％

f_N = m_N /m_sample * 100 ％
The analysis results indicate that the compound is 61.0% C, 15.4% H, and 23.7% N by mass.
<h3>Example 3.11: Determining a Compound’s Empirical Formula from the Masses of Its Elements</h3>

A sample of the black mineral hematite (<a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_03_03_hematite.jpg">Figure 12</a>), an oxide of iron found in many iron ores, contains 34.97 g of iron and 15.03 g of oxygen. What is the empirical formula of hematite?

<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_03_03_hematite.jpg", width = "20%">Figure 12: Hematite is an iron oxide that is used in jewelry. (credit: Mauro Cateb)

<h4>Solution</h4>

For this problem, we are given the mass in grams of each element.

m[Fe] = 34.97 g

m[O] = 15.03 g

ratio_molar = ?

Begin by finding the chemical amount of each:
n[Fe] =m[Fe] /M[Fe] 

n[O] =m[O] /M[O] 

There is less iron than oxygen, so calculate the oxygen-to-iron molar ratio:

ratio[O:Fe] = n[O] / n[Fe]

The ratio is of iron to oxygen is 1 : 1.500, so the formula is

! FeO_{1.5}

Finally, multiply all coefficients by two to get the smallest possible whole number subscripts while still maintaining the correct iron-to-oxygen ratio:

! Fe2O3! empirical formula

The empirical formula is Fe2O3.
<h3>Example 3.12: Determining an Empirical Formula from Percent Composition</h3>

The bacterial fermentation of grain to produce ethanol forms a gas with a percent composition of 27.29% C and 72.71% O (Figure 3). What is the empirical formula for this gas?<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_03_03_BrewTank.jpg", width="30%">Figure 13: An oxide of carbon is removed from these fermentation tanks through the large copper pipes at the top. (credit: “Dual Freq”/Wikimedia Commons)

<h4>Solution</h4>

f_C = 27.29 ％

f_O = 72.71 ％

ratio_molar = ?

Since the scale for percentages is 100, it is most convenient to calculate the mass of elements present in a sample weighing 100 g. (The calculation is “most convenient” because if a compound contains 27.29% carbon by mass, a 100-g sample will contain 27.29 g of carbon, and so on for the other elements. This makes it easier to follow the calculation in your head, but you would get the same answer for any mass you decide to start with because in the end, this mass cancels out.)

m_total = 100 g

m_C = f_C * m_total

m_O = f_O * m_total

To get the molar ratio, we have to calculate the chemical amounts of carbon and oxygen present.
n_C =m_C /M[C]

n_O =m_O /M[O]
Coefficients for the tentative empirical formula are derived by dividing each molar amount by the lesser of the two:

coeff_C = n_C / min(n_C ,n_O )

coeff_O = n_O / min(n_C ,n_O )

Since the resulting ratio is one carbon to two oxygen atoms, the empirical formula is CO2.
<h3>Example 3.13: Determination of the Molecular Formula for Nicotine</h3>

Nicotine, an alkaloid in the nightshade family of plants that is mainly responsible for the addictive nature of cigarettes, contains 74.02% C, 8.710% H, and 17.27% N. If 40.57 g of nicotine contains 0.2500 mol nicotine, what is the molecular formula?

<h4>Solution</h4>

f_C = 74.02 ％

f_H = 8.710  ％

f_N = 17.27  ％

m_sample = 40.57 g

n_sample = 0.2500 mol

Determining the molecular formula from the provided data will require comparison of the compound’s empirical formula mass to its molar mass. As the first step, use the percent composition to derive the compound’s empirical formula.

n_C = m_sample * f_C / M[C]

n_H = m_sample * f_H / M[H]

n_N = m_sample * f_N / M[N]

Now, divide by the chemical amount of nicotine present in the sample to obtain the coefficients of the molecular formula.

coeff_C = n_C / n_sample 

coeff_H = n_H / n_sample 

coeff_N = n_N / n_sample

The molecular formula is C10H14N2. As a check, we can calculate the molar mass of nicotine from the experimental data and compare it to that obtained from the molecular formula

M_nicotine = m_sample / n_sample

M[C10H14N2] = sum(10 M[C], 14 M[H], 2 M[N])
<h3>Example 3.14: Calculating Molar Concentrations</h3>

A 355-mL soft drink sample contains 0.133 mol of sucrose (table sugar). What is the concentration of sucrose in the beverage?

<h4>Solution</h4>

V_solution = 355. mL

n_sucrose = 0.133 mol

Since the chemical amount of solute and the volume of solution are both given, the concentration can be calculated using its definition. 

c_sucrose = n_sucrose / V_solution

If we want to switch to the more common units mol/L, or short M:

__tutor__ = 1

c_sucrose using M



<h3>Example 3.15: Deriving Moles and Volumes from Molar Concentrations</h3>

How much sugar (chemical amount) is contained in a modest sip (~10 mL) of the soft drink from <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/3-3-molarity/#fs-idm98982768">Example 13</a>?

<h4>Solution</h4>

V_sip = 10 mL

n_sugar = ?

We derived derived the concentration of sugar, 0.375 M, in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/3-3-molarity/#fs-idm98982768">Example 13</a>, 0.375 M:

c_sugar = 0.375 M

In this case, we can rearrange the definition of concentration to isolate the quantity sought, n_sugar, the chemical amount of sugar. 

@ c_sugar = n_sugar / V_sip

n_sugar = c_sugar * V_sip

Or expressed in millimol:

n_sugar using mmol



<h3>Example 3.16: Calculating Molar Concentrations from the Mass of Solute</h3>

Distilled white vinegar (<a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_03_04_vinegar.jpg">Figure 15</a>) is a solution of acetic acid, CH3CO2H, in water. A 0.500-L vinegar solution contains 25.2 g of acetic acid. What is the concentration of the acetic acid solution in units of mol/L or M?

<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_03_04_vinegar.jpg", width = "40%">Figure 15: Distilled white vinegar is a solution of acetic acid in water.

<h4>Solution</h4>

V_solution = 0.500 L

m[CH3CO2H] = 25.2 g

c[CH3CO2H] = ?

As in previous examples, the definition of concentration is the primary equation used to calculate the quantity sought. In this case, the mass of solute is provided instead of its molar amount, so we must use the solute’s molar mass to obtain the chemical amount of solute:

n[CH3CO2H] = m[CH3CO2H] /M[CH3CO2H]

c[CH3CO2H] = n[CH3CO2H] / V_solution 

c[CH3CO2H] using M



<h3>Example 3.17: Determining the Mass of Solute in a Given Volume of Solution</h3>

How many grams of NaCl are contained in 0.250 L of a 5.30-M solution?

<h4>Solution</h4>

m_NaCl = ?

c_NaCl = 5.30 M

V_solution = 0.250 L

The volume and concentration of the solution are specified, so the chemical amount (mol) of solute is easily computed as demonstrated in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/3-3-molarity/#fs-idm64107376">Example 14</a>:

n_NaCl = c_NaCl V_solution 

Finally, this chemical amount is used to derive the mass of NaCl:

m_NaCl = n_NaCl M[NaCl]



<h3>Example 3.18: Determining the Volume of Solution Containing a Given Mass of Solute</h3>

In <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/chapter/3-3-molarity/#fs-idm81897840">Example 15</a>, we found the typical concentration of vinegar to be 0.839 M. What volume of vinegar contains 75.6 g of acetic acid?

<h4>Solution</h4>

c_acetic_acid = 0.839 M

m_acetic_acid = 75.6 g

V_vinegar = ?

First, use the molar mass to calculate chemical amount of acetic acid (CH3CO2H) from the given mass:

n_acetic_acid = m_acetic_acid / M[CH3CO2H]

Then, use the concentration of the solution to calculate the volume of solution containing this chemical amount of solute:

@ c_acetic_acid = n_acetic_acid / V_vinegar

V_vinegar = n_acetic_acid / c_acetic_acid 

We could have combined these two steps into one to get the same answer:

@ V_vinegar = m_acetic_acid / (M[CH3CO2H] c_acetic_acid)



<h3>Example 3.19: Determining the Concentration of a Diluted Solution</h3>

If 0.850 L of a 5.00-M solution of copper nitrate, Cu(NO3)2, is diluted to a volume of 1.80 L by the addition of water, what is the concentration of the diluted solution?

<h4>Solution</h4>

We are given the volume and concentration of a stock solution, V1 and c1, and the volume of the resultant diluted solution, V2. We need to find the concentration of the diluted solution, c2. 

V1 = 0.850 L

c1 = 5.00 M

V2 = 1.80 L

c2 = ?

We thus rearrange the dilution equation in order to isolate c2:

@ c1 V1 = c2 V2

@ c2 = c1 V1 / V2

Since the stock solution is being diluted by more than two-fold (volume is increased from 0.85 L to 1.80 L), we would expect the diluted solution’s concentration to be less than one-half 5 M. We will compare this ballpark estimate to the calculated result to check for any gross errors in computation (for example, such as an improper substitution of the given quantities). Substituting the given values for the terms on the right side of this equation yields:

c2 = c1 (V1 / V2)

This result compares well to our ballpark estimate (it’s a bit less than one-half the stock concentration, 5 M).



<h3>Example 3.22: Calculation of Percent by Mass</h3>

A 5.0-g sample of spinal fluid contains 3.75 mg (0.00375 g) of glucose. What is the percent by mass of glucose in spinal fluid?

<h4>Solution</h4>

m_sample = 5.0 g

m_glucose = 3.75 mg

The spinal fluid sample contains roughly 4 mg of glucose in 5000 mg of fluid, so the mass fraction of glucose should be a bit less than one part in 1000, or about 0.1%. Substituting the given masses into the equation defining mass percentage yields:

f_glucose = m_glucose / m_sample * 100 ％

The computed mass percentage agrees with our rough estimate (it’s a bit less than 0.1%).

Note that while any mass unit may be used to compute a mass percentage (mg, g, kg, oz, and so on), the same unit must be used for both the solute and the solution before you can cancel the mass units, yielding a dimensionless ratio.



<h3>Example 3.23: Calculations using Mass Percentage</h3>

“Concentrated” hydrochloric acid is an aqueous solution of 37.2% HCl that is commonly used as a laboratory reagent. The density of this solution is 1.19 g/mL. What mass of HCl is contained in 0.500 L of this solution?

<h4>Solution</h4>

f_HCL_in_solution = 37.2 ％

ρ_solution = 1.19 g/mL

V_solution = 0.500 L

m_HCl = ?

The HCl concentration is near 40%, so a 100-g portion of this solution would contain about 40 g of HCl. Since the solution density isn’t greatly different from that of water (1 g/mL), a reasonable estimate of the HCl mass in 500 g (0.5 L) of the solution is about five times greater than that in a 100 g portion, or 5 times 40 g equals 200 g. 

In order to derive the mass of solute in a solution from its mass percentage, we need to know the corresponding mass of the solution. Using the solution density given, we can convert the solution’s volume to mass, and then use the given mass percentage to calculate the solute mass. This mathematical approach is outlined in this flowchart:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_03_05_Example2_img.jpg", width="50%"}

m_solution = ρ_solution V_solution 

m_HCl = f_HCL_in_solution m_solution 

This mass of HCl is consistent with our rough estimate of approximately 200 g.





<h3>Example 3.24: Calculations using Volume Percentage</h3>

Rubbing alcohol (isopropanol) is usually sold as a 70%vol aqueous solution. If the density of isopropyl alcohol is 0.785 g/mL, how many grams of isopropyl alcohol are present in a 355 mL bottle of rubbing alcohol?

<h4>Solution</h4>

f_by_vol = 70 ％

V_solution = 355. mL

ρ_iPrOH = 0.785 g/mL

m_iPrOH = ?

Per the definition of volume percentage, the isopropanol volume is 70% of the total solution volume. Multiplying the isopropanol volume by its density yields the requested mass:

m_iPrOH = f_by_vol V_solution ρ_iPrOH 



<h3>Example 3.25: Calculation of Parts per Million and Parts per Billion Concentrations</h3>

According to the EPA, when the fraction by mass of lead in tap water reaches 15 ppb, certain remedial actions must be taken. What is this "concentration" in ppm? At this level, what mass of lead (μg) would be contained in a typical glass of water (300 mL)?

<h4>Solution</h4>

ppm = (1/1000000)

ppb = (1/1000000000)

V_glass = 300 mL

f_lead = 15. ppb

f_lead in ppm

The definition of the ppb unit may be used to calculate the requested mass if the mass of the solution is provided. However, only the volume of solution (300 mL) is given, so we must use the density to derive the corresponding mass. We can assume the density of tap water to be roughly the same as that of pure water (~1.00 g/mL), since the concentrations of any dissolved substances should not be very large.

ρ_solution = 1.00 g/mL

m_solution = ρ_solution V_glass

m_lead = f_lead m_solution

Finally, convert this mass to the requested unit of micrograms:

m_lead using μg



<h3>Example 5.1b: Measuring Heat</h3>

How much heat, in joules, must be added to a 5.00 × 10^2-g iron skillet to increase its temperature from 25 °C to 250 °C? The specific heat of iron is 0.451 J/g °C.

<h4>Answer:</h4>


m_skillet = 5.00E2 g

T_initial = 25. °aC

T_final = 250. °aC

cp = 0.451 J/(g °ΔC)

q = m_skillet cp (T_final -T_initial )

Think about it:

a) q is positive because the energy of the skillet increased

a) q is negative because the energy of the skillet increased

a) q is positive because the energy of the skillet decreased

a) q is negative because the energy of the skillet decreased



<h3>Example 5.2b: Determining Other Quantities</h3>

A piece of unknown metal weighs 217 g. When the metal piece absorbs 1.43 kJ of heat, its temperature increases from 24.5 °C to 39.1 °C. Determine the specific heat of this metal, and predict its identity.

<h4>Answer:</h4>


m_metal = 217. g

q = 1.43 kJ

T_initial = 24.5 °aC

T_final = 39.1 °aC

c_p_metal = ?

c_p_metal = q / (m_metal (T_final -T_initial ))

c_p_metal using J g

Think about it: If a substance has a higher heat capacity...
a) you need less heat to change the temperature of that substance
b) you need more heat to change the temperature of that substance
c) you need less heat to melt that substance
d) you need more heat to melt that substance 


<h3>Example 5.3b: Heat Transfer between Substances at Different Temperatures</h3>

A 248-g piece of copper is dropped into 390 mL of water at 22.6 °C. The final temperature of the water was measured as 39.9 °C. Calculate the initial temperature of the piece of copper. Assume that all heat transfer occurs between the copper and the water.

<h4>Answer:</h4>


m[Cu] = 248. g

V_water = 390. mL

T_water_initial = 22.6 °aC

T_final = 39.9 °aC

T[Cu]initial = ?

c_p[Cu] = 0.385 J / (g °ΔC) # Table 1

c_p_water = 4.184 J / (g °ΔC)

@ q1 = m1 c_p_1 ΔT_1

@ q2 = m2 c_p_2 ΔT_2

@ q1 = -q2

m_water = V_water * 1 g/mL
ΔT_water = T_final - T_water_initial
q_water = m_water c_p_water ΔT_water
q[Cu] = -q_water

ΔT[Cu] = q[Cu] /(m[Cu] c_p[Cu]) 

T[Cu]initial = T_final - ΔT[Cu] 

Think about it: In which direction did the heat flow?
a) From copper to water because the copper got hotter and the water got colder
b) From copper to water because the copper got colder and the water got hotter
c) From water to copper because the copper got hotter and the water got colder
d) From water to copper because the copper got colder and the water got hotter
<h3>Example 13.1: Ion Concentrations in Pure Water</h3>

What are the hydronium ion concentration and the hydroxide ion concentration in pure water at 25 °C?

<h4>Solution</h4>

[H3O+] = ?

[OH-] = ?

The autoionization of water yields the same number of hydronium and hydroxide ions. Therefore, in pure water, [H3O+] = [OH-]. At 25 °C:

@ K_w = [H3O+][OH-] = 1.0e-14

Because in this case, [H3O+] = [OH-],

@ K_w = [H3O+]^2

So we can calculate [H3O+] from K_w for pure water (also works for any neutral aqueous solution):

K_w = 1.0e-14

[H3O+] = sqrt(K_w)

[OH-] = [H3O+]

The hydronium ion concentration and the hydroxide ion concentration are the same, and we find that both equal 1.0e-7 M.



<h3>Example 13.1b: Ion Concentrations in Pure Water</h3>

The ion product of water at 80 °C is 2.4 × 10^-13. What are the concentrations of hydronium and hydroxide ions in pure water at 80 °C?
<h4>Answer:</h4>

T = 80. °aC

K_w_80 = 2.4E-13

[H3O+] = ?

[OH-] = ?

@ K_w_80 = [H3O+] [OH-]

[H3O+] = sqrt(K_w_80)

[OH-] = [H3O+] 

Think about it: Compared to room temperature, at a higher temperature the hydronium ion concentration [H3O+] is

a) higher because water dissociates more

b) lower because water dissociates more

c) higher because water dissociates less

d) lower becuase water dissociates less



<h3>Example 13.2: The Inverse Proportionality of [H3O+] and [OH−]</h3>

A solution of carbon dioxide in water has a hydronium ion concentration of 2.0e-6 M. What is the concentration of hydroxide ion at 25 °C?

<h4>Solution</h4>

c[H3O+] = 2.0e-6 M

[H3O+] = c[H3O+] / 1 M # (in PQcalc, c_solute has units and [solute] does not)

[OH-] = ?

We know the value of the ion-product constant for water at 25 °C:

! 2H2O(l) <=> H3O+(aq) + OH-(aq)! K_w=[H3O+][OH-]

K_w =1.0e-14

Thus, we can calculate the missing equilibrium concentration.

Rearrangement of the K_w expression yields that [OH-] is directly proportional to the inverse of [H3O+]:

[OH-]=K_w/[H3O+]

c[OH-] = 1 M [OH-]

The hydroxide ion concentration in water is reduced to 5.0 × 10^-9 M as the hydrogen ion concentration increases to 2.0 × 10^-6 M. This is expected from Le Châtelier’s principle; the autoionization reaction shifts to the left to reduce the stress of the increased hydronium ion concentration and the [OH^-] is reduced relative to that in pure water.

A check of these concentrations confirms that our arithmetic is correct:

K_w_check =[H3O+][OH-]

Think about it: For pure water, [H3O+] = [OH-] = 1.0e-7. The concentration of hydroxide for the solution considered here is:

a) larger than 1.0e-7 M because [H3O+] is larger than 1.0e-7

b) larger than 1.0e-7 M because [H3O+] is smaller than 1.0e-7

c) smaller than 1.0e-7 M because [H3O+] is larger than 1.0e-7

d) smaller than 1.0e-7 M because [H3O+] is smaller than 1.0e-7

a) its charge becomes more negative and there is one hydrogen more
