<h3>Example 13.4b: Calculation of pH from [H3O+]</h3>

Water exposed to air contains carbonic acid, H2CO3, due to the reaction between carbon dioxide and water:

!CO2(aq) + H2O(l) <=> H2CO3(aq)

Air-saturated water has a hydronium ion concentration caused by the dissolved CO2 of 2.0e-6 M, about 20-times larger than that of pure water. Calculate the pH of the solution at 25 °C.

<h4>Answer:</h4>
5.7


c[H3O+] = 2.0E-6M

pH = ?

The pH is defined as pH = -log([H+]). Note that the argument of the logarithm (what you calculate the logarithm of) can't have units, and the value of the logarithm won't have units.

[H3O+] = c[H3O+] / 1 M

You can estimate that the pH will be around 6 because the order of magnitude of [H+] is 1.0e-6

pH = -log([H3O+])

Think about it: Is the pH acidic or basic (neutral would be pH = 7, so it is not neutral):

a) It is acidic because the pH is higher than 7

a) It is acidic because the pH is lower than 7

a) It is basic because the pH is higher than 7

a) It is basic because the pH is lower than 7





<h3>Example 13.4: Calculation of pH from [H3O+]</h3>

What is the pH of stomach acid, a solution of HCl with a hydronium ion concentration of 1.2e-3 M?

<h4>Solution</h4>

c[H3O+] = 1.2e-3 M

pH = ?

The pH is defined as 

@ pH = -log([H+]) = -log(c[H+]/1 M)

Before we can take the logaritm, we have to divide c[H3O+] by the standard concentration, 1M, to get the pure number [H3O+]

[H3O+] = c[H3O+] / 1M

pH=-log([H3O+])

(The use of logarithms is explained in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/back-matter/essential-mathematics/">Appendix B</a>. Recall that, as we have done here, when taking the log of a value, keep as many decimal places in the result as there are significant figures in the value.)



<h3>Example 13.5: Calculation of Hydronium Ion Concentration from pH</h3>

Calculate the hydronium ion concentration of blood, the pH of which is 7.3 (slightly alkaline).

<h4>Solution</h4>

pH = 7.3

c[H3O+] = ?

@ pH=-log([H+])

We need to discuss some math before we can proceed. If you know how to solve for [H+], you can skip ahead.

<hr> 

So how do you solve for [H+], which is locked away inside the logarithm function? You solve for the argument of a function by first rearranging so that only the function is on one side of the equation, and then applying the inverse function to both sides. So...

1) Isolate the function on the left hand side of the equation:

@ log([H+]) = -pH 

2) Apply the inverse function to both sides. The inverse function of the base-10 logarithm is raising 10 to the power of:

@ 10^log([H+]) = 10^(-pH)

3) Applying the inverse function to a function pops out the argument

@ [H+] = 10^(-pH)

So 

@[H+] = 10^(-pH) 

is equivalent to 

@log([H+]) = -pH

Depending on which direction your calculation goes, one or the other form is more useful. If the steps above are unfamiliar to you, it is nice to have both forms available for reference.

<hr> Now we are ready to continue with the problem

[H3O+] = 10^-pH

(On a pocket calculator take the antilog, or the “inverse” log, of -7.3, or calculate 10^-7.3.)

To get the concentration (with units), multiply by 1 M:

c[H3O+] = 1 M [H3O+]



<h3>Example 13.5b: Calculation of Hydronium Ion Concentration from pH</h3>

Calculate the hydronium ion concentration of a solution with a pH of -1.07.

<h4>Answer:</h4>


pH = -1.07 

c[H3O+] = ?

@ -pH = log([H+])

[H3O+] = 10^-pH 

c[H3O+] = 1M [H3O+] 

Think about it: Mostly, pH values are in the range of 0 to 14. Here, we have a negative value for the pH. What does that mean?

a) The solution is very acidic, and the concentration of H+ is negative.

b) The solution is very acidic, and the concentration of H+ is higher than 1 M.

c) The solution is very basic, and the concentration of H+ is negative.

d) The solution is very basic, and the concentration of H+ is higher than 1 M.



<h3>Example 13.6: Calculation of pOH</h3>

What are the pOH and the pH of a 0.0125-M solution of potassium hydroxide, KOH?

<h4>Solution</h4>

[KOH] = 0.0125

pH = ?

pOH = ?

Potassium hydroxide is a highly soluble ionic compound and completely dissociates when dissolved in dilute solution, yielding c[OH-] = 0.0125 M. 

[OH-] = [KOH]

Because we know c[OH-], it makes sense to calculate the pOH first:

pOH = -log([OH-])

The pH can be found from the pOH:

@ pH + pOH = 14.00

pH = 14.00 - pOH

The hydronium ion concentration of vinegar is approximately 4 × 10^-3 M. What are the corresponding values of pOH and pH?

<h4>Answer:</h4>

pH = ?

pOH = ?

In this case, we start by calculating the pH because we know the H3O+ concentration.

pH = -log([H3O+])

pOH = 14.00 - pH

a) It is acidic because pH is small and pOH is large.

b) It is acidic because pOH is small and pH is large.

<h3>Example 13.7: Calculation of Percent Ionization from pH</h3>

Calculate the percent ionization of a 0.125-M solution of nitrous acid (a weak acid), with a pH of 2.09.

<h4>Solution</h4>

pH = 2.09

[HNO2]0 = 0.125

The percent ionization for an acid is:

@ fraction_ionized = [H3O+]eq/[HNO2]0 * 100％

The chemical equation for the dissociation of the nitrous acid is

!HNO2(aq) + H2O(l) <=> NO2-(aq) + H3O+(aq)

Since 10^-pH = [H3O+], we find:

[H3O+]eq = 10^-pH

fraction_ionized = [H3O+]eq/[HNO2]0 * 100％

Remember, the logarithm 2.09 indicates a hydronium ion concentration with only two significant figures.





<h3>Example 13.7b: Calculation of Percent Ionization from pH</h3>

Calculate the percent ionization of a 0.10-M solution of acetic acid with a pH of 2.89.
<h4>Answer:</h4>

[acetic acid]0 = 0.10

pH = 2.89 

fraction_ionization = ?

Plan 1) [H3O+]eq 2) percent ionization

[H3O+]eq = 10^-pH

fraction_ionization = [H3O+]eq /[acetic acid]0 * 100 ％

Think about it: What kind of acid is acetic acid?

a) It is strong because the percent ionization is near 100%.

b) It is strong because the percent ionization is low.

c) It is weak because the percent ionization is near 100%.

d) It is weak because the percent ionization is low.



<h3>Example 13.8: The Product K_a * K_b = K_w</h3>

Use the K_b for the nitrite ion, NO2-, to calculate the K_a for its conjugate acid.

<h4>Solution</h4>K_b for NO2- is given in this section as 2.17e-11. 

K_b[NO2-] = 2.17e-11

The conjugate acid of NO2- is HNO2:

!NO2- + H+ <=> HNO2! K_a

!HNO2 + OH- <=> NO2- + H2O! K_b

K_a for HNO2 can be calculated using the relationship:

@ K_a * K_b = K_w

K_w = 1.0e-14

Solving for Ka, we get:

K_a[NO2-] = K_w / K_b[NO2-] 

This answer can be verified by finding the K_a for HNO2 in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/back-matter/ionization-constants-of-weak-acids/">Appendix H</a>.



<h3>Example 13.8b: The Product K_a * K_b = K_w</h3>

We can determine the relative acid strengths of NH4+ and HCN by comparing their ionization constants. The ionization constant of HCN is given in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/back-matter/ionization-constants-of-weak-acids/">Appendix H</a> as 4.9e-10. The ionization constant of NH4+ is not listed, but the ionization constant of its conjugate base, NH3, is listed as 1.8e-5. Determine the ionization constant of NH4+, and decide which is the stronger acid, HCN or NH4+.

<h4>Answer:</h4>

NH4+ is the slightly stronger acid (K_a for NH4+ = 5.6e-10)

K_a[HCN] = 4.9e-10

K_a[NH3] = ?

K_b[NH3] = 1.8e-5

K_w = 1.00e-14

K_a[NH4+] =K_w / K_b[NH3] 

A larger K_a means that the ionization of an acid is more on the side of the products, so NH4+ is a stronger acid than HCN.



<h3>Example 13.9: Determination of K_a from Equilibrium Concentrations</h3>

Acetic acid is the principal ingredient in vinegar (<a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_14_03_Vinegar.jpg">Figure 11</a>); that's why it tastes sour. At equilibrium, a solution contains c[CH3CO2H] = 0.0787 M and c[H3O+]=c[CH3CO2-]=0.00118M. What is the value of K_a for acetic acid?

<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_14_03_Vinegar.jpg", width = "50%">Figure 11Vinegar is a solution of acetic acid, a weak acid. (credit: modification of work by “HomeSpot HQ”/Flickr)

<h4>Solution</h4>

[CH3CO2H]eq = 0.0787

[H3O+]eq =0.00118 

[CH3CO2-]eq =0.00118

K_a = ?

We are asked to calculate an equilibrium constant from equilibrium concentrations. At equilibrium, the value of the equilibrium constant is equal to the reaction quotient for the reaction:

!CH3CO2H(aq) + H2O(l) <=> H3O+(aq) + CH3CO2- (aq)

@ K_a= [H3O+]eq [CH3CO2-]eq / [CH3CO2H]eq

K_a= [H3O+]eq [CH3CO2-]eq / [CH3CO2H]eq



<h3>Example 13.9b: Determination of Ka from Equilibrium Concentrations</h3>

What is the equilibrium constant for the ionization of the HSO4- ion, the weak acid used in some household cleansers:

! HSO4-(aq) + H2O(l) <=> H3O+(aq) + SO4^2-(aq)

In one mixture of NaHSO4 and Na2SO4 at equilibrium, c[H3O+] = 0.027 M; c[HSO4-]=0.29M; and c[SO4^2-]=0.13M.

<h4>Answer:</h4>

Ka for HSO4- = 1.2 × 10^-

[H3O+] = 0.027 

[HSO4-] = 0.29 

[SO4^2-] = 0.13

K_a = [H3O+] [SO4^2-] / [HSO4-] 

Think about it: The problem states that HSO4- is a weak acid. How is K_a related to the strength of an acid:

a) The larger K_a, the stronger the acid
b) The smaller K_a, the stronger the acid
c) You would need to know the concentration of the acid as well to make a statement
d) You would need to know the pH of the solution as well to make a statement

<h3>Example 13.10: Determination of Kb from Equilibrium Concentrations</h3>

Caffeine, C8H10N4O2 is a weak base. What is the value of Kb for caffeine if a solution at equilibrium has c[C8H10N4O2] = 0.050 M; c[C8H10N4O2H+] = 5.0e-3 M; and c[OH-] = 2.5e-3 M?

<h4>Solution</h4>

[C8H10N4O2]eq = 0.050

[C8H10N4O2H+]eq = 5.0e-3

[OH-]eq = 2.5e-3

At equilibrium, the value of the equilibrium constant is equal to the reaction quotient for the reaction:

!C8H10N4O2(aq) + H2O(l) <=> C8H10N4O2H+(aq) + OH-(aq)

K_b = [C8H10N4O2H+]eq [OH-]eq/[C8H10N4O2]eq



<h3>Example 13.10b: Determination of Kb from Equilibrium Concentrations</h3>

What is the equilibrium constant for the ionization of the HPO4^2- ion, a weak base:

!HPO4^2-(aq) + H2O(l) <=> H2PO4-(aq) + OH-(aq)

In a solution containing a mixture of NaH2PO4 and Na2HPO4 at equilibrium, c[OH-] = 1.3e-6 M; c[H2PO4-]=0.042M; and c[HPO4^2-]=0.341M.

<h4>Answer:</h4>

Kb for HPO42-=1.6×10-

[OH-]eq = 1.3e-6

[H2PO4-]eq =0.042

[HPO4^2-]eq =0.341

K_b = [H2PO4-]eq [OH-]eq / [HPO4^2-]eq

Think about it: What does a K_b of 1.6e-7 mean?
a) Solutions of this base in water have close to neutral pH
b) This base is weaker than one with a larger K_b
c) This base is weaker than one with a smaller K_b
d) The K_b is so low, this substance is not considered a base but instead a spectator ion

<h3>Example 13.11: Determination of Ka or Kb from pH</h3>

The pH of a 0.0516-M solution of nitrous acid, HNO2, is 2.34. What is its K_a?

! HNO2(aq) + H2O(l) <=> H3O+(aq) + NO2-(aq)

<h4>Solution</h4>

pH = 2.34

[HNO2]init = 0.0516

K_a = ?

We determine an equilibrium constant starting with the initial concentrations of HNO2, H3O+, and NO2- as well as one of the final concentrations, the concentration of hydronium ion at equilibrium. (Remember that pH is simply another way to express the concentration of hydronium ion.)

We can solve this problem with the following steps in which x is a change in concentration of a species in the reaction:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_14_03_steps_img.jpg", width="30%"}

We can summarize the various concentrations and changes as shown here (the concentration of water does not appear in the expression for the equilibrium constant, so we do not need to consider its concentration):

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_14_03_ICETable1_img.jpg", width="50%"}

To get the various values in the ICE (Initial, Change, Equilibrium) table, we first calculate [H3O+], the equilibrium concentration of H3O+, from the pH:

[H3O+]eq = 10^-pH

The change in concentration of H3O+, Δ[H3O+], is the difference between the equilibrium concentration of H3O+, which we determined from the pH, and the initial concentration, [H3O]i. The initial concentration of H3O+ is its concentration in pure water, which is so much less than the final concentration that we approximate it as zero (~0).

Δ[H3O+] = [H3O+]eq - 0

The change in concentration of NO2- is equal to the change in concentration of [H3O+]. For each 1 mol of H3O+ that forms, 1 mol of NO2- forms. The equilibrium concentration of HNO2 is equal to its initial concentration plus the change in its concentration.

Δ[NO2-] = Δ[H3O+]

Δ[HNO2] = -Δ[H3O+]

[NO2-]eq = Δ[NO2-] + 0

[HNO2]eq = Δ[HNO2] + [HNO2]init

Now we can fill in the ICE table with the concentrations at equilibrium, as shown here:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_14_03_ICETable2_img.jpg", width="50%"}

Finally, we calculate the value of the equilibrium constant using the data in the table:

K_a = [H3O+]eq [NO2-]eq /[HNO2]eq 



<h3>Example 13.11b: Determination of Ka or Kb from pH</h3>

The pH of a solution of household ammonia, a 0.950-M solution of NH3, is 11.612. What is K_b for NH3.

<h4>Answer:</h4>
[NH3]init = 0.950

pH = 11.612

K_b = ? 

! NH3 + H2O <=> NH4+ + OH-

@K_b = [NH4+]eq [OH-]eq / [NH3]eq

pOH = 14.00 - pH

[OH-]eq = 10^-pOH

Δ[OH-] = [OH-]eq - 0

Δ[NH4+] = Δ[OH-]  

Δ[NH3] =  -Δ[OH-]  

[NH3]eq = [NH3]init + Δ[NH3] 

[NH4+]eq = Δ[NH4+] + 0

K_b = [NH4+]eq [OH-]eq / [NH3]eq 

Think about it: What is a good estimate for the pH of a strong base at the same concentration (0.959 M)?

a) You would need to know the K_b of that strong base to make an estimate

b) pH ~ 0 because the concentration is close to 1 M

c) pH ~ 14 because pOH ~ 0

d) pH would be neutral you need an acid (H+ donor) to change the pH



<h3>Example 13.12: Equilibrium Concentrations in a Solution of a Weak Acid</h3>

Formic acid, HCO2H, is the irritant that causes the body’s reaction to ant stings (<a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_14_03_AntSting.jpg">Figure 12</a>).

<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_14_03_AntSting.jpg", width="30%">Figure 12: The pain of an ant’s sting is caused by formic acid. (credit: John Tann)

What is the concentration of hydronium ion and the pH in a 0.534-M solution of formic acid?

! HCO2H(aq) + H2O(l) <=> H3O+(aq) + HCO2-(aq)

<h4>Solution</h4>

K_a=1.8e-4

[HCO2H]init = 0.534

Determine x and equilibrium concentrations. The equilibrium expression is:

@K_a = [H3O+]eq [HCO2-]eq / [HCO2H]eq

The concentration of water does not appear in the expression for the equilibrium constant, so we do not need to consider its change in concentration when setting up the ICE table.

The table shows initial concentrations (concentrations before the acid ionizes), changes in concentration, and equilibrium concentrations follows (the data given in the problem appear in color):

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_14_03_ICETable3_img.jpg", width="50%"}

Solve for x and the equilibrium concentrations. At equilibrium:

@ K_a = [H3O+][HCO2-]/[HCO2H] = (x)(x)/([HCO2H]init - x)

Because the initial concentration of acid is reasonably large and K_a is very small, we assume that x << [HCO2H]init, which permits us to simplify the denominator term as ([HCO2H]init - x) = [HCO2H]init. This gives:

@ K_a = x^2 / [HCO2H]init

Now solve for x as follows:

@ [HCO2H]init K_a = x^2

@ x = sqrt(K_a [HCO2H]init)

x = sqrt(K_a [HCO2H]init)

To check the assumption that x is small compared to 0.534, we calculate the ratio and express it in percent:

ratio = x / 0.534 * 100 ％

x is less than 5% of the initial concentration; the assumption is valid.

We find the equilibrium concentration of hydronium ion in this formic acid solution from its initial concentration and the change in that concentration as indicated in the last line of the table:

[H3O+]eq = 0 + x

The pH of the solution can be found by taking the negative log of the [H3O+], so:

pH = -log([H3O+]eq)



<h3>Example 13.12b: Equilibrium Concentrations in a Solution of a Weak Acid</h3>

Only a small fraction of a weak acid ionizes in aqueous solution. What is the percent ionization of acetic acid in a 0.100-M solution of acetic acid, CH3CO2H?

! CH3CO2H(aq) + H2O(l) <=> H3O+(aq) + CH3CO2-(aq) !K_a=1.8e-5

(Hint: Determine [CH3CO2-] at equilibrium.) Recall that the percent ionization is the fraction of acetic acid that is ionized × 100％, or [CH3CO2-]/[CH3CO2H]initial×100.

<h4>Answer:</h4>


[CH3CO2H]initial = 0.100

K_a = 1.8e-5

@ K_a = [H3O+]eq [CH3CO2-]eq / [CH3CO2H]eq = x^2 /([CH3CO2H]initial - x)

x = sqrt(K_a [CH3CO2H]initial)

[CH3CO2-]eq = x

fraction_ionized = [CH3CO2-]eq / [CH3CO2H]initial * 100％

Think about it: What concentration of a strong acid such as HCl would result in a similar concentration of H3O+ and a similar pH as 0.100 M acetic acid?

a) same concentration

b) strong acids always result in a more acidic solution, regardless of concentration

c) 1.34% of 0.100 M, so about 1 mM

d) there is no H3O+ in a solution of acetic acid (the acetic acid itself makes it taste sour)



e) acetic acid is not acidic





<h3>Example 13.20: pH Changes in Buffered and Unbuffered Solutions</h3>

(a) Calculate the pH of an acetate buffer that is a mixture with 0.100 M acetic acid and 0.100 M sodium acetate.

<h4>Solution</h4>

To determine the pH of the buffer solution we use a typical equilibrium calculation (as illustrated in earlier Examples):

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_14_06_steps1_img.jpg", width="50%"}

Determine the direction of change. The equilibrium in a mixture of H3O^+, CH3CO2-, and CH3CO2H is:

! CH3CO2H(aq) + H2O(l) <=> H3O+(aq) + CH3CO2-(aq)

The equilibrium constant for CH3CO2H is not given, so we look it up in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/back-matter/ionization-constants-of-weak-acids/">Appendix H</a>: 

K_a = 1.8e-5

[CH3CO2H]init = 0.10

[CH3CO2-]init = 0.10

With 

@[CH3CO2H]init = [CH3CO2-]init = 0.10 M and [H3O+] = ~0 M

the reaction shifts to the right to form H3O+.

Determine x and equilibrium concentrations. A table of changes and concentrations follows:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_14_06_ICETable16_img.jpg", width="50%"}

Solve for x and the equilibrium concentrations. We find:

@K_a = [CH3CO2-]eq [H3O+] / [CH3CO2H]eq = ([CH3CO2-]init+x) x / ([CH3CO2H]init - x)

@x = K_a ([CH3CO2H]init - x)/([CH3CO2-]init+x)

x = K_a

and

[H3O+] = 0 + x

Thus:

pH = -log([H3O+])

Check the work. If we calculate all calculated equilibrium concentrations, we find that the equilibrium value of the reaction coefficient, Q = K_a.

<hr>

(b) Calculate the pH after 1.00 mL of 0.100 M NaOH is added to 100 mL of this buffer, giving a solution with a volume of 101 mL.

First, we calculate the concentrations of an intermediate mixture resulting from the complete reaction between the acid in the buffer and the added base. Then we determine the concentrations of the mixture at the new equilibrium:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_14_06_steps2_img.jpg", width="50%"}

Determine the chemical amount of NaOH. One milliliter (0.0010 L) of 0.10 M NaOH contains:

c[NaOH] = 0.100 M

V[NaOH] = 1.00 mL

n[NaOH] = c[NaOH] V[NaOH]

Determine the chemical amount of CH2CO2H. Before reaction, 100 mL of the buffer solution contains:

V[CH3CO2H] = 100 mL

c[CH3CO2H] = 0.100 M

n[CH3CO2H] = V[CH3CO2H] * c[CH3CO2H]

n[NaCH3CO2] = n[CH3CO2H] #(same volume and concentration as CH3CO2H)

Solve for the amount of NaCH3CO2 produced. The 1.0 × 10^-4 mol of NaOH neutralizes 1.0 × 10^-4 mol of CH3CO2H, leaving:

n[CH3CO2H]mix = n[CH3CO2H] - n[NaOH]

and producing 1.0 × 10^-4 mol of NaCH3CO2. This makes a total of:

n[NaCH3CO2]mix = n[NaCH3CO2] + n[NaOH]

Find the concentration of the products. After reaction, CH3CO2H and NaCH3CO2 are contained in 101 mL of the intermediate solution, so:

V_mix = 101. mL

[CH3CO2H]mix = n[CH3CO2H]mix / V_mix

[NaCH3CO2]mix = n[NaCH3CO2]mix / V_mix

Now we calculate the pH after the intermediate solution, which is 0.098 M in CH3CO2H and 0.100 M in NaCH3CO2, comes to equilibrium. The calculation is very similar to that in part (a) of this example:

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_14_06_steps3_img.jpg", width="50%"}

This series of calculations gives a pH of 4.75. Thus the addition of the base barely changes the pH of the solution (<a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_14_06_compare.jpg">Figure 17</a>).

<hr>

(c) For comparison, calculate the pH after 1.00 mL of 0.100 M NaOH is added to 100 mL of a solution of an unbuffered solution with a pH of 4.74 (a 1.8e-5 M solution of HCl). The volume of the final solution is 101 mL.

<h4>Solution</h4>

V[HCl] = 100. mL

c[HCl] = 1.8e-5 M

This 1.8 × 10^-5-M solution of HCl has the same hydronium ion concentration as the 0.10-M solution of acetic acid-sodium acetate buffer described in part (a) of this example. The solution contains:

n[HCl] = c[HCl] V[HCl]

As shown in part (b), 1 mL of 0.10 M NaOH contains 1.0 × 10^-4 mol of NaOH. When the NaOH and HCl solutions are mixed, the HCl is the limiting reagent in the reaction. All of the HCl reacts, and the amount of NaOH that remains is:

n[NaOH]mix = n[NaOH] - n[HCl]

The concentration of NaOH is:

c[NaOH]mix = n[NaOH]mix / V_mix

The pOH of this solution is:

pOH = -log(c[NaOH]mix / 1 M)

The pH is:

pH = 14.00-pOH

The pH changes from 4.74 to 10.99 in this unbuffered solution. This compares to the change of 4.74 to 4.75 that occurred when the same amount of NaOH was added to the buffered solution described in part (b).



<h3>Example 13.20b: pH Changes in Buffered and Unbuffered Solutions</h3>

Show that adding 1.0 mL of 0.10 M HCl changes the pH of 100 mL of a 1.8e-5 M HCl solution from 4.74 to 3.00.

<h4>Answer:</h4>

pH = 3.0

V_add = 1.0 mL

c_add = 0.10 M

V_init = 100. mL

c_init = 1.8E-5M

pH_init = ?

pH_mix = ?

@pH_init = 4.74 

@pH_mix = 3.00 

pH_init = -log(c_init / 1 M)

c_mix = (c_init V_init + c_add V_add) / (V_init + V_add)

pH_mix = -log(c_mix / 1M)



Think about it: What had a greater influence on the pH in the mix, i.e. brought in more H+ ions?

a) The solution with the higher volume and higher concentration

b) The solution with the higher volume and lower concentration

c) The solution with the lower volume and higher concentration

d) The solution with the lower volume and lower concentration



<h3>Example 13.21: Calculating pH for Titration Solutions: Strong Acid+Strong Base</h3>

A titration is carried out for 25.00 mL of 0.100 M HCl (strong acid) with 0.100 M of a strong base NaOH the titration curve is shown in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_14_07_titration.jpg">Figure 21</a>. Calculate the pH at these volumes of added base solution:

(a) 0.00 mL

(b) 12.50 mL

(c) 25.00 mL

(d) 37.50 mL

<h4>Solution</h4>

c[HCl] = 0.100 M

c[NaOH] = 0.100 M

V[HCl] = 25.00 mL

V[NaOH]a = 0.00 mL

V[NaOH]b = 12.50 mL

V[NaOH]c = 25.00 mL

V[NaOH]d = 37.50 mL

pH_a = ?

pH_b = ?

pH_c = ?

pH_d = ?

<hr>Part (a) Determining the pH before any NaOH is added is straightforward. We are looking at a strong acid with known concentration, so we know the concentration of H3O+ and can calculate the pH

pH_a = -log(c[HCl] / 1 M)

<hr>Plan for parts (b), (c), and (d)

The HCl solution provides H3O+, and the added NaOH provides OH-. The H3O+ and OH- ions neutralize each other; the species that was in excess remains, and its concentration determines the pH. For each point in the titration, we have to determine the amount of H3O+ and OH- present before neutralization to figure out what remains after neutralization. At first, H3O+ will be in excess and the solution will be acidic. Eventually, sufficient OH- is added to neutralize all of the H3O+ with OH- remaining, and the solution will be basic.

! H3O+ + OH- -> 2H2O

The amount of H3O+ provided by the HCl solution is the same for all points in the titration (because we are not adding any more HCl solution during the titration):

n[H3O+]initial = c[HCl] V[HCl] 

The amount of OH- provided by the NaOH solution and the total volume of the solution changes as the titration proceeds, so we have to calculate these separately for parts (b), (c) and (d)

<hr>Part (b)

Because strong bases dissociate completely, the amount of OH- ions provided by the NaOH solution is equal to the amount of NaOH added. 

n[OH-]initial_b = c[NaOH] V[NaOH]b

In the neutralization reaction, H3O+ and OH- react in a ratio of 1:1, so the limiting reactant is simply the one present at lower amount. However, we will also show this by calculating how much H3O+ and OH- remain after neutralization

n[->]b = min(n[H3O+]initial/1, n[OH-]initial_b/1)

n[H3O+]remaining_b = n[H3O+]initial - n[->]b

n[OH-]remaining_b = n[OH-]initial_b - n[->]b

So OH- is used up and some H3O+ remains. For calculating the pH, we need a concentration, not an amount, so we will calculate the volume of the mixture at point (b), then calculate the concentration and then the pH:

V_total_b = V[HCl] + V[NaOH]b 

c[H3O+]remaining_b = n[H3O+]remaining_b / V_total_b 

pH_b = -log(c[H3O+]remaining_b / 1 M)

<hr>Part (c)

n[OH-]initial_c = c[NaOH] V[NaOH]c 

n[->]c = min(n[H3O+]initial/1, n[OH-]initial_c/1)

n[H3O+]remaining_c = n[H3O+]initial - n[->]c

n[OH-]remaining_c = n[OH-]initial_c - n[->]c

At point (c), the initial amounts of H3O+ and OH- are equal. After neutralization, it is as if we had not added any strong acid or base to the solution. Because of the autodissociation of water, however, the H3O+ concentration is not zero.

K_w = 1.00e-14

pH_c = -log(sqrt(K_w))

<hr> Part (d)

n[OH-]initial_d = c[NaOH] V[NaOH]d 

n[->]d = min(n[H3O+]initial/1, n[OH-]initial_d/1)

n[H3O+]remaining_d = n[H3O+]initial - n[->]d

n[OH-]remaining_d = n[OH-]initial_d - n[->]d

At point (d), OH- is in excess over H3O+, i.e. after neutralization, some OH- will remain and determine the pH.

V_total_d = V[HCl] + V[NaOH]d  

c[OH-]remaining_d  = n[OH-]remaining_d /V_total_d 

Now we can first calculate the pOH, and from that the pH

pOH_d = -log(c[OH-]remaining_d / 1M)

pH_d = 14.00 - pOH_d

To summarize, the initial pH is 1.00, which then goes up to 1.48, 7.00 and 12.30 in the course of the titration.



<h3>Example 13.21b: Calculating pH for Titration Solutions: Strong Acid/Strong Base</h3>

Calculate the pH for the strong acid/strong base titration between 50.0 mL of 0.100 M HNO3(aq) and 0.200 M NaOH (titrant) at the listed volumes of added base: 0.00 mL, 15.0 mL, 25.0 mL, and 40.0 mL.

<h4>Answer:</h4>
V[HNO3] = 50.0 mL

c[HNO3] = 0.100 M

c[NaOH] = 0.200 M

V[NaOH]a = 0.00 mL

V[NaOH]b = 15.0 mL

V[NaOH]c = 25.0 mL

V[NaOH]d = 40.0 mL

<hr> Part (a): pH of strong acid

pH_a = -log(c[HNO3] / 1M)

<hr> Part (b): mixture of strong acid and strong base

! HNO3 + NaOH -> H2O + Na+ + NO3- ! 1:1 stoichiometry

n[H3O+]initial = c[HNO3] V[HNO3] 

n[OH-]initial_b = c[NaOH] V[NaOH]b  

H3O+ is in excess. Let's calculate the concentration:
n[H3O+]remaining_b = n[H3O+]initial - n[OH-]initial_b
V_total_b = V[HNO3] + V[NaOH]b
c[H3O+]remaining_b = n[H3O+]remaining_b / V_total_b

pH_b = -log(c[H3O+]remaining_b / 1M)

<hr> Part (c): mixture of strong acid and strong base

n[OH-]initial_c = c[NaOH] V[NaOH]c 

Stoichiometric amounts, neutral solution

pH_c = 7.00

<hr> Part (d): mixture of strong acid and strong base

n[OH-]initial_d = c[NaOH] V[NaOH]d 

OH- is in excess. Let's calculate the concentration:
n[OH-]remaining_d = n[OH-]initial_d - n[H3O+]initial
V_total_d = V[HNO3] + V[NaOH]d
c[OH-]remaining_d = n[OH-]remaining_d / V_total_d

pOH_d = -log(c[OH-]remaining_d / 1M)

pH_d = 14.00 - pOH_d


<h3>Example 16.1: Redistribution of Matter during a Spontaneous Process</h3>

Describe how matter is redistributed when the following spontaneous processes take place:

(a) A solid sublimes.

(b) A gas condenses.

(c) A drop of food coloring added to a glass of water forms a solution with uniform color.

<h4>Solution</h4>

<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_16_02_Process.jpg">Figure 6(credit a: modification of work by Jenny Downing; credit b: modification of work by “Fuzzy Gerdes”/Flickr; credit c: modification of work by Sahar Atwa)

(a) Sublimation is the conversion of a solid (relatively high density) to a gas (much lesser density). This process yields a much greater dispersal of matter, since the molecules will occupy a much greater volume after the solid-to-gas transition.

(b) Condensation is the conversion of a gas (relatively low density) to a liquid (much greater density). This process yields a much lesser dispersal of matter, since the molecules will occupy a much lesser volume after the solid-to-gas transition.

(c) The process in question is dilution. The food dye molecules initially occupy a much smaller volume (the drop of dye solution) than they occupy once the process is complete (in the full glass of water). The process therefore entails a greater dispersal of matter. The process may also yield a more uniform dispersal of matter, since the initial state of the system involves two regions of different dye concentrations (high in the drop, zero in the water), and the final state of the system contains a single dye concentration throughout.



<h3>Example 16.1b: Redistribution of Matter during a Spontaneous Process</h3>
Describe how matter and/or energy is redistributed when you empty a canister of compressed air into a room.
<h4>Answer:</h4>
This is also a dilution process, analogous to example (c). It entails both a greater and more uniform dispersal of matter as the compressed air in the canister is permitted to expand into the lower-pressure air of the room

<h3>Example 16.2: Determination of ΔS</h3>

Consider the system shown here. What is the change in entropy for a process that converts the system from distribution (a) to (c)?

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_16_03_Matter_img.jpg", width="50%"}

<h4>Solution</h4>

We are interested in the following change:

The initial number of microstates is one, the final six:

W_a = 1

W_c = 6

@ΔS = k_B ln(W_c/W_a)

R_gas = 8.314 J/(K mol)

N_A = 6.022e23 / mol

k_B = R_gas / N_A

ΔS = k_B ln(W_c/W_a)

The sign of this result is consistent with expectation; since there are more microstates possible for the final state than for the initial state, the change in entropy should be positive.





<h3>Example 16.2b: Determination of ΔS</h3>

Consider the system shown in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_16_03_Energy.jpg">Figure 9</a>. What is the change in entropy for the process where all the energy is transferred from the hot object (AB) to the cold object (CD)?

<h4>Answer:</h4>

no change, same number of micro states



<h3>Example 16.3: Predicting the Sign of ∆S</h3>

Predict the sign of the entropy change for the following processes. Indicate the reason for each of your predictions.

![a] One mole liquid water at room temperature  ->  one mole liquid water at 50 °C

![b] Ag+(aq) +Cl-(aq) -> AgCl(s)

![c] C6H6(l) +15/2O2(g) -> 6CO2(g) +3H2O(l)

![d] NH3(s) -> NH3(l)

<h4>Solution</h4>

(a) positive, temperature increases

(b) negative, reduction in the number of ions (particles) in solution, decreased dispersal of matter

(c) negative, net decrease in the amount of gaseous species

(d) positive, phase transition from solid to liquid, net increase in dispersal of matter



<h3>Example 16.3b: Predicting the Sign of ∆S</h3>

Predict the sign of the enthalpy change for the following processes. Give a reason for your prediction.

![a] NaNO3(s) -> Na +(aq) +NO3-(aq)

![b] !the freezing of liquid water

![c] CO2(s) -> CO2(g)

![d] CaCO(s) -> CaO(s) +CO2(g)

<h4>Answer:</h4>

(a) Positive; The solid dissolves to give an increase of mobile ions in solution. 

(b) Negative; The liquid becomes a more ordered solid. 

(c) Positive; The relatively ordered solid becomes a gas. 

(d) Positive; There is a net production of one mole of gas



<h3>Example 16.4: Will Ice Spontaneously Melt?</h3>

The entropy change for the process

!H2O(s) -> H2O(l)

is 22.1 J/K and requires that the surroundings transfer 6.00 kJ of heat to the system. Is the process spontaneous at -10.00 °C? Is it spontaneous at  +10.00 °C?

<h4>Solution</h4>

ΔS_sys = 22.1 J/K

q_surr = -6.00 kJ

@ΔSsurr = q_surr / T

T1 = -10.00 °aC

T2 = 10.00 °aC

We can assess the spontaneity of the process by calculating the entropy change of the universe. If ΔSuniv is positive, then the process is spontaneous. At both temperatures, ΔSsys = 22.1 J/K and qsurr = -6.00 kJ.

At -10.00 °C (263.15 K), the following is true:

ΔS_surr_1 = q_surr / T1

ΔS_univ = ΔS_sys + ΔS_surr_1

ΔS_univ < 0, so melting is nonspontaneous (not spontaneous) at -10.0 °C.

At 10.00 °C (283.15 K), the following is true:

ΔS_surr_2 = q_surr / T2

ΔS_univ_2 = ΔS_sys + ΔS_surr_2

ΔSuniv > 0, so melting is spontaneous at 10.00 °C.



<h3>Example 16.4b: Will Ice Spontaneously Melt?</h3>

In example 16.4, ΔS_univ for melting of ice was determined at two different temperatures:

ΔS_univ = -0.7 J/K for T = -10.00 °C

ΔS_univ =  0.9 J/K for T =  10.00 °C

Using this information, determine if liquid water will spontaneously freeze at the same temperatures. What can you say about the values of Suniv?

<h4>Answer:</h4>

Entropy is a state function, and freezing is the opposite of melting. At -10.00 °C spontaneous,  +0.7 J/K; at  +10.00 °C nonspontaneous, -0.9 J/K



<h3>Example 16.5: Determination of ΔS°</h3>

Calculate the standard entropy change for the following process:

!H2O(g) -> H2O(l)

<h4>Solution</h4>

The value of the standard entropy change at room temperature, ΔS°_298, is the difference between the standard entropy of the product, H2O(l), and the standard entropy of the reactant, H2O(g).

@ΔS°_298 = S°[H2O(l)]298 - S°[H2O(g)]298

S°[H2O(l)]298 = 70.0 J /(mol K)

S°[H2O(g)]298 = 188.8 J / (mol K)

ΔS°_298 = S°[H2O(l)]298 - S°[H2O(g)]298

The value for ΔS°_298 is negative, as expected for this phase transition (condensation), which the previous section discussed.



<h3>Example 16.5b: Determination of ΔS°</h3>

Calculate the standard entropy change for the following process:

!H2(g) +C2H4(g) -> C2H6(g)

<h4>Answer:</h4>

S°[H2(g)] = 130.7 J /(K mol)

S°[C2H4(g)] = 219.3 J /(K mol)

S°[C2H6(g)] = 229.2 J /(K mol)

ΔS° = S°[C2H6(g)] - S°[C2H4(g)]  - S°[H2(g)] 

-120.6 J mol^-1 K^-

Think about it: We expect the entropy to...

a) increase because the number of gas particles increases

a) decrease because the number of gas particles increases

a) increase because the number of gas particles decreases

a) decrease because the number of gas particles decreases



<h3>Example 16.6: Determination of ΔS°</h3>

Calculate the standard entropy change for the combustion of methanol, CH3OH:

!2CH3OH(l) +3O2(g) -> 2CO2(g) +4H2O(l)

<h4>Solution</h4>

The value of the standard entropy change is equal to the difference between the standard entropies of the products and the entropies of the reactants scaled by their stoichiometric coefficients.

@ΔS°_298= sum(ν S°[products]298) - sum(ν S°[reactants]298)

S°[CO2(g)]298 = 213.8 J/(mol K)

S°[H2O(l)]298 = 70.0 J/(mol K)

S°[CH3OH(l)]298 = 126.8 J/(mol K)

S°[O2(g)]298 = 205.03 J/(mol K)

ΔS°_298= sum(2 S°[CO2(g)]298, 4 S°[H2O(l)]298) - sum(2 S°[CH3OH(l)]298, 3 S°[O2(g)]298)

!ΔS°= -161.1J/mol·K

Think about it: The entropy in this reaction decreases (less freedom). What would you expect for the same reaction if water as a product would form as a gas (S°[H2O(g)]298 is 188.8 J/(mol K))?

a) The entropy would increase because the number of gas particles increases

b) The entropy would increase because the number of gas particles increases

c) The entropy would increase because the number of gas particles increases

d) The entropy would increase because the number of gas particles increases

<h3>Example 16.7: Evaluation of ΔG° Change from ΔH° and ΔS°</h3>

Use standard enthalpy and entropy data from <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/back-matter/standard-thermodynamic-properties-for-selected-substances/">Appendix G</a> to calculate the standard free energy change for the vaporization of water at room temperature (298 K). What does the computed value for ΔG° say about the spontaneity of this process?

<h4>Solution</h4>

The process of interest is the following:

!H2O(l) -> H2O(g)

The standard change in free energy may be calculated using the following equation:

@ΔG298°=ΔH°-T ΔS°

From <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/back-matter/standard-thermodynamic-properties-for-selected-substances/">Appendix G</a>, here is the data:

Substance

ΔHf°[H2O(l)] = -286.83 kJ/mol

S°[H2O(l)] = 70.0 J/(K mol)

ΔHf°[H2O(g)] = -241.82 kJ/mol

S°[H2O(g)] = 188.8 J/(K mol)

Combining 

ΔH° = ΔHf°[H2O(g)] - ΔHf°[H2O(l)]

ΔS° = S°[H2O(g)] - S°[H2O(l)]

T = 298 K

ΔG° = ΔH° - T ΔS°

At 298 K (25 °C) ΔG298°>0, and so boiling is nonspontaneous (not spontaneous).

Think about it: ΔH° for boiling water is positive because:

a) The freedom of particles increases

b) The freedom of particles decreases

c) You gain energy when breaking hydrogen bonds

d) You expend energy to break hydrogen bonds

ΔᵣG° = ΔG°[H2] + ΔG°[C2H4] - ΔG°[C2H6]

Think about it: Looking at the chemical reaction, what could you have predicted:

a) That the entropy increases because the number of particles increases

a) That the entropy decreases because the number of particles increases

a) That the reaction is spontaneous because the number of particles increases

<h3>Example 16.8: Calculation of ΔG298°</h3>

Consider the decomposition of yellow mercury(II) oxide.

!HgO(s,yellow) -> Hg(l) +1/2O2(g)

Calculate the standard free energy change at room temperature, ΔG298°, using (a) standard free energies of formation and (b) standard enthalpies of formation and standard entropies. Do the results indicate the reaction to be spontaneous or nonspontaneous under standard conditions?

<h4>Solution</h4>

The required data are available in <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/back-matter/standard-thermodynamic-properties-for-selected-substances/">Appendix G</a> and are shown here.

<table style="text-align:center">

<tr style="border-bottom: 2px solid black;">

<td>Compound   <td>ΔGf°(kJ/mol) </td>   <td>ΔHf° (kJ/mol)</td>  <td>S298 (J/(K mol))</td></tr>

<tr><td>HgO (s, yellow)</td>  <td>-58.43</td>  <td>-90.46</td>  <td>71.13</tr>

<tr><td>Hg(l)          </td>  <td>0     </td>  <td>0      </td> <td>75.9 </tr>

<tr><td>O2(g)          </td>  <td>0     </td>  <td>0      </td> <td>205.2</tr>

</table>

(a) Using free energies of formation

@ΔG298°=∑νGS298°(products)-∑νΔG298°(reactants)

ΔG298°[HgO(s,yellow)] = -58.43 kJ/mol

ΔG298°[->] = -ΔG298°[HgO(s,yellow)]

(b) Using enthalpies and entropies of formation:

@ΔH298°=∑νΔH298°(products)-∑νΔH298°(reactants)

ΔH298°[HgO(s,yellow)] = -90.46 kJ/mol

ΔH298°[->] = -ΔH298°[HgO(s,yellow)]

@ΔS298°=∑νΔS298°(products)-∑νΔS298°(reactants)

ΔS298°[Hg(l)] = 75.9 J/(mol K)

ΔS298°[O2(g)] = 205.2 J/(mol K)

ΔS298°[HgO(s,yellow)] = 71.13 J/(mol K)

ΔS298°[->] = ΔS298°[Hg(l)] +(1/2)ΔS298°[O2(g)] - ΔS298°[HgO(s,yellow)]

@ΔG°=ΔH° - T ΔS°

T = 298.15 K

ΔG°[->] = ΔH298°[->] - T ΔS298°[->]

Both ways to calculate the standard free energy change at 25 °C give the same numerical value (to three significant figures), and both predict that the process is nonspontaneous (not spontaneous) at room temperature.

<h3>Example 9.2: Calculation of Barometric Pressure</h3>

Show the calculation supporting the claim that atmospheric pressure near sea level corresponds to the pressure exerted by a column of mercury that is about 760 mm high. The density of mercury = 13.6 g/cm^3.

<h4>Solution</h4>

h[Hg]column = 760. mm

ρ[Hg] = 13.6 g/cm^3

@P_hydrostatic = h_column ρ g0

g0 = 9.81 m/s^2

P_hydrostatic = h[Hg]column ρ[Hg] g0

P_hydrostatic using atm

The calculation supports the claim.



<h3>Example 9.2b: Calculation of Barometric Pressure</h3>

P_normal using kg m s

ρ_water = 1.00 kg/L

@P_hydrostatic = h_column ρ g0

g0 = 9.81 m/s^2

h_column = P_normal / (ρ_water g0)

Think about it: If I fill a graduated cylinder (height of 30 cm) with water, cover the opening with parafilm and invert it, will the water flow out?

<h3>Example 9.9: Using the Ideal Gas Law</h3>

Methane, CH4, is being considered for use as an alternative automotive fuel to replace gasoline. One gallon of gasoline could be replaced by 655 g of CH4. What is the volume of this much methane at 25 °C and 745 torr?

<h4>Solution</h4>

V[CH4] = ?

m[CH4] = 655. g

T = 25. °aC

P = 745. torr

@ P V_ = n R T

R = 8.314 J/(mol K)

n[CH4] = m[CH4] / M[CH4]

V[CH4] = n[CH4] R T / P

It would require 1020 L (269 gal) of gaseous methane at about 1 atm of pressure to replace 1 gal of gasoline. It requires a large container to hold enough methane at 1 atm to replace several gallons of gasoline.

V[CH4] using L



<h3>Example 9.9b: Using the Ideal Gas Law</h3>

Calculate the pressure in atm of 2520 moles of hydrogen gas stored at 27 °C in the 180-L storage tank of a modern hydrogen-powered car.

<h4>Answer:</h4>

P = ?

n[H2] = 2520. mol

T = 27. °aC

V[H2] = 180. L

@ P V_gas = n R T

R = 8.314 J/(mol K)
R using atm L

P = n[H2] R T / V[H2]

Think about it: If the highest safe pressure for gas storage cylinders is 150 atm, would it be safe to transport the hydrogen gas under these conditions:
a) Yes, because the calculated pressure is lower than 150 atm
b) Yes, because the calculated pressure is higher than 150 atm
c) No, because the calculated pressure is lower than 150 atm
d) No, because the calculated pressure is higher than 150 atm




<h3>Example 9.10: Using the Combined Gas Law</h3>

When filled with air, a typical scuba tank with a volume of 13.2 L has a pressure of 153 atm (<a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_09_02_Scuba.jpg">Figure 16</a>). If the water temperature is 27 °C, how many liters of air will such a tank provide to a diver’s lungs at a depth of approximately 70 feet in the ocean where the pressure is 3.13 atm?

<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_09_02_Scuba.jpg">Figure 16:Scuba divers use compressed air to breathe while underwater. (credit: modification of work by Mark Goodchild)

<h3>Solution</h3>

V_tank = 13.2 L

P_tank = 153. atm

T_tank = 27. °aC

P_in_lungs = 3.13 atm

T_in_lungs = 37. °aC

V_in_lungs = ?

@ P1 V1/ T1 = P2 V2 / T2

V_in_lungs = V_tank (T_in_lungs / T_tank) (P_tank / P_in_lungs)

(Note: Be advised that this particular example is one in which the assumption of ideal gas behavior is not very reasonable, since it involves gases at relatively high pressures and low temperatures. Despite this limitation, the calculated volume can be viewed as a good “ballpark” estimate.)





<h3>Example 9.11: Derivation of a Density Formula from the Ideal Gas Law</h3>

Use PV = nRT to derive a formula for the density of gas in g/L

<h4>Solution</h4>

@ P V_gas = n_gas R T

The density is defined as mass per volume. To get mass into the equation, multiply each side by the molar mass of the gas:

@ M_gas P V_gas =  m_gas R T

To have m_gas / V_gas on the right hand side, divide by V_gas, R, and T:

@ M_gas P / (R T) =  m_gas / V_gas

Finally, we can substiture ρ, the symbol for density to arrive at

@ ρ = m_gas / V_gas = M_gas P / (R T)



<h3>Example 9.11b: Derivation of a Density Formula from the Ideal Gas Law</h3>

A gas was found to have a density of 0.0847 g/L at 17.0 °C and a pressure of 760 torr. What is its molar mass? What is the gas?

<h4>Answer:</h4>

ρ_gas = 0.0847 g/L

T = 17.0 °aC

P = 760. torr

M_gas = ?

@ ρ_gas = P M_gas / (R T)

R = 8.314 J/(mol K)
n_gas_arbitrary = 1 mol
V_gas = n_gas_arbitrary R T / P
m_gas = V_gas ρ_gas

M_gas = m_gas / n_gas_arbitrary

The gas is H2(g)

Think about it: Howe does this density compare to that of liquid water?
a) Liquid water is less dense, ρ[H2O] = 1000 g/L
b) Liquid water is more dense, ρ[H2O] = 1000 g/L
c) Liquid water is less dense, ρ[H2O] = 1 g/L
d) Liquid water is more dense, ρ[H2O] = 1 g/L



<h3>Example 9.12: Empirical/Molecular Formula Problems Using the Ideal Gas Law and Density of a Gas</h3>

Cyclopropane, a gas once used with oxygen as a general anesthetic, is composed of 85.7% carbon and 14.3% hydrogen by mass. Find the empirical formula. If 1.56 g of cyclopropane occupies a volume of 1.00 L at 0.984 atm and 50 °C, what is the molecular formula for cyclopropane?

<h4>Solution</h4>

f_C = 0.857

f_H = 0.143

m_gas = 1.56 g

V_gas = 1.00 L

P = 0.984 atm

T = 50 °aC

Strategy 1: Figure out the mass of carbon and hydrogen from mass and mass fractions, and the respective chemical amounts via the molar mass of these elements. Then, figure out the chemical amount of the gas molecule via the ideal gas law. The ratio of amount of atom vs amount of molecule gives the coefficient for that type of atom.

m_C = f_C m_gas

m_H = f_H m_gas

n_C = m_C / M[C]

n_H = m_H / M[H]

Now we use the ideal gas law to figure out the chemical amount of the gas molecule:

@ P V_gas = n_molecule R T

R = 8.314 J/(mol K)

n_molecule = P V_gas / (R T)

coeff_C = n_C / n_molecule

coeff_H = n_H / n_molecule

Molecular formula is C3H6

<hr>

Strategy 2: First solve the empirical formula problem using methods discussed earlier. Assume 1.56 g (because we actually know the mass of the sample). After calculating the chemical amounts of atoms as in strategy 1, divide by the smallest value (amount of carbon) to get the stoichiometric ratio n[C] : n[H]. In the last step, realize that the smallest whole number ratio is the empirical formula:

ratio[H : C] = n_H / n_C

The ratio of H to C is very close to 2, so the empirical formula is CH2, and we can calculate the molar mass of this empirical formula:

M[CH2] = M[CH2]

Then, we calculate the molar mass of the gas molecule by dividing the mass of the sample by the chemical amount (obtained from ideal gas law):

M_molecule = m_gas/ n_molecule

Because the molar mass of the molecule is about three time higher than that of the empirical formula, we have to multiply the subscripts in the empirical formula CH2 by three to obtain the molecular formula, C3H6.





<h3>Example 9.12b: Empirical/Molecular Formula Problems Using the Ideal Gas Law and Density of a Gas</h3>

Acetylene, a fuel used welding torches, is comprised of 92.3% C and 7.7% H by mass. Find the empirical formula. If 1.10 g of acetylene occupies of volume of 1.00 L at 1.15 atm and 59.5 °C, what is the molecular formula for acetylene?

<h4>Answer:</h4>

m_sample = 1.10 g

V_sample = 1.00 L

P = 1.15 atm

T = 59.5°aC

f_C = 0.923

f_H = 0.077

m_C = f_C * m_sample

m_H = f_H * m_sample

n_C = m_C / M[C]

n_H = m_H / M[H]

Amounts are equal, empirical formula is CH and the corresponding molar mass:

M[CH] = M[CH]

For molar mass of the molecule, we apply the ideal gas law to calculate the amount of molecule present in the sample

@ P V_gas = n R T

R = 8.314 J/(mol K)

n_molecule = P V_sample / (R T)

M_molecule = m_sample / n_molecule

# This molar mass is twice as high as that for the empirical formula, so the molecular formula is C2H2

Empirical formula, CH; Molecular formula, C2H2

Think about it: Given that an atom of carbon has about 12 times the mass of an atom of hydrogen, what ratio of carbon to hydrogen could we have estimated from the given information:
a) There are many more carbon atoms than hydrogen atoms because the composition is so uneven (92% vs 8%).
a) There are many more hydrogen atoms than carbon atoms in acetylen because hydrogen atoms are so much lighter.
a) The ratio of masses and the ratio of percent composition by mass are similar, so the stoichiometry is close to 1:1.
a) We can't estimate anything without a calculator because there are too many numbers to juggle at the same time.


<h3>Example 9.13: Determining the Molar Mass of a Volatile Liquid</h3>

The approximate molar mass of a volatile liquid can be determined by:

<ol><li> Heating a sample of the liquid in a flask with a tiny hole at the top, which converts the liquid into gas that may escape through the hole (Westfield State students do this experiment in the General Chemistry I lab).

<li> Removing the flask from heat at the instant when the last bit of liquid becomes gas, at which time the flask will be filled with only gaseous sample at ambient pressure

<li> Sealing the flask and permitting the gaseous sample to condense to liquid, and then weighing the flask to determine the sample’s mass (see <a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_09_03_liquidgas.jpg">Figure 19</a>)

<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_09_03_liquidgas.jpg">Figure 19. </li>When the volatile liquid in the flask is heated past its boiling point, it becomes gas and drives air out of the flask.</ol>

<hr>

Using this procedure, a sample of chloroform gas weighing 0.494 g is collected in a flask with a volume of 129 cm^3 at 99.6 °C when the atmospheric pressure is 742.1 mm Hg. What is the approximate molar mass of chloroform?

<h4>Solution</h4>

m_gas = 0.494 g

V_gas = 129. mL

T_gas = 99.6 °aC

P = 742.1 mmHg

Use the ideal gas law to find the amount of gas

@ P V_gas = n R T

R = 8.314 J/(mol K)

n_gas = P V_gas / (R T_gas)

M_gas = m_gas / n_gas

<h3>Example 5.4b: Identifying a Metal by Measuring Specific Heat</h3>

A 92.9-g piece of a silver/gray metal is heated to 178.0 °C, and then quickly transferred into 75.0 mL of water initially at 24.0 °C. After 5 minutes, both the metal and the water have reached the same temperature: 29.7 °C. Determine the specific heat and the identity of the metal. (Note: You should find that the specific heat is close to that of two different metals. Explain how you can confidently determine the identity of the metal).

<h4>Answer:</h4>
m_metal = 92.9 g

T_metal_initial = 178.0 °aC

V_water = 75.0 mL

T_water_initial = 24.0 °aC

T_final = 29.7 °aC

c_p_water = 4.186 J /(g °ΔC)

c_p_metal = ?

ρ_water = 1.00 g/mL

m_water = V_water ρ_water 

q_water = m_water c_p_water (T_final - T_water_initial)

q_metal = -q_water

c_p_metal = q_metal / (m_metal (T_final -T_metal_initial ))

Fits gold or iridium. Gold has different color, so maybe iridium
Think about it: What can we say about the sign (+/-) of the specific heat capacity of a substance?
a) It is always positive because heat is absorbed by the substance when increasing its temperature
b) It is always positive because heat is given off by the substance when increasing its temperature
c) It is always negative because heat is absorbed by the substance when increasing its temperature
d) It is always negative because heat is given off by the substance when increasing its temperature


<h3>Example 5.5b: Heat Produced by an Exothermic Reaction</h3>

When 100 mL of 0.200 M NaCl(aq) and 100 mL of 0.200 M AgNO3(aq), both at 21.9 °C, are mixed in a coffee cup calorimeter, the temperature increases to 23.5 °C as solid AgCl forms. How much heat is produced by this precipitation reaction? What assumptions did you make to determine your value?

<h4>Answer:</h4>
V[NaCl sol] = 100. mL

c[NaCl] = 0.200 M

V[AgNO3 sol] = 100. mL

[AgNO3] = 0.200 M

T_initial = 21.9 °aC

T_final = 23.5 °aC

q_reaction = ?

c_p_water = 4.186 J/(K g)

ρ_water = 1.00 g / mL

V_mixture = V[NaCl sol] +V[AgNO3 sol] 

m_mixture = ρ_water V_mixture 

q_water = c_p_water m_mixture (T_final -T_initial )
q_reaction  = - q_water
Think about it: Is this reaction endothermic or exothermic? We know this reaction is...
a) exothermic because q_reaction is positive
b) endothermic because q_reaction is positive
c) exothermic because q_reaction is negative
d) endothermic because q_reaction is negative



<h3>Example 5.6b: Heat Flow in an Instant Ice Pack</h3>

When a 3.00-g sample of KCl was added to 3.00 × 10^2 g of water in a coffee cup calorimeter, the temperature decreased by 1.05 °C. How much heat is involved in the dissolution of the KCl? What assumptions did you make?

<h4>Answer:</h4>
m[KCl] = 3.00 g

m_water = 3.00E2 g

ΔT = -1.05 °ΔC

q_dissolution = ?

c_p_water = 4.186 J/(K g)

q_water = c_p_water m_water ΔT 
q_dissolution = -q_water

For the calculation, we assumed solution behaves like 300 g of water in terms of heat capacity.

a) exothermic for cold packs and endothermic for hot packs
b) endothermic for cold packs and exothermic for hot packs
c) endothermic for both hot and cold packs
d) exothermic for both hot and cold packs

<h3>Example 5.7b: Bomb Calorimetry</h3>

When 0.963 g of benzene, C6H6, is burned in a bomb calorimeter, the temperature of the calorimeter increases by 8.39 °C. The bomb has a heat capacity of 784 J/°C and is submerged in 925 mL of water. How much heat was produced by the combustion of the glucose sample?

<h4>Answer:</h4>
m[C6H6] = 0.963 g

ΔT = 8.39 °ΔC

C_bomb = 784. J/K

V_water = 925. mL

q_combustion = ?

ρ_water = 1.00 g/mL

c_p_water = 4.186 J/(K g)

m_water = V_water ρ_water 

q_water = m_water c_p_water ΔT 

q_bomb = C_bomb ΔT 

q_combustion = -(q_water +q_bomb )

Think about it: Why use a bomb calorimeter instead of an open calorimeter for this experiment?
a) Benzene is highly explosive, so you need to contain the reaction in a steel cylinder.
b) Because the products are gases, it would be difficult to capture their thermal energy before they escape.
c) Benzene would not burn in the presence of water.
d) Benzene would evaporate too quickly in an open calorimeter.

<h3>Example 5.8b: Measurement of an Enthalpy Change</h3>

When 1.34 g Zn(s) reacts with 60.0 mL of 0.750 M HCl(aq), 3.14 kJ of heat are produced. Determine the enthalpy change per mole of zinc reacting for the reaction:

!Zn(s) +2HCl(aq) -> ZnCl2(aq) +H2(g)

<h4>Answer:</h4>
m[Zn] = 1.34 g

V[HCl] = 60.0 mL

c[HCl] = 0.750 M

q_reaction = -3.14 kJ

ΔH_reaction = ?

n[Zn] = m[Zn] / M[Zn]

n[HCl] = c[HCl] V[HCl] 

Zn is limiting and HCl is in excess.

ΔH_reaction = q_reaction / n[Zn] 
Think about it: What does it mean if a quantity is designated "specific" (as in specific heat capacity) as opposed to "molar" (as in molar mass or molar enthalpy change)?
a) "specific" means per chemical amount and "molar" means per mass.
b) "molar" means per chemical amount and "specific" means per mass.
c) "molar" refers to your back teeth in this context.
d) "specific" refers to the opposite of general in this context.





<h3>Example 5.9b: Another Example of the Measurement of an Enthalpy Change</h3>

<h4>Answer:</h4>
When 1.42 g of iron reacts with 1.80 g of chlorine, 3.22 g of FeCl2(s) and 8.60 kJ of heat is produced. What is the molar enthalpy change for the reaction (i.e. enthalpy per mole of FeCl2(s) produced)?

m[Fe] = 1.42 g

m[Cl2] = 1.80 g

m[FeCl2] = 3.22 g

!Fe + Cl2 -> FeCl2

Stoichiometric amounts, all Fe and Cl2 are turned over to FeCl2.

q_reaction = -8.60 kJ

n[FeCl2] = m[FeCl2] / M[FeCl2] 

ΔH = q_reaction / n[FeCl2] 

The production of one mole of ZnCl2 releases 339 kJ.

!Fe + Cl2 -> FeCl2 !ΔH = -339. kJ/mol
Think about it: What changes when you double the amount of reactants (i.e. what are the extensive and the intensive quantities)?
a) The heat produced and the molar enthalpy both double.
a) The heat produced doubles, the molar enthalpy is unchanged.
a) The molar enthalpy doubles, the heat produced is unchanged.
a) Neither the heat produced nor the molar enthalpy change.

<h3>Example 5.11b: Evaluating an Enthalpy of Formation</h3>

Hydrogen gas, H2, reacts explosively with gaseous chlorine, Cl2, to form hydrogen chloride, HCl(g). How much heat is produced/required for the reaction of 1 mole of H2(g) with 1 mole of Cl2(g) if both the reactants and products are at standard state conditions? The standard enthalpy of formation of HCl(g) is -92.3 kJ/mol.
<h4>Answer:</h4>

ΔHf°[HCl(g)] = -92.3 kJ/mol

! 1/2 H2(g) + 1/2 Cl2(g) -> HCl(g) ! ΔᵣH° = -92.3 kJ/mol

Reactants are at stoichiometric ratio

n[H2(g)] = 1 mol

ν[H2(g)] = 1/2

n[HCl(g)] = n[H2(g)] /ν[H2(g)]

q = ΔHf°[HCl(g)] n[HCl(g)] 

Think about it: Why do you need to write down a balanced chemical equation of the formation of HCl?
a) To practice balancing chemical equation
b) Because otherwise you'd be done too quickly with the problem
c) Because you need the stoichiometric coefficients to solve the problem
d) Because it is a joy to balance chemical equations, and nobody should be deprived of it

<h3>Example 5.13b: Stepwise Calculation of ΔHf° Using Hess’s Law</h3>

Calculate ΔH for the process:

!N2(g) +2O2(g) -> 2NO2(g)

from the following information:

!N2(g) +O2(g) -> 2NO(g)!ΔH = 180.5 kJ/mol

!NO(g) +1/2O2(g) -> NO2(g)!ΔH = -57.06 kJ/mol

<h4>Answer:</h4>

!N2(g) +O2(g) -> 2NO(g) ! ΔH = 180.5 kJ/mol 

!2NO(g) + O2(g) -> 2NO2(g) ! ΔH = 2 * (-57.06 kJ/mol)

ΔH1 = 180.5 kJ/mol

ΔH2 = -57.06 kJ/mol

ΔH_combined = ΔH1 + 2 ΔH2

<h3>Example 5.14b: A More Challenging Problem Using Hess’s Law</h3>

Aluminum chloride can be formed from its elements:

![i] 2Al(s) +3Cl2(g) -> 2AlCl3(s)!ΔH° = ?

Use the reactions here to determine the ΔH° for reaction (i):

![ii] HCl(g) -> HCl(aq)!ΔH°_ii =-74.8kJ

![iii] H2(g) +Cl2(g) -> 2HCl(g)!ΔH°_iii=-185kJ

![iv] AlCl3(aq) -> AlCl3(s)!ΔH°_iv= +323kJ/mol

![v] 2Al(s) +6HCl(aq) -> 2AlCl3(aq) +3H2(g)!ΔH°_v =-1049kJ

<h4>Answer:</h4>


![v] 2Al(s) +6HCl(aq) -> 2AlCl3(aq) +3H2(g)

![iii*3] 3 H2(g) +3 Cl2(g) -> 6 HCl(g)

![ii*6] 6 HCl(g) -> 6 HCl(aq)

![iv*2] 2 AlCl3(aq) -> 2 AlCl3(s)

ΔH°_ii = -74.8 kJ/mol

ΔH°_iii = -185 kJ/mol

ΔH°_iv = 323 kJ/mol

ΔH°_v = -1049 kJ/mol

ΔH° = ?

ΔH° = sum(ΔH°_v, 3 ΔH°_iii, 6 ΔH°_ii, 2 ΔH°_iv)

Think about it: Other than multiplying all coefficients in a balanced chemical equation by the same number, what other manipulation is allowed and will change the molar reaction enthalpy in a predictable manner?
a) Increasing one coefficient and decreasing another on the product side, as long as their sum stays constant
b) Adding a pure element on the side of the reactants
c) Swapping reactants and products
d) Removing all species in the gas phase from the equation because they have no mass



<h3>Example 5.15b: Using Hess’s Law</h3>

Calculate the heat of combustion of 1 mole of ethanol, C2H5OH(l), when H2O(l) and CO2(g) are formed. Use the following enthalpies of formation: C2H5OH(l), -278 kJ/mol; H2O(l), -286 kJ/mol; and CO2(g), -394 kJ/mol.

<h4>Answer:</h4>

!C2H5OH + 7/2 O2 -> 2 CO2(g) + 3 H2O(l)

ΔHf°[C2H5OH] = -278 kJ/mol

ΔHf°[H2O(l)] = -286 kJ/mol

ΔHf°[CO2(g)] = -394 kJ/mol

ΔHᵣ° =  sum(2 ΔHf°[CO2(g)], 3 ΔHf°[H2O(l)], -ΔHf°[C2H5OH])

Think about it: Why isn't the enthalpy of formation for dioxygen given in the question, even though dioxygen is part of all combustion reactions?

<h3>Example 2.1: Testing Dalton’s Atomic Theory</h3>

In the following drawing, the green spheres represent atoms of a certain element. The purple spheres represent atoms of another element. If the spheres touch, they are part of a single unit of a compound. Does the following chemical change represented by these symbols violate any of the ideas of Dalton’s atomic theory? If so, which one?

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_02_01_Dalton6_img.jpg"}

<h4>Solution</h4>

The starting materials consist of two green spheres and two purple spheres. The products consist of only one green sphere and one purple sphere. This violates Dalton’s postulate that atoms are neither created nor destroyed during a chemical change, but are merely redistributed. (In this case, atoms appear to have been destroyed.)



<h3>Example 2.1b: Testing Dalton’s Atomic Theory</h3>

In the following drawing, the green spheres represent atoms of a certain element. The purple spheres represent atoms of another element. If the spheres touch, they are part of a single unit of a compound. Does the following chemical change represented by these symbols violate any of the ideas of Dalton’s atomic theory? If so, which one?

{"https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_02_01_Dalton8_img.jpg"}

<h4>Answer:</h4>
There are 4 green atoms and 2 blue atoms on either side of the equation, so it is all good.



<h3>Example 2.2: Laws of Definite and Multiple Proportions</h3>

A sample of compound A (a clear, colorless gas) is analyzed and found to contain 4.27 g carbon and 5.69 g oxygen. A sample of compound B (also a clear, colorless gas) is analyzed and found to contain 5.19 g carbon and 13.84 g oxygen. Are these data an example of the law of definite proportions, the law of multiple proportions, or neither? What do these data tell you about substances A and B?

<h4>Solution</h4>

In compound A, the mass ratio of carbon to oxygen is:

m[C]_compA = 4.27 g

m[O]_compA = 5.69 g

r[C:O]_compA = m[C]_compA / m[O]_compA

In compound B, the mass ratio of carbon to oxygen is:

m[C]_compB = 5.19 g

m[O]_compB = 13.84 g

r[C:O]_compB = m[C]_compB / m[O]_compB

The ratios are different, so this is not the law of definite proportions (and compound A is different from compound B because it has different composition).

The ratio of these ratios is:

r_r = r[C:O]_compA / r[C:O]_compB

This supports the law of multiple proportions. This means that A and B are different compounds, with A having twice as much carbon per amount of oxygen (or twice as much oxygen per amount of carbon) as B. A possible pair of compounds that would fit this relationship would be compA = CO and compB = CO2.



<h3>Example 2.3: Composition of an Atom</h3>

Iodine is an essential trace element in our diet; it is needed to produce thyroid hormone. Insufficient iodine in the diet can lead to the development of a goiter, an enlargement of the thyroid gland (<a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_02_03_Iodine.jpg">Figure 12</a>).

<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_02_03_Iodine.jpg">Figure 12(a) Insufficient iodine in the diet can cause an enlargement of the thyroid gland called a goiter. (b) The addition of small amounts of iodine to salt, which prevents the formation of goiters, has helped eliminate this concern in the US where salt consumption is high. (credit a: modification of work by “Almazi”/Wikimedia Commons; credit b: modification of work by Mike Mozart)

The addition of small amounts of iodine to table salt (iodized salt) has essentially eliminated this health concern in the United States, but as much as 40% of the world’s population is still at risk of iodine deficiency. The iodine atoms are added as anions, and each has a 1- charge and a mass number of 127. Determine the numbers of protons, neutrons, and electrons in one of these iodine anions.

<h4>Solution</h4>The atomic number of iodine (53) tells us that a neutral iodine atom contains 53 protons in its nucleus and 53 electrons outside its nucleus. 

protons[I atom] = 53

electrons[I atom] = protons[I atom]

Because the sum of the numbers of protons and neutrons equals the mass number, 127, the number of neutrons is

number_mass = 127

neutrons[I atom] = number_mass - protons[I atom]

Since the iodine is added as a 1- anion, the number of electrons in the ion is

electrons[I- ion] = electrons[I atom] + 1

The number of protons and neutrons is the same as in the atom (no changes in the nucleus during ionization)



<h3>Example 2.3b: Composition of an Atom</h3>

An ion of platinum has a mass number of 195 and contains 74 electrons. How many protons and neutrons does it contain, and what is its charge?

<h4>Answer:</h4>

From the periodic table, we read off the number of protons:

protons = 78

Protons and neutrons contribute to the mass number (the mass of the electrons can be neglected as it is smaller by three orders of magnitude).

neutrons = 195 - protons

Protons have positive charge and electrons have negative charge, so the charge of protons and electrons combined is:

electrons = 74

charge = protons - electrons

(b) selenium

(c) barium

(d) lithium

(a) noble gas; (b) chalcogen; (c) alkaline earth metal; (d) alkali metal



<h3>Example 2.3b: Composition of an Atom</h3>

An ion of platinum has a mass number of 195 and contains 74 electrons. How many protons and neutrons does it contain, and what is its charge?

<h4>Answer:</h4>
@ A[Pt] = N[p+ + n^0] ! The mass number A is equal to the number of protons and neutrons

N[p+ + n^0] = 195 

N[e-] = 74
N_charge = ?  
N[n^0] = ?

N[p+] = 78

N[n^0] = N[p+ + n^0] -N[p+] 

N_charge = N[p+] -N[e-] 

The charge is plus four, so it is a Pt(IV)-ion 

Think about it: Why is this ion charged?

1) All elements that are metals are charged always

2) There are more neutrons than protons in the nucleus

3) There are more protons than neutrons in the nucleus

4) The number of protons and electrons is unequal



<h3>Example 2.4b: Calculation of Average Atomic Mass</h3>

A sample of magnesium is found to contain 78.70% of ^24Mg atoms (mass 23.98 amu), 10.13% of ^25Mg atoms (mass 24.99 amu), and 11.17% of ^26Mg atoms (mass 25.98 amu). Calculate the average mass of a Mg atom.

<h4>Answer:</h4>
f[^24Mg] = 78.70  ％

m[^24Mg] = 23.98 Da 

f[^25Mg] = 10.13 ％

m[^25Mg] = 24.99 Da

f[^26Mg] = 11.17 ％

m[^26Mg] = 25.98 Da 

m_average = f[^24Mg] m[^24Mg] +f[^25Mg] m[^25Mg] +f[^26Mg] m[^26Mg] 

Think about it: Is this average mass reasonable?

a) Yes, because it is higher than the highest isotopic mass

b) Yes, because it is lower than the lowest isotopic mass

c) Yes, because it is close to the mass of the common isotope

d) No, because it is inbetween the three masses



<h3>Example 2.5b: Calculation of Percent Abundance</h3>

Naturally occurring copper consists of ^63Cu (mass 62.9296 amu) and ^65Cu (mass 64.9278 amu), with an average mass of 63.546 amu. What is the percent composition of Cu in terms of these two isotopes?

<h4>Answer:</h4>
m[lo] = 62.9296 Da 

m[hi] = 64.9278 Da 

m_average = 63.546 Da

f[lo] = ? 

f[hi] = ? 

@ m_average = f[lo] m[lo] + f[hi] m[hi]

@ f[lo] + f[hi] = 1 

Substitute 1 - f[lo] = f[hi] to eliminate f[hi] 

@  m_average = f[lo] m[lo] + (1 - f[lo]) m[hi] 

Now solve for f[lo]

@  m_average = f[lo] m[lo] + m[hi] - f[lo] m[hi] 

@  m_average = f[lo] (m[lo] - m[hi]) + m[hi]  

@  m_average - m[hi] = f[lo] (m[lo] - m[hi]) 

@  (m_average - m[hi]) / (m[lo] - m[hi]) = f[lo]

f[lo] = (m_average - m[hi]) / (m[lo] - m[hi]) * 100％

f[hi] = 1 - f[lo] 

The sample contains 69.15 % 63-Cu and 30.85% 65-Cu

Think about it: Could you have predicted that the sample contains more 63-Cu and less 65-Cu?

a) Yes, because the isotopes with lower mass are more stable.

b) No, because the isotopes with lower mass are less stable.

c) Yes, because the average mass is closer to that of 63-Cu.

d) No, because the average mass is closer to that of 65-Cu

<h3>Example 2.4: Calculation of Average Atomic Mass</h3>

A meteorite found in central Indiana contains traces of the noble gas neon picked up from the solar wind during the meteorite’s trip through the solar system. Analysis of a sample of the gas showed that it consisted of 91.84% ^20Ne (mass 19.9924 Da), 0.47% ^21Ne (mass 20.9940 Da), and 7.69% ^22Ne (mass 21.9914 Da). What is the average mass of the neon in the solar wind?

<h4>Solution</h4>

f[20] = 91.84 ％

f[21] = 0.47 ％

f[22] = 7.69 ％

m[20] = 19.9924 Da

m[21] = 20.9940 Da

m[22] = 21.9914 Da

m_average = ?

m_average = f[20] m[20] +f[21] m[21] +f[22] m[22] 

The average mass of the neon in the solar wind is 20.151 Da. (The average mass of a terrestrial neon atom is 20.1796 Da. This result demonstrates that we may find slight differences in the natural abundance of isotopes, depending on their origin.)



<h3>Example 2.5: Calculation of Percent Abundance</h3>

Naturally occurring chlorine consists of ^35Cl (mass 34.96885 Da) and ^37Cl (mass 36.96590 Da), with an average mass of 35.453 Da. What is the percent composition of Cl in terms of these two isotopes?

<h4>Solution</h4>

m[35] = 34.96885 Da

m[37] = 36.96590 Da

m_average = 35.453 Da

f[37] = ?

f[35] = ?

The average mass of chlorine is the fraction that is ^35Cl times the mass of ^35Cl plus the fraction that is ^37Cl times the mass of ^37Cl.

@ m_average = f[35] m[35] + f[37] m[37]@[Equation 1]

The fraction that is ^35Cl and the fraction that is ^37Cl must add up to 1, so the fraction of ^37Cl must equal 1 minus the fraction of ^35Cl

@ f[37] = 1 - f[35] @[Equation 2]

Substituting equation 2 into equation 1 gives

@ m_average = f[35] m[35] + (1 - f[35])m[37]

Now we have an equation with a single unknown f[35], we can solve for it and calculate it:

@ f[35]m[37] - f[35] m[35] = m[37] - m_average @ ...terms with f[35] on the left

@ f[35](m[37] - m[35]) = m[37] - m_average @ ...isolated f[35] on the left

@ f[35] = (m[37] - m_average)/(m[37] - m[35]) @ ...solved for f[35]

f[35] = (m[37] - m_average)/(m[37] - m[35]) 100 ％

To determine f[37], we can use the fact that the relative ambundances add up to 1 (equation 2)

f[37] = 1 - f[35]

Therefore, chlorine consists of 75.76% ^35Cl and 24.24% ^37Cl.



<h3>Example 2.6: Empirical and Molecular Formulas</h3>

Molecules of glucose (blood sugar) contain 6 carbon atoms, 12 hydrogen atoms, and 6 oxygen atoms. What are the molecular and empirical formulas of glucose?

<h4>Solution</h4>

The molecular formula is: 

!C6H12O6 !molecular formula

(because one molecule actually contains 6 C, 12 H, and 6 O atoms)

The empirical formula shows the simplest whole-number ratio of the elements in a compound. We can take the numbers in the molecular formula and divide them all by the smallest number to get a tentative empirical formula. If all the numbers are whole numbers, we are done. If there are fractions such as halves or thirds, we have to multiply everything again (by 2 or 3, respectively) to arrive at the simplest whole-number ratio.

N[C] = 6

N[H] = 12

N[O] = 6

smallest = N[C] ! or N[O]

N[C]tentative = N[C] / smallest

N[H]tentative = N[H] / smallest

N[O]tentative = N[O] / smallest

In this case, all numbers are whole, so the simplest whole-number ratio C to H to O atoms in glucose is 1:2:1. (You are welcome to figure this out in your head if it is obvious to you from just looking at the formula.)

The empirical formula is:

!CH2O !empirical formula

Atoms of each of the following elements are essential for life. Give the group name for the following elements:

(a) chlorine

(b) calcium

(c) sodium

(d) sulfur

<h4>Solution</h4>

The family names are as follows (look on the periodic table to see which group - column - they are in; groups 1, 2, 16, 17 and 18 have common group names):

(a) chlorine: halogen

(b) calcium: alkaline earth metal

(c) sodium: alkali metal

(d) sulfur: chalcogen



<h3>Example 2.8: Composition of Ions</h3>

An ion found in some compounds used as antiperspirants contains 13 protons and 10 electrons. What is its symbol?

<h4>Solution</h4>

N[p+] = 13

N[e-] = 10

Because the number of protons remains unchanged when an atom forms an ion, the atomic number of the element must be 13. Knowing this lets us use the periodic table to identify the element as Al (aluminum). The Al atom has lost three electrons and thus has three more positive charges (13) than it has electrons (10). 

N_charge = N[p+] - N[e-]

This is the aluminum cation, Al^3+.



<h3>Example 2.9: Formation of Ions</h3>

Magnesium and nitrogen react to form an ionic compound. Predict which forms an anion, which forms a cation, and the charges of each ion. Write the symbol for each ion and name them.

<h4>Solution</h4>

Magnesium’s position in the periodic table (group 2) tells us that it is a metal. Metals form positive ions (cations). A magnesium atom must lose two electrons to have the same number electrons as an atom of the previous noble gas, neon. Thus, a magnesium atom will form a cation with two fewer electrons than protons and a charge of 2^+. 

The symbol for and name of the ion is 

! Mg^2+ ! magnesium ion

Nitrogen’s position in the periodic table (group 15) reveals that it is a nonmetal. Nonmetals form negative ions (anions). A nitrogen atom must gain three electrons to have the same number of electrons as an atom of the following noble gas, neon. Thus, a nitrogen atom will form an anion with three more electrons than protons and a charge of 3^-. The symbol for and name of the ion is 

!N^3- ! nitride ion



<h3>Example 2.10: Predicting the Formula of an Ionic Compound</h3>

The gemstone sapphire (<a href="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_02_06_Sapphire.jpg">Figure 31</a>) is mostly a compound of aluminum and oxygen that contains aluminum cations, Al^3+, and oxygen anions, O^2-. What is the formula of this compound?

<img src="https://opentextbc.ca/chemistry/wp-content/uploads/sites/150/2016/05/CNX_Chem_02_06_Sapphire.jpg">Figure 31Although pure aluminum oxide is colorless, trace amounts of iron and titanium give blue sapphire its characteristic color. (credit: modification of work by Stanislav Doronenko)

<h4>Solution</h4>

Because the ionic compound must be electrically neutral, it must have the same number of positive and negative charges. 

Charge[Al^3+] = 3

Charge[O^2-] = -2
If you were to combine the ions in a 1:1 ratio, there would be an excess of positive charge.

Two aluminum ions, each with a charge of 3+, would give us six positive charges, and three oxide ions, each with a charge of 2-, would give us six negative charges. The formula and the dissociation equation would be 

! Al2O3 -> 2 Al^3+ + 3 O^2-

You can figure out that both the left hand side and the right hand side have a total charge of zero, either by looking at the chemical equation above or by using the calculator (see below):

@Charge_total = ν[Al^3+] Charge[Al^3+] + ν[O^2-] Charge[O^2-] = 0

ν[Al^3+] = 2

ν[O^2-] = 3

Charge_total = ν[Al^3+] Charge[Al^3+] + ν[O^2-] Charge[O^2-]



<h3>Example 2.11: Predicting the Formula of a Compound with a Polyatomic Anion</h3>

Baking powder contains calcium dihydrogen phosphate, an ionic compound composed of the ions Ca^2+ and H2PO4-. What is the formula of this compound?

<h4>Solution</h4>

The positive and negative charges must balance, and this ionic compound must be electrically neutral. Thus, we must have two negative charges to balance the 2+ charge of the calcium ion. This requires a ratio of one Ca^2+ ion to two H2PO4- ions. We designate this by enclosing the formula for the dihydrogen phosphate ion in parentheses and adding a subscript 2. The formula and dissociation equation is 

!Ca(H2PO4)2 -> Ca^2+ + 2 H2PO4-

Inspection or adding up the charges shows that with this stoichiometry, the ionic compound is indeed net neutral.

ν[Ca^2+] = 1

ν[H2PO4^2-] = 2

Charge[Ca^2+] = 2

Charge[H2PO4-] = -1

Charge_total  = ν[Ca^2+] Charge[Ca^2+] +ν[H2PO4^2-] Charge[H2PO4-] 



<h3>Example 2.12: Predicting the Type of Bonding in Compounds</h3>

Predict whether the following compounds are ionic or molecular. For ionic compounds, show the dissociation equation:

(a) KI, the compound used as a source of iodine in table salt

(b) H2O2, the bleach and disinfectant hydrogen peroxide

(c) CHCl3, the anesthetic chloroform

(d) Li2CO3, a source of lithium in antidepressants

<h4>Solution</h4>

(a) Potassium (group 1) is a metal, and iodine (group 17) is a nonmetal; KI is predicted to be ionic.

(b) Hydrogen (group 1) is a nonmetal, and oxygen (group 16) is a nonmetal; H2O2 is predicted to be molecular.

(c) Carbon (group 14) is a nonmetal, hydrogen (group 1) is a nonmetal, and chlorine (group 17) is a nonmetal; CHCl3 is predicted to be molecular.

(d) Lithium (group 1) is a metal, and carbonate is a polyatomic ion; Li2CO3 is predicted to be ionic.

![a] KI -> K+ + I-

![d] Li2CO3 -> 2 Li+ + CO3^2-



<h3>Example 2.13: Naming Ionic Compounds</h3>

Name the following ionic compounds, which contain a metal that can have more than one ionic charge:

(a) Fe2S3

(b) CuSe

(c) GaN

(d) CrCl3

(e) Ti2(SO4)3

<h4>Solution</h4>The anions in these compounds have a fixed negative charge (S^2-, Se^2- , N^3-, Cl^-, and SO4^2-), and the compounds must be neutral. Because the total number of positive charges in each compound must equal the total number of negative charges, the positive ions must be Fe^3+, Cu^2+, Ga^3+, Cr^3+, and Ti^3+. These charges are used in the names of the metal ions:

(a) iron(III) sulfide

(b) copper(II) selenide

(c) gallium(III) nitride

(d) chromium(III) chloride

(e) titanium(III) sulfate
